We can sell the car.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCharge him salvage or something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99re we going to get food?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Amory.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHonestly,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Kerry, eying him reprovingly, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you doubt\r\n      Kerry\xe2\x80\x99s ability for three short days? Some people have lived on\r\n      nothing for years at a time. Read the Boy Scout Monthly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThree days,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory mused, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\xe2\x80\x99ve got classes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOne of the days is the Sabbath.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cJust the same, I can only cut six more classes, with over a\r\n      month and a half to go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThrow him out!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a long walk back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAmory, you\xe2\x80\x99re running it out, if I may coin a new phrase.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHadn\xe2\x80\x99t you better get some dope on yourself, Amory?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory subsided resignedly and drooped into a contemplation of the\r\n      scenery. Swinburne seemed to fit in somehow.\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cOh, winter\xe2\x80\x99s rains and ruins are over, And all the seasons of snows\r\n   and sins; The days dividing lover and lover, The light that loses,\r\n   the night that wins; And time remembered is grief forgotten, And\r\n   frosts are slain and flowers begotten, And in green underwood and\r\n   cover, Blossom by blossom the spring begins.\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cThe full streams feed on flower of\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter, Amory? Amory\xe2\x80\x99s thinking about poetry, about\r\n      the pretty birds and flowers. I can see it in his eye.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99m not,\xe2\x80\x9d he lied. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m thinking about the Princetonian. I\r\n      ought to make up to-night; but I can telephone back, I suppose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kerry respectfully, \xe2\x80\x9cthese important men\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory flushed and it seemed to him that Ferrenby, a defeated\r\n      competitor, winced a little. Of course, Kerry was only kidding,\r\n      but he really mustn\xe2\x80\x99t mention the Princetonian.\r\n\r\n      It was a halcyon day, and as they neared the shore and the salt\r\n      breezes scurried by, he began to picture the ocean and long,\r\n      level stretches of sand and red roofs over blue sea. Then they\r\n      hurried through the little town and it all flashed upon his\r\n      consciousness to a mighty paean of emotion....\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, good Lord! _Look_ at it!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLet me out, quick\xe2\x80\x94I haven\xe2\x80\x99t seen it for eight years! Oh,\r\n      gentlefolk, stop the car!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat an odd child!\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Alec.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI do believe he\xe2\x80\x99s a bit eccentric.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The car was obligingly drawn up at a curb, and Amory ran for the\r\n      boardwalk. First, he realized that the sea was blue and that\r\n      there was an enormous quantity of it, and that it roared and\r\n      roared\xe2\x80\x94really all the banalities about the ocean that one could\r\n      realize, but if any one had told him then that these things were\r\n      banalities, he would have gaped in wonder.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNow we\xe2\x80\x99ll get lunch,\xe2\x80\x9d ordered Kerry, wandering up with the\r\n      crowd. \xe2\x80\x9cCome on, Amory, tear yourself away and get practical.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll try the best hotel first,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cand thence and so\r\n      forth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      They strolled along the boardwalk to the most imposing hostelry\r\n      in sight, and, entering the dining-room, scattered about a table.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cEight Bronxes,\xe2\x80\x9d commanded Alec, \xe2\x80\x9cand a club sandwich and\r\n      Juliennes. The food for one. Hand the rest around.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory ate little, having seized a chair where he could watch the\r\n      sea and feel the rock of it. When luncheon was over they sat and\r\n      smoked quietly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the bill?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Some one scanned it.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cEight twenty-five.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cRotten overcharge. We\xe2\x80\x99ll give them two dollars and one for the\r\n      waiter. Kerry, collect the small change.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The waiter approached, and Kerry gravely handed him a dollar,\r\n      tossed two dollars on the check, and turned away. They sauntered\r\n      leisurely toward the door, pursued in a moment by the suspicious\r\n      Ganymede.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSome mistake, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Kerry took the bill and examined it critically.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo mistake!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, shaking his head gravely, and, tearing it\r\n      into four pieces, he handed the scraps to the waiter, who was so\r\n      dumfounded that he stood motionless and expressionless while they\r\n      walked out.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t he send after us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Kerry; \xe2\x80\x9cfor a minute he\xe2\x80\x99ll think we\xe2\x80\x99re the\r\n      proprietor\xe2\x80\x99s sons or something; then he\xe2\x80\x99ll look at the check\r\n      again and call the manager, and in the meantime\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      They left the car at Asbury and street-car\xe2\x80\x99d to Allenhurst, where\r\n      they investigated the crowded pavilions for beauty. At four there\r\n      were refreshments in a lunch-room, and this time they paid an\r\n      even smaller per cent on the total cost; something about the\r\n      appearance and savoir-faire of the crowd made the thing go, and\r\n      they were not pursued.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou see, Amory, we\xe2\x80\x99re Marxian Socialists,\xe2\x80\x9d explained Kerry. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\r\n      don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in property and we\xe2\x80\x99re putting it to the great\r\n      test.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNight will descend,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory suggested.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWatch, and put your trust in Holiday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      They became jovial about five-thirty and, linking arms, strolled\r\n      up and down the boardwalk in a row, chanting a monotonous ditty\r\n      about the sad sea waves. Then Kerry saw a face in the crowd that\r\n      attracted him and, rushing off, reappeared in a moment with one\r\n      of the homeliest girls Amory had ever set eyes on. Her pale mouth\r\n      extended from ear to ear, her teeth projected in a solid wedge,\r\n      and she had little, squinty eyes that peeped ingratiatingly over\r\n      the side sweep of her nose. Kerry presented them formally.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cName of Kaluka, Hawaiian queen! Let me present Messrs. Connage,\r\n      Sloane, Humbird, Ferrenby, and Blaine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The girl bobbed courtesies all around. Poor creature; Amory\r\n      supposed she had never before been noticed in her life\xe2\x80\x94possibly\r\n      she was half-witted. While she accompanied them (Kerry had\r\n      invited her to supper) she said nothing which could\r\n      discountenance such a belief.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cShe prefers her native dishes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Alec gravely to the waiter,\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cbut any coarse food will do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      All through supper he addressed her in the most respectful\r\n      language, while Kerry made idiotic love to her on the other side,\r\n      and she giggled and grinned. Amory was content to sit and watch\r\n      the by-play, thinking what a light touch Kerry had, and how he\r\n      could transform the barest incident into a thing of curve and\r\n      contour. They all seemed to have the spirit of it more or less,\r\n      and it was a relaxation to be with them. Amory usually liked men\r\n      individually, yet feared them in crowds unless the crowd was\r\n      around him. He wondered how much each one contributed to the\r\n      party, for there was somewhat of a spiritual tax levied. Alec and\r\n      Kerry were the life of it, but not quite the centre. Somehow the\r\n      quiet Humbird, and Sloane, with his impatient superciliousness,\r\n      were the centre.\r\n\r\n      Dick Humbird had, ever since freshman year, seemed to Amory a\r\n      perfect type of aristocrat. He was slender but well-built\xe2\x80\x94black\r\n      curly hair, straight features, and rather a dark skin. Everything\r\n      he said sounded intangibly appropriate. He possessed infinite\r\n      courage, an averagely good mind, and a sense of honor with a\r\n      clear charm and _noblesse oblige_ that varied it from\r\n      righteousness. He could dissipate without going to pieces, and\r\n      even his most bohemian adventures never seemed \xe2\x80\x9crunning it out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      People dressed like him, tried to talk as he did.... Amory\r\n      decided that he probably held the world back, but he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      have changed him. ...\r\n\r\n      He differed from the healthy type that was essentially middle\r\n      class\xe2\x80\x94he never seemed to perspire. Some people couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\n      familiar with a chauffeur without having it returned; Humbird\r\n      could have lunched at Sherry\xe2\x80\x99s with a colored man, yet people\r\n      would have somehow known that it was all right. He was not a\r\n      snob, though he knew only half his class. His friends ranged from\r\n      the highest to the lowest, but it was impossible to \xe2\x80\x9ccultivate\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      him. Servants worshipped him, and treated him like a god. He\r\n      seemed the eternal example of what the upper class tries to be.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s like those pictures in the Illustrated London News of the\r\n      English officers who have been killed,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory had said to Alec.\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d Alec had answered, \xe2\x80\x9cif you want to know the shocking\r\n      truth, his father was a grocery clerk who made a fortune in\r\n      Tacoma real estate and came to New York ten years ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory had felt a curious sinking sensation.\r\n\r\n      This present type of party was made possible by the surging\r\n      together of the class after club elections\xe2\x80\x94as if to make a last\r\n      desperate attempt to know itself, to keep together, to fight off\r\n      the tightening spirit of the clubs. It was a let-down from the\r\n      conventional heights they had all walked so rigidly.\r\n\r\n      After supper they saw Kaluka to the boardwalk, and then strolled\r\n      back along the beach to Asbury. The evening sea was a new\r\n      sensation, for all its color and mellow age was gone, and it\r\n      seemed the bleak waste that made the Norse sagas sad; Amory\r\n      thought of Kipling\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cBeaches of Lukanon before the sealers came.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      It was still a music, though, infinitely sorrowful.\r\n\r\n      Ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock found them penniless. They had suppered greatly on\r\n      their last eleven cents and, singing, strolled up through the\r\n      casinos and lighted arches on the boardwalk, stopping to listen\r\n      approvingly to all band concerts. In one place Kerry took up a\r\n      collection for the French War Orphans which netted a dollar and\r\n      twenty cents, and with this they bought some brandy in case they\r\n      caught cold in the night. They finished the day in a\r\n      moving-picture show and went into solemn systematic roars of\r\n      laughter at an ancient comedy, to the startled annoyance of the\r\n      rest of the audience. Their entrance was distinctly strategic,\r\n      for each man as he entered pointed reproachfully at the one just\r\n      behind him. Sloane, bringing up the rear, disclaimed all\r\n      knowledge and responsibility as soon as the others were scattered\r\n      inside; then as the irate ticket-taker rushed in he followed\r\n      nonchalantly.\r\n\r\n      They reassembled later by the Casino and made arrangements for\r\n      the night. Kerry wormed permission from the watchman to sleep on\r\n      the platform and, having collected a huge pile of rugs from the\r\n      booths to serve as mattresses and blankets, they talked until\r\n      midnight, and then fell into a dreamless sleep, though Amory\r\n      tried hard to stay awake and watch that marvellous moon settle on\r\n      the sea.\r\n\r\n      So they progressed for two happy days, up and down the shore by\r\n      street-car or machine, or by shoe-leather on the crowded\r\n      boardwalk; sometimes eating with the wealthy, more frequently\r\n      dining frugally at the expense of an unsuspecting restaurateur.\r\n      They had their photos taken, eight poses, in a quick-development\r\n      store. Kerry insisted on grouping them as a \xe2\x80\x9cvarsity\xe2\x80\x9d football\r\n      team, and then as a tough gang from the East Side, with their\r\n      coats inside out, and himself sitting in the middle on a\r\n      cardboard moon. The photographer probably has them yet\xe2\x80\x94at least,\r\n      they never called for them. The weather was perfect, and again\r\n      they slept outside, and again Amory fell unwillingly asleep.\r\n\r\n      Sunday broke stolid and respectable, and even the sea seemed to\r\n      mumble and complain, so they returned to Princeton via the Fords\r\n      of transient farmers, and broke up with colds in their heads, but\r\n      otherwise none the worse for wandering.\r\n\r\n      Even more than in the year before, Amory neglected his work, not\r\n      deliberately but lazily and through a multitude of other\r\n      interests. Co-ordinate geometry and the melancholy hexameters of\r\n      Corneille and Racine held forth small allurements, and even\r\n      psychology, which he had eagerly awaited, proved to be a dull\r\n      subject full of muscular reactions and biological phrases rather\r\n      than the study of personality and influence. That was a noon\r\n      class, and it always sent him dozing. Having found that\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9csubjective and objective, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d answered most of the questions,\r\n      he used the phrase on all occasions, and it became the class joke\r\n      when, on a query being levelled at him, he was nudged awake by\r\n      Ferrenby or Sloane to gasp it out.\r\n\r\n      Mostly there were parties\xe2\x80\x94to Orange or the Shore, more rarely to\r\n      New York and Philadelphia, though one night they marshalled\r\n      fourteen waitresses out of Childs\xe2\x80\x99 and took them to ride down\r\n      Fifth Avenue on top of an auto bus. They all cut more classes\r\n      than were allowed, which meant an additional course the following\r\n      year, but spring was too rare to let anything interfere with\r\n      their colorful ramblings. In May Amory was elected to the\r\n      Sophomore Prom Committee, and when after a long evening\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      discussion with Alec they made out a tentative list of class\r\n      probabilities for the senior council, they placed themselves\r\n      among the surest. Oh it\xe2\x80\x99s so hard to write you what I really _feel_ when I think\r\n about you so much; you\xe2\x80\x99ve gotten to mean to me a _dream_ that I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n put on paper any more. I\r\n read it over about six times, especially the last part, but I do wish,\r\n sometimes, you\xe2\x80\x99d be more _frank_ and tell me what you really do think\r\n of me, yet your last letter was too good to be true, and I can hardly\r\n wait until June! A gambling\r\n      fever swept through the sophomore class and they bent over the\r\n      bones till three o\xe2\x80\x99clock many a sultry night. After one session\r\n      they came out of Sloane\xe2\x80\x99s room to find the dew fallen and the\r\n      stars old in the sky.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s borrow bicycles and take a ride,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory suggested.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAll right. I\xe2\x80\x99m not a bit tired and this is almost the last night\r\n      of the year, really, because the prom stuff starts Monday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      They found two unlocked bicycles in Holder Court and rode out\r\n      about half-past three along the Lawrenceville Road.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you going to do this summer, Amory?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t ask me\xe2\x80\x94same old things, I suppose. A month or two in Lake\r\n      Geneva\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m counting on you to be there in July, you know\xe2\x80\x94then\r\n      there\xe2\x80\x99ll be Minneapolis, and that means hundreds of summer hops,\r\n      parlor-snaking, getting bored\xe2\x80\x94But oh, Tom,\xe2\x80\x9d he added suddenly,\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9chasn\xe2\x80\x99t this year been slick!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d declared Tom emphatically, a new Tom, clothed by Brooks,\r\n      shod by Franks, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve won this game, but I feel as if I never\r\n      want to play another. You\xe2\x80\x99re all right\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99re a rubber ball, and\r\n      somehow it suits you, but I\xe2\x80\x99m sick of adapting myself to the\r\n      local snobbishness of this corner of the world. I want to go\r\n      where people aren\xe2\x80\x99t barred because of the color of their neckties\r\n      and the roll of their coats.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou can\xe2\x80\x99t, Tom,\xe2\x80\x9d argued Amory, as they rolled along through the\r\n      scattering night; \xe2\x80\x9cwherever you go now you\xe2\x80\x99ll always\r\n      unconsciously apply these standards of \xe2\x80\x98having it\xe2\x80\x99 or \xe2\x80\x98lacking\r\n      it.\xe2\x80\x99 For better or worse we\xe2\x80\x99ve stamped you; you\xe2\x80\x99re a Princeton\r\n      type!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, then,\xe2\x80\x9d complained Tom, his cracked voice rising\r\n      plaintively, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy do I have to come back at all? I\xe2\x80\x99ve learned all\r\n      that Princeton has to offer. Two years more of mere pedantry and\r\n      lying around a club aren\xe2\x80\x99t going to help. They\xe2\x80\x99re just going to\r\n      disorganize me, conventionalize me completely. Even now I\xe2\x80\x99m so\r\n      spineless that I wonder how I get away with it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, but you\xe2\x80\x99re missing the real point, Tom,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory interrupted.\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve just had your eyes opened to the snobbishness of the\r\n      world in a rather abrupt manner. Princeton invariably gives the\r\n      thoughtful man a social sense.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou consider you taught me that, don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked\r\n      quizzically, eying Amory in the half dark.\r\n\r\n      Amory laughed quietly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDidn\xe2\x80\x99t I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSometimes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said slowly, \xe2\x80\x9cI think you\xe2\x80\x99re my bad angel. I\r\n      might have been a pretty fair poet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCome on, that\xe2\x80\x99s rather hard. You chose to come to an Eastern\r\n      college. Either your eyes were opened to the mean scrambling\r\n      quality of people, or you\xe2\x80\x99d have gone through blind, and you\xe2\x80\x99d\r\n      hate to have done that\xe2\x80\x94been like Marty Kaye.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d he agreed, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99re right. I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have liked it. Still,\r\n      it\xe2\x80\x99s hard to be made a cynic at twenty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI was born one,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m a cynical idealist.\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\n      paused and wondered if that meant anything.\r\n\r\n      They reached the sleeping school of Lawrenceville, and turned to\r\n      ride back.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s good, this ride, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d Tom said presently.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes; it\xe2\x80\x99s a good finish, it\xe2\x80\x99s knock-out; everything\xe2\x80\x99s good\r\n      to-night. Oh, for a hot, languorous summer and Isabelle!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, you and your Isabelle! I\xe2\x80\x99ll bet she\xe2\x80\x99s a simple one... let\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      say some poetry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      So Amory declaimed \xe2\x80\x9cThe Ode to a Nightingale\xe2\x80\x9d to the bushes they\r\n      passed.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll never be a poet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory as he finished. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not\r\n      enough of a sensualist really; there are only a few obvious\r\n      things that I notice as primarily beautiful: women, spring\r\n      evenings, music at night, the sea; I don\xe2\x80\x99t catch the subtle\r\n      things like \xe2\x80\x98silver-snarling trumpets.\xe2\x80\x99 I may turn out an\r\n      intellectual, but I\xe2\x80\x99ll never write anything but mediocre poetry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      They rode into Princeton as the sun was making colored maps of\r\n      the sky behind the graduate school, and hurried to the\r\n      refreshment of a shower that would have to serve in place of\r\n      sleep. By noon the bright-costumed alumni crowded the streets\r\n      with their bands and choruses, and in the tents there was great\r\n      reunion under the orange-and-black banners that curled and\r\n      strained in the wind. Amory looked long at one house which bore\r\n      the legend \xe2\x80\x9cSixty-nine.\xe2\x80\x9d There a few gray-haired men sat and\r\n      talked quietly while the classes swept by in panorama of life.\r\n\r\n\r\n      UNDER THE ARC-LIGHT\r\n\r\n      Then tragedy\xe2\x80\x99s emerald eyes glared suddenly at Amory over the\r\n      edge of June. On the night after his ride to Lawrenceville a\r\n      crowd sallied to New York in quest of adventure, and started back\r\n      to Princeton about twelve o\xe2\x80\x99clock in two machines. It had been a\r\n      gay party and different stages of sobriety were represented.\r\n      Amory was in the car behind; they had taken the wrong road and\r\n      lost the way, and so were hurrying to catch up.\r\n\r\n      It was a clear night and the exhilaration of the road went to\r\n      Amory\xe2\x80\x99s head. He had the ghost of two stanzas of a poem forming\r\n      in his mind. ...\r\n\r\n  So the gray car crept nightward in the dark and there was no life\r\n  stirred as it went by....  As the still ocean paths before the shark\r\n  in starred and glittering waterways, beauty-high, the moon-swathed\r\n  trees divided, pair on pair, while flapping nightbirds cried across\r\n  the air....\r\n  A moment by an inn of lamps and shades, a yellow inn under a yellow\r\n  moon\xe2\x80\x94then silence, where crescendo laughter fades... the car swung\r\n  out again to the winds of June, mellowed the shadows where the\r\n  distance grew, then crushed the yellow shadows into blue....\r\n\r\n      They jolted to a stop, and Amory peered up, startled. A woman was\r\n      standing beside the road, talking to Alec at the wheel. Afterward\r\n      he remembered the harpy effect that her old kimono gave her, and\r\n      the cracked hollowness of her voice as she spoke:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou Princeton boys?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, there\xe2\x80\x99s one of you killed here, and two others about\r\n      dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9c_My God!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLook!\xe2\x80\x9d She pointed and they gazed in horror. Under the full\r\n      light of a roadside arc-light lay a form, face downward in a\r\n      widening circle of blood.\r\n\r\n      They sprang from the car. Amory thought of the back of that\r\n      head\xe2\x80\x94that hair\xe2\x80\x94that hair... and then they turned the form over.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s Dick\xe2\x80\x94Dick Humbird!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Christ!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cFeel his heart!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Then the insistent voice of the old crone in a sort of croaking\r\n      triumph:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s quite dead, all right. The car turned over. Two of the men\r\n      that weren\xe2\x80\x99t hurt just carried the others in, but this one\xe2\x80\x99s no\r\n      use.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory rushed into the house and the rest followed with a limp\r\n      mass that they laid on the sofa in the shoddy little front\r\n      parlor. Sloane, with his shoulder punctured, was on another\r\n      lounge. He was half delirious, and kept calling something about a\r\n      chemistry lecture at 8:10.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what happened,\xe2\x80\x9d said Ferrenby in a strained voice.\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDick was driving and he wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t give up the wheel; we told him\r\n      he\xe2\x80\x99d been drinking too much\xe2\x80\x94then there was this damn curve\xe2\x80\x94oh, my\r\n      _God!_...\xe2\x80\x9d He threw himself face downward on the floor and broke\r\n      into dry sobs.\r\n\r\n      The doctor had arrived, and Amory went over to the couch, where\r\n      some one handed him a sheet to put over the body. With a sudden\r\n      hardness, he raised one of the hands and let it fall back\r\n      inertly. The brow was cold but the face not expressionless. He\r\n      looked at the shoe-laces\xe2\x80\x94Dick had tied them that morning. _He_\r\n      had tied them\xe2\x80\x94and now he was this heavy white mass. All that\r\n      remained of the charm and personality of the Dick Humbird he had\r\n      known\xe2\x80\x94oh, it was all so horrible and unaristocratic and close to\r\n      the earth. All tragedy has that strain of the grotesque and\r\n      squalid\xe2\x80\x94so useless, futile... the way animals die.... Amory was\r\n      reminded of a cat that had lain horribly mangled in some alley of\r\n      his childhood.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSome one go to Princeton with Ferrenby.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory stepped outside the door and shivered slightly at the late\r\n      night wind\xe2\x80\x94a wind that stirred a broken fender on the mass of\r\n      bent metal to a plaintive, tinny sound.\r\n\r\n\r\n      CRESCENDO!\r\n\r\n      Next day, by a merciful chance, passed in a whirl. When Amory was\r\n      by himself his thoughts zigzagged inevitably to the picture of\r\n      that red mouth yawning incongruously in the white face, but with\r\n      a determined effort he piled present excitement upon the memory\r\n      of it and shut it coldly away from his mind.\r\n\r\n      Isabelle and her mother drove into town at four, and they rode up\r\n      smiling Prospect Avenue, through the gay crowd, to have tea at\r\n      Cottage. The clubs had their annual dinners that night, so at\r\n      seven he loaned her to a freshman and arranged to meet her in the\r\n      gymnasium at eleven, when the upper classmen were admitted to the\r\n      freshman dance. She was all he had expected, and he was happy and\r\n      eager to make that night the centre of every dream. At nine the\r\n      upper classes stood in front of the clubs as the freshman\r\n      torchlight parade rioted past, and Amory wondered if the\r\n      dress-suited groups against the dark, stately backgrounds and\r\n      under the flare of the torches made the night as brilliant to the\r\n      staring, cheering freshmen as it had been to him the year before.\r\n\r\n      The next day was another whirl. They lunched in a gay party of\r\n      six in a private dining-room at the club, while Isabelle and\r\n      Amory looked at each other tenderly over the fried chicken and\r\n      knew that their love was to be eternal. They danced away the prom\r\n      until five, and the stags cut in on Isabelle with joyous abandon,\r\n      which grew more and more enthusiastic as the hour grew late, and\r\n      their wines, stored in overcoat pockets in the coat room, made\r\n      old weariness wait until another day. The stag line is a most\r\n      homogeneous mass of men. It fairly sways with a single soul. A\r\n      dark-haired beauty dances by and there is a half-gasping sound as\r\n      the ripple surges forward and some one sleeker than the rest\r\n      darts out and cuts in. Then when the six-foot girl (brought by\r\n      Kaye in your class, and to whom he has been trying to introduce\r\n      you all evening) gallops by, the line surges back and the groups\r\n      face about and become intent on far corners of the hall, for\r\n      Kaye, anxious and perspiring, appears elbowing through the crowd\r\n      in search of familiar faces.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI say, old man, I\xe2\x80\x99ve got an awfully nice\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSorry, Kaye, but I\xe2\x80\x99m set for this one. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got to cut in on a\r\n      fella.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, the next one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x94ah\xe2\x80\x94er\xe2\x80\x94I swear I\xe2\x80\x99ve got to go cut in\xe2\x80\x94look me up when she\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      got a dance free.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      It delighted Amory when Isabelle suggested that they leave for a\r\n      while and drive around in her car. For a delicious hour that\r\n      passed too soon they glided the silent roads about Princeton and\r\n      talked from the surface of their hearts in shy excitement. Amory\r\n      felt strangely ingenuous and made no attempt to kiss her.\r\n\r\n      Next day they rode up through the Jersey country, had luncheon in\r\n      New York, and in the afternoon went to see a problem play at\r\n      which Isabelle wept all through the second act, rather to Amory\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      embarrassment\xe2\x80\x94though it filled him with tenderness to watch her.\r\n      He was tempted to lean over and kiss away her tears, and she\r\n      slipped her hand into his under cover of darkness to be pressed\r\n      softly.\r\n\r\n      Then at six they arrived at the Borges\xe2\x80\x99 summer place on Long\r\n      Island, and Amory rushed up-stairs to change into a dinner coat.\r\n      As he put in his studs he realized that he was enjoying life as\r\n      he would probably never enjoy it again. Everything was hallowed\r\n      by the haze of his own youth. He had arrived, abreast of the best\r\n      in his generation at Princeton. He was in love and his love was\r\n      returned. Turning on all the lights, he looked at himself in the\r\n      mirror, trying to find in his own face the qualities that made\r\n      him see clearer than the great crowd of people, that made him\r\n      decide firmly, and able to influence and follow his own will.\r\n      There was little in his life now that he would have changed. ...\r\n      Oxford might have been a bigger field.\r\n\r\n      Silently he admired himself. How conveniently well he looked, and\r\n      how well a dinner coat became him. He stepped into the hall and\r\n      then waited at the top of the stairs, for he heard footsteps\r\n      coming. It was Isabelle, and from the top of her shining hair to\r\n      her little golden slippers she had never seemed so beautiful.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIsabelle!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, half involuntarily, and held out his arms.\r\n      As in the story-books, she ran into them, and on that\r\n      half-minute, as their lips first touched, rested the high point\r\n      of vanity, the crest of his young egotism.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n      CHAPTER 3. The Egotist Considers\r\n\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOuch! Let me go!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He dropped his arms to his sides.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYour shirt stud\xe2\x80\x94it hurt me\xe2\x80\x94look!\xe2\x80\x9d She was looking down at her\r\n      neck, where a little blue spot about the size of a pea marred its\r\n      pallor.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Isabelle,\xe2\x80\x9d he reproached himself, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m a goopher. Really,\r\n      I\xe2\x80\x99m sorry\xe2\x80\x94I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t have held you so close.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      She looked up impatiently.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Amory, of course you couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it, and it didn\xe2\x80\x99t hurt\r\n      much; but what _are_ we going to do about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9c_Do_ about it?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94that spot; it\xe2\x80\x99ll disappear in a\r\n      second.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt isn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, after a moment of concentrated gazing,\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s still there\xe2\x80\x94and it looks like Old Nick\xe2\x80\x94oh, Amory, what\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\n      we do! It\xe2\x80\x99s _just_ the height of your shoulder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMassage it,\xe2\x80\x9d he suggested, repressing the faintest inclination\r\n      to laugh.\r\n\r\n      She rubbed it delicately with the tips of her fingers, and then a\r\n      tear gathered in the corner of her eye, and slid down her cheek.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Amory,\xe2\x80\x9d she said despairingly, lifting up a most pathetic\r\n      face, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll just make my whole neck _flame_ if I rub it. What\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\n      I do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      A quotation sailed into his head and he couldn\xe2\x80\x99t resist repeating\r\n      it aloud.\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cAll the perfumes of Arabia will not whiten this little hand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      She looked up and the sparkle of the tear in her eye was like\r\n      ice.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not very sympathetic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory mistook her meaning.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIsabelle, darling, I think it\xe2\x80\x99ll\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t touch me!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried. \xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t I enough on my mind and you\r\n      stand there and _laugh!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Then he slipped again.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, it _is_ funny, Isabelle, and we were talking the other day\r\n      about a sense of humor being\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      She was looking at him with something that was not a smile,\r\n      rather the faint, mirthless echo of a smile, in the corners of\r\n      her mouth.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, shut up!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried suddenly, and fled down the hallway\r\n      toward her room. Amory stood there, covered with remorseful\r\n      confusion.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDamn!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      When Isabelle reappeared she had thrown a light wrap about her\r\n      shoulders, and they descended the stairs in a silence that\r\n      endured through dinner.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIsabelle,\xe2\x80\x9d he began rather testily, as they arranged themselves\r\n      in the car, bound for a dance at the Greenwich Country Club,\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99re angry, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll be, too, in a minute. Let\xe2\x80\x99s kiss and make\r\n      up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Isabelle considered glumly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI hate to be laughed at,\xe2\x80\x9d she said finally.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t laugh any more. I\xe2\x80\x99m not laughing now, am I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t be so darned feminine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Her lips curled slightly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be anything I want.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory kept his temper with difficulty. He became aware that he\r\n      had not an ounce of real affection for Isabelle, but her coldness\r\n      piqued him. He wanted to kiss her, kiss her a lot, because then\r\n      he knew he could leave in the morning and not care. On the\r\n      contrary, if he didn\xe2\x80\x99t kiss her, it would worry him.... It would\r\n      interfere vaguely with his idea of himself as a conqueror. It\r\n      wasn\xe2\x80\x99t dignified to come off second best, _pleading_, with a\r\n      doughty warrior like Isabelle.\r\n\r\n      Perhaps she suspected this. At any rate, Amory watched the night\r\n      that should have been the consummation of romance glide by with\r\n      great moths overhead and the heavy fragrance of roadside gardens,\r\n      but without those broken words, those little sighs....\r\n\r\n      Afterward they suppered on ginger ale and devil\xe2\x80\x99s food in the\r\n      pantry, and Amory announced a decision.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m leaving early in the morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d he countered.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s no need.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHowever, I\xe2\x80\x99m going.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, if you insist on being ridiculous\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t put it that way,\xe2\x80\x9d he objected.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x94just because I won\xe2\x80\x99t let you kiss me. Do you think\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNow, Isabelle,\xe2\x80\x9d he interrupted, \xe2\x80\x9cyou know it\xe2\x80\x99s not that\xe2\x80\x94even\r\n      suppose it is. We\xe2\x80\x99ve reached the stage where we either ought to\r\n      kiss\xe2\x80\x94or\xe2\x80\x94or\xe2\x80\x94nothing. It isn\xe2\x80\x99t as if you were refusing on moral\r\n      grounds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      She hesitated.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI really don\xe2\x80\x99t know what to think about you,\xe2\x80\x9d she began, in a\r\n      feeble, perverse attempt at conciliation. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re so funny.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHow?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I thought you had a lot of self-confidence and all that;\r\n      remember you told me the other day that you could do anything you\r\n      wanted, or get anything you wanted?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory flushed. He _had_ told her a lot of things.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, you didn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to feel so self-confident to-night. Maybe\r\n      you\xe2\x80\x99re just plain conceited.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99m not,\xe2\x80\x9d he hesitated. \xe2\x80\x9cAt Princeton\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, you and Princeton! You\xe2\x80\x99d think that was the world, the way\r\n      you talk! Perhaps you _can_ write better than anybody else on\r\n      your old Princetonian; maybe the freshmen _do_ think you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\n      important\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t understand\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I do,\xe2\x80\x9d she interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cI _do_, because you\xe2\x80\x99re always\r\n      talking about yourself and I used to like it; now I don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHave I to-night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s just the point,\xe2\x80\x9d insisted Isabelle. \xe2\x80\x9cYou got all upset\r\n      to-night. You just sat and watched my eyes. Besides, I have to\r\n      think all the time I\xe2\x80\x99m talking to you\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99re so critical.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI make you think, do I?\xe2\x80\x9d Amory repeated with a touch of vanity.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a nervous strain\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94this emphatically\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cand when you analyze\r\n      every little emotion and instinct I just don\xe2\x80\x99t have \xe2\x80\x99em.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI know.\xe2\x80\x9d Amory admitted her point and shook his head helplessly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s go.\xe2\x80\x9d She stood up.\r\n\r\n      He rose abstractedly and they walked to the foot of the stairs.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat train can I get?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s one about 9:11 if you really must go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I\xe2\x80\x99ve got to go, really. Good night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGood night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      They were at the head of the stairs, and as Amory turned into his\r\n      room he thought he caught just the faintest cloud of discontent\r\n      in her face. He lay awake in the darkness and wondered how much\r\n      he cared\xe2\x80\x94how much of his sudden unhappiness was hurt\r\n      vanity\xe2\x80\x94whether he was, after all, temperamentally unfitted for\r\n      romance.\r\n\r\n      When he awoke, it was with a glad flood of consciousness. The\r\n      early wind stirred the chintz curtains at the windows and he was\r\n      idly puzzled not to be in his room at Princeton with his school\r\n      football picture over the bureau and the Triangle Club on the\r\n      wall opposite. Then the grandfather\xe2\x80\x99s clock in the hall outside\r\n      struck eight, and the memory of the night before came to him. He\r\n      was out of bed, dressing, like the wind; he must get out of the\r\n      house before he saw Isabelle. What had seemed a melancholy\r\n      happening, now seemed a tiresome anticlimax. He was dressed at\r\n      half past, so he sat down by the window; felt that the sinews of\r\n      his heart were twisted somewhat more than he had thought. What an\r\n      ironic mockery the morning seemed!\xe2\x80\x94bright and sunny, and full of\r\n      the smell of the garden; hearing Mrs. Borge\xe2\x80\x99s voice in the\r\n      sun-parlor below, he wondered where was Isabelle.\r\n\r\n      There was a knock at the door.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThe car will be around at ten minutes of nine, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He returned to his contemplation of the outdoors, and began\r\n      repeating over and over, mechanically, a verse from Browning,\r\n      which he had once quoted to Isabelle in a letter:\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cEach life unfulfilled, you see, It hangs still, patchy and scrappy;\r\n   We have not sighed deep, laughed free, Starved, feasted,\r\n   despaired\xe2\x80\x94been happy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      But his life would not be unfulfilled. He took a sombre\r\n      satisfaction in thinking that perhaps all along she had been\r\n      nothing except what he had read into her; that this was her high\r\n      point, that no one else would ever make her think. Yet that was\r\n      what she had objected to in him; and Amory was suddenly tired of\r\n      thinking, thinking!\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDamn her!\xe2\x80\x9d he said bitterly, \xe2\x80\x9cshe\xe2\x80\x99s spoiled my year!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n      THE SUPERMAN GROWS CARELESS\r\n\r\n      On a dusty day in September Amory arrived in Princeton and joined\r\n      the sweltering crowd of conditioned men who thronged the streets.\r\n      It seemed a stupid way to commence his upper-class years, to\r\n      spend four hours a morning in the stuffy room of a tutoring\r\n      school, imbibing the infinite boredom of conic sections. Mr.\r\n      Rooney, pander to the dull, conducted the class and smoked\r\n      innumerable Pall Malls as he drew diagrams and worked equations\r\n      from six in the morning until midnight.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNow, Langueduc, if I used that formula, where would my A point\r\n      be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Langueduc lazily shifts his six-foot-three of football material\r\n      and tries to concentrate.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94ah\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m damned if I know, Mr. Rooney.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, why of course, of course you can\xe2\x80\x99t _use_ that formula.\r\n      _That\xe2\x80\x99s_ what I wanted you to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, sure, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDo you see why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou bet\xe2\x80\x94I suppose so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIf you don\xe2\x80\x99t see, tell me. I\xe2\x80\x99m here to show you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, Mr. Rooney, if you don\xe2\x80\x99t mind, I wish you\xe2\x80\x99d go over that\r\n      again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGladly. Now here\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98A\xe2\x80\x99...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The room was a study in stupidity\xe2\x80\x94two huge stands for paper, Mr.\r\n      Rooney in his shirt-sleeves in front of them, and slouched around\r\n      on chairs, a dozen men: Fred Sloane, the pitcher, who absolutely\r\n      _had_ to get eligible; \xe2\x80\x9cSlim\xe2\x80\x9d Langueduc, who would beat Yale this\r\n      fall, if only he could master a poor fifty per cent; McDowell,\r\n      gay young sophomore, who thought it was quite a sporting thing to\r\n      be tutoring here with all these prominent athletes.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThose poor birds who haven\xe2\x80\x99t a cent to tutor, and have to study\r\n      during the term are the ones I pity,\xe2\x80\x9d he announced to Amory one\r\n      day, with a flaccid camaraderie in the droop of the cigarette\r\n      from his pale lips. \xe2\x80\x9cI should think it would be such a bore,\r\n      there\xe2\x80\x99s so much else to do in New York during the term. I suppose\r\n      they don\xe2\x80\x99t know what they miss, anyhow.\xe2\x80\x9d There was such an air of\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cyou and I\xe2\x80\x9d about Mr. McDowell that Amory very nearly pushed him\r\n      out of the open window when he said this. ... Next February his\r\n      mother would wonder why he didn\xe2\x80\x99t make a club and increase his\r\n      allowance... simple little nut....\r\n\r\n      Through the smoke and the air of solemn, dense earnestness that\r\n      filled the room would come the inevitable helpless cry:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t get it! Repeat that, Mr. Rooney!\xe2\x80\x9d Most of them were so\r\n      stupid or careless that they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t admit when they didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      understand, and Amory was of the latter. He found it impossible\r\n      to study conic sections; something in their calm and tantalizing\r\n      respectability breathing defiantly through Mr. Rooney\xe2\x80\x99s fetid\r\n      parlors distorted their equations into insoluble anagrams. He\r\n      made a last night\xe2\x80\x99s effort with the proverbial wet towel, and\r\n      then blissfully took the exam, wondering unhappily why all the\r\n      color and ambition of the spring before had faded out. Somehow,\r\n      with the defection of Isabelle the idea of undergraduate success\r\n      had loosed its grasp on his imagination, and he contemplated a\r\n      possible failure to pass off his condition with equanimity, even\r\n      though it would arbitrarily mean his removal from the\r\n      Princetonian board and the slaughter of his chances for the\r\n      Senior Council.\r\n\r\n      There was always his luck.\r\n\r\n      He yawned, scribbled his honor pledge on the cover, and sauntered\r\n      from the room.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIf you don\xe2\x80\x99t pass it,\xe2\x80\x9d said the newly arrived Alec as they sat\r\n      on the window-seat of Amory\xe2\x80\x99s room and mused upon a scheme of\r\n      wall decoration, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99re the world\xe2\x80\x99s worst goopher. Your stock\r\n      will go down like an elevator at the club and on the campus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, hell, I know it. Why rub it in?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Cause you deserve it. Anybody that\xe2\x80\x99d risk what you were in line\r\n      for _ought_ to be ineligible for Princetonian chairman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, drop the subject,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory protested. \xe2\x80\x9cWatch and wait and shut\r\n      up. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want every one at the club asking me about it, as if\r\n      I were a prize potato being fattened for a vegetable show.\xe2\x80\x9d One\r\n      evening a week later Amory stopped below his own window on the\r\n      way to Renwick\xe2\x80\x99s, and, seeing a light, called up:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Tom, any mail?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Alec\xe2\x80\x99s head appeared against the yellow square of light.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes, your result\xe2\x80\x99s here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      His heart clamored violently.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it, blue or pink?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t know. Better come up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He walked into the room and straight over to the table, and then\r\n      suddenly noticed that there were other people in the room.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Lo, Kerry.\xe2\x80\x9d He was most polite. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, men of Princeton.\xe2\x80\x9d They\r\n      seemed to be mostly friends, so he picked up the envelope marked\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cRegistrar\xe2\x80\x99s Office,\xe2\x80\x9d and weighed it nervously.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWe have here quite a slip of paper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOpen it, Amory.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cJust to be dramatic, I\xe2\x80\x99ll let you know that if it\xe2\x80\x99s blue, my\r\n      name is withdrawn from the editorial board of the Prince, and my\r\n      short career is over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He paused, and then saw for the first time Ferrenby\xe2\x80\x99s eyes,\r\n      wearing a hungry look and watching him eagerly. Amory returned\r\n      the gaze pointedly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWatch my face, gentlemen, for the primitive emotions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He tore it open and held the slip up to the light.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cPink or blue?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSay what it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re all ears, Amory.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSmile or swear\xe2\x80\x94or something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      There was a pause... a small crowd of seconds swept by... then he\r\n      looked again and another crowd went on into time.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBlue as the sky, gentlemen....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n      AFTERMATH\r\n\r\n      What Amory did that year from early September to late in the\r\n      spring was so purposeless and inconsecutive that it seems\r\n      scarcely worth recording. He was, of course, immediately sorry\r\n      for what he had lost. His philosophy of success had tumbled down\r\n      upon him, and he looked for the reasons.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYour own laziness,\xe2\x80\x9d said Alec later.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94something deeper than that. I\xe2\x80\x99ve begun to feel that I was\r\n      meant to lose this chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re rather off you at the club, you know; every man that\r\n      doesn\xe2\x80\x99t come through makes our crowd just so much weaker.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI hate that point of view.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOf course, with a little effort you could still stage a\r\n      comeback.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m through\xe2\x80\x94as far as ever being a power in college is\r\n      concerned.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBut, Amory, honestly, what makes me the angriest isn\xe2\x80\x99t the fact\r\n      that you won\xe2\x80\x99t be chairman of the Prince and on the Senior\r\n      Council, but just that you didn\xe2\x80\x99t get down and pass that exam.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNot me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory slowly; \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m mad at the concrete thing. My\r\n      own idleness was quite in accord with my system, but the luck\r\n      broke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYour system broke, you mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMaybe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, what are you going to do? Get a better one quick, or just\r\n      bum around for two more years as a has-been?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know yet...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Amory, buck up!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMaybe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory\xe2\x80\x99s point of view, though dangerous, was not far from the\r\n      true one. If his reactions to his environment could be tabulated,\r\n      the chart would have appeared like this, beginning with his\r\n      earliest years:\r\n\r\n 1. The fundamental Amory.\r\n 2. Amory plus Beatrice.\r\n 3. Amory plus Beatrice plus Minneapolis.\r\n\r\n      Then St. Regis\xe2\x80\x99 had pulled him to pieces and started him over\r\n      again:\r\n\r\n 4. Amory plus St. Regis\xe2\x80\x99.\r\n 5. Amory plus St. Regis\xe2\x80\x99 plus Princeton.\r\n\r\n      That had been his nearest approach to success through conformity.\r\n      The fundamental Amory, idle, imaginative, rebellious, had been\r\n      nearly snowed under. He had conformed, he had succeeded, but as\r\n      his imagination was neither satisfied nor grasped by his own\r\n      success, he had listlessly, half-accidentally chucked the whole\r\n      thing and become again:\r\n\r\n 6. The fundamental Amory.\r\n\r\n\r\n      FINANCIAL\r\n\r\n      His father died quietly and inconspicuously at Thanksgiving. The\r\n      incongruity of death with either the beauties of Lake Geneva or\r\n      with his mother\xe2\x80\x99s dignified, reticent attitude diverted him, and\r\n      he looked at the funeral with an amused tolerance. He decided\r\n      that burial was after all preferable to cremation, and he smiled\r\n      at his old boyhood choice, slow oxidation in the top of a tree.\r\n      The day after the ceremony he was amusing himself in the great\r\n      library by sinking back on a couch in graceful mortuary\r\n      attitudes, trying to determine whether he would, when his day\r\n      came, be found with his arms crossed piously over his chest\r\n      (Monsignor Darcy had once advocated this posture as being the\r\n      most distinguished), or with his hands clasped behind his head, a\r\n      more pagan and Byronic attitude.\r\n\r\n      What interested him much more than the final departure of his\r\n      father from things mundane was a tri-cornered conversation\r\n      between Beatrice, Mr. Barton, of Barton and Krogman, their\r\n      lawyers, and himself, that took place several days after the\r\n      funeral. For the first time he came into actual cognizance of the\r\n      family finances, and realized what a tidy fortune had once been\r\n      under his father\xe2\x80\x99s management. He took a ledger labelled \xe2\x80\x9c1906\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      and ran through it rather carefully. The total expenditure that\r\n      year had come to something over one hundred and ten thousand\r\n      dollars. Forty thousand of this had been Beatrice\xe2\x80\x99s own income,\r\n      and there had been no attempt to account for it: it was all under\r\n      the heading, \xe2\x80\x9cDrafts, checks, and letters of credit forwarded to\r\n      Beatrice Blaine.\xe2\x80\x9d The dispersal of the rest was rather minutely\r\n      itemized: the taxes and improvements on the Lake Geneva estate\r\n      had come to almost nine thousand dollars; the general up-keep,\r\n      including Beatrice\xe2\x80\x99s electric and a French car, bought that year,\r\n      was over thirty-five thousand dollars. The rest was fully taken\r\n      care of, and there were invariably items which failed to balance\r\n      on the right side of the ledger.\r\n\r\n      In the volume for 1912 Amory was shocked to discover the decrease\r\n      in the number of bond holdings and the great drop in the income.\r\n      In the case of Beatrice\xe2\x80\x99s money this was not so pronounced, but\r\n      it was obvious that his father had devoted the previous year to\r\n      several unfortunate gambles in oil. Very little of the oil had\r\n      been burned, but Stephen Blaine had been rather badly singed. The\r\n      next year and the next and the next showed similar decreases, and\r\n      Beatrice had for the first time begun using her own money for\r\n      keeping up the house. Yet her doctor\xe2\x80\x99s bill for 1913 had been\r\n      over nine thousand dollars.\r\n\r\n      About the exact state of things Mr. Barton was quite vague and\r\n      confused. There had been recent investments, the outcome of which\r\n      was for the present problematical, and he had an idea there were\r\n      further speculations and exchanges concerning which he had not\r\n      been consulted.\r\n\r\n      It was not for several months that Beatrice wrote Amory the full\r\n      situation. The entire residue of the Blaine and O\xe2\x80\x99Hara fortunes\r\n      consisted of the place at Lake Geneva and approximately a half\r\n      million dollars, invested now in fairly conservative six-per-cent\r\n      holdings. In fact, Beatrice wrote that she was putting the money\r\n      into railroad and street-car bonds as fast as she could\r\n      conveniently transfer it.\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cI am quite sure,\xe2\x80\x9d she wrote to Amory, \xe2\x80\x9cthat if there is one thing we\r\n  can be positive of, it is that people will not stay in one place. \r\n  This Ford person has certainly made the most of that idea.  So I am\r\n  instructing Mr. Barton to specialize on such things as Northern\r\n  Pacific and these Rapid Transit Companies, as they call the\r\n  street-cars.  I shall never forgive myself for not buying Bethlehem\r\n  Steel.  I\xe2\x80\x99ve heard the most fascinating stories.  You must go into\r\n  finance, Amory.  I\xe2\x80\x99m sure you would revel in it. You start as a\r\n  messenger or a teller, I believe, and from that you go up\xe2\x80\x94almost\r\n  indefinitely.  I\xe2\x80\x99m sure if I were a man I\xe2\x80\x99d love the handling of\r\n  money; it has become quite a senile passion with me. Before I get any\r\n  farther I want to discuss something.  A Mrs. Bispam, an overcordial\r\n  little lady whom I met at a tea the other day, told me that her son,\r\n  he is at Yale, wrote her that all the boys there wore their summer\r\n  underwear all during the winter, and also went about with their heads\r\n  wet and in low shoes on the coldest days.  Now, Amory, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\n  whether that is a fad at Princeton too, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t want you to be so\r\n  foolish.  It not only inclines a young man to pneumonia and infantile\r\n  paralysis, but to all forms of lung trouble, to which you are\r\n  particularly inclined.  You cannot experiment with your health.  I\r\n  have found that out.  I will not make myself ridiculous as some\r\n  mothers no doubt do, by insisting that you wear overshoes, though I\r\n  remember one Christmas you wore them around constantly without a\r\n  single buckle latched, making such a curious swishing sound, and you\r\n  refused to buckle them because it was not the thing to do.  The very\r\n  next Christmas you would not wear even rubbers, though I begged you. \r\n  You are nearly twenty years old now, dear, and I can\xe2\x80\x99t be with you\r\n  constantly to find whether you are doing the sensible thing.\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cThis has been a very _practical_ letter.  I warned you in my last\r\n  that the lack of money to do the things one wants to makes one quite\r\n  prosy and domestic, but there is still plenty for everything if we\r\n  are not too extravagant.  Take care of yourself, my dear boy, and do\r\n  try to write at least _once_ a week, because I imagine all sorts of\r\n  horrible things if I don\xe2\x80\x99t hear from you. Affectionately,           \r\n  MOTHER.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n      FIRST APPEARANCE OF THE TERM \xe2\x80\x9cPERSONAGE\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Monsignor Darcy invited Amory up to the Stuart palace on the\r\n      Hudson for a week at Christmas, and they had enormous\r\n      conversations around the open fire. Monsignor was growing a\r\n      trifle stouter and his personality had expanded even with that,\r\n      and Amory felt both rest and security in sinking into a squat,\r\n      cushioned chair and joining him in the middle-aged sanity of a\r\n      cigar.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve felt like leaving college, Monsignor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAll my career\xe2\x80\x99s gone up in smoke; you think it\xe2\x80\x99s petty and all\r\n      that, but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNot at all petty. I think it\xe2\x80\x99s most important. I want to hear\r\n      the whole thing. Everything you\xe2\x80\x99ve been doing since I saw you\r\n      last.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory talked; he went thoroughly into the destruction of his\r\n      egotistic highways, and in a half-hour the listless quality had\r\n      left his voice.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat would you do if you left college?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Monsignor.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t know. I\xe2\x80\x99d like to travel, but of course this tiresome war\r\n      prevents that. Anyways, mother would hate not having me graduate.\r\n      I\xe2\x80\x99m just at sea. Kerry Holiday wants me to go over with him and\r\n      join the Lafayette Esquadrille.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou know you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like to go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSometimes I would\xe2\x80\x94to-night I\xe2\x80\x99d go in a second.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, you\xe2\x80\x99d have to be very much more tired of life than I think\r\n      you are. I know you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m afraid you do,\xe2\x80\x9d agreed Amory reluctantly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt just seemed an\r\n      easy way out of everything\xe2\x80\x94when I think of another useless,\r\n      draggy year.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I know; but to tell you the truth, I\xe2\x80\x99m not worried about\r\n      you; you seem to me to be progressing perfectly naturally.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory objected. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve lost half my personality in a year.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNot a bit of it!\xe2\x80\x9d scoffed Monsignor. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve lost a great amount\r\n      of vanity and that\xe2\x80\x99s all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLordy! I feel, anyway, as if I\xe2\x80\x99d gone through another fifth form\r\n      at St. Regis\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d Monsignor shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cThat was a misfortune; this has\r\n      been a good thing. Whatever worth while comes to you, won\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\n      through the channels you were searching last year.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat could be more unprofitable than my present lack of pep?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps in itself... but you\xe2\x80\x99re developing. This has given you\r\n      time to think and you\xe2\x80\x99re casting off a lot of your old luggage\r\n      about success and the superman and all. People like us can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      adopt whole theories, as you did. If we can do the next thing,\r\n      and have an hour a day to think in, we can accomplish marvels,\r\n      but as far as any high-handed scheme of blind dominance is\r\n      concerned\xe2\x80\x94we\xe2\x80\x99d just make asses of ourselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBut, Monsignor, I can\xe2\x80\x99t do the next thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAmory, between you and me, I have only just learned to do it\r\n      myself. I can do the one hundred things beyond the next thing,\r\n      but I stub my toe on that, just as you stubbed your toe on\r\n      mathematics this fall.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhy do we have to do the next thing? It never seems the sort of\r\n      thing I should do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWe have to do it because we\xe2\x80\x99re not personalities, but\r\n      personages.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a good line\xe2\x80\x94what do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cA personality is what you thought you were, what this Kerry and\r\n      Sloane you tell me of evidently are. Personality is a physical\r\n      matter almost entirely; it lowers the people it acts on\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen\r\n      it vanish in a long sickness. But while a personality is active,\r\n      it overrides \xe2\x80\x98the next thing.\xe2\x80\x99 Now a personage, on the other\r\n      hand, gathers. He is never thought of apart from what he\xe2\x80\x99s done.\r\n      He\xe2\x80\x99s a bar on which a thousand things have been hung\xe2\x80\x94glittering\r\n      things sometimes, as ours are; but he uses those things with a\r\n      cold mentality back of them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd several of my most glittering possessions had fallen off\r\n      when I needed them.\xe2\x80\x9d Amory continued the simile eagerly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes, that\xe2\x80\x99s it; when you feel that your garnered prestige and\r\n      talents and all that are hung out, you need never bother about\r\n      anybody; you can cope with them without difficulty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBut, on the other hand, if I haven\xe2\x80\x99t my possessions, I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\n      helpless!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAbsolutely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s certainly an idea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNow you\xe2\x80\x99ve a clean start\xe2\x80\x94a start Kerry or Sloane can\r\n      constitutionally never have. You brushed three or four ornaments\r\n      down, and, in a fit of pique, knocked off the rest of them. The\r\n      thing now is to collect some new ones, and the farther you look\r\n      ahead in the collecting the better. But remember, do the next\r\n      thing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHow clear you can make things!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      So they talked, often about themselves, sometimes of philosophy\r\n      and religion, and life as respectively a game or a mystery. The\r\n      priest seemed to guess Amory\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts before they were clear in\r\n      his own head, so closely related were their minds in form and\r\n      groove.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhy do I make lists?\xe2\x80\x9d Amory asked him one night. \xe2\x80\x9cLists of all\r\n      sorts of things?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBecause you\xe2\x80\x99re a mediaevalist,\xe2\x80\x9d Monsignor answered. \xe2\x80\x9cWe both\r\n      are. It\xe2\x80\x99s the passion for classifying and finding a type.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a desire to get something definite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the nucleus of scholastic philosophy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI was beginning to think I was growing eccentric till I came up\r\n      here. It was a pose, I guess.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t worry about that; for you not posing may be the biggest\r\n      pose of all. Pose\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBut do the next thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      After Amory returned to college he received several letters from\r\n      Monsignor which gave him more egotistic food for consumption.\r\n\r\n  I am afraid that I gave you too much assurance of your inevitable\r\n  safety, and you must remember that I did that through faith in your\r\n  springs of effort; not in the silly conviction that you will arrive\r\n  without struggle.  Some nuances of character you will have to take\r\n  for granted in yourself, though you must be careful in confessing\r\n  them to others.  You are unsentimental, almost incapable of\r\n  affection, astute without being cunning and vain without being proud.\r\n  Don\xe2\x80\x99t let yourself feel worthless; often through life you will really\r\n  be at your worst when you seem to think best of yourself; and don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n  worry about losing your \xe2\x80\x9cpersonality,\xe2\x80\x9d as you persist in calling it;\r\n  at fifteen you had the radiance of early morning, at twenty you will\r\n  begin to have the melancholy brilliance of the moon, and when you are\r\n  my age you will give out, as I do, the genial golden warmth of 4 P.M.\r\n  If you write me letters, please let them be natural ones.  Your last,\r\n  that dissertation on architecture, was perfectly awful\xe2\x80\x94 so \xe2\x80\x9chighbrow\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n  that I picture you living in an intellectual and emotional vacuum;\r\n  and beware of trying to classify people too definitely into types;\r\n  you will find that all through their youth they will persist\r\n  annoyingly in jumping from class to class, and by pasting a\r\n  supercilious label on every one you meet you are merely packing a\r\n  Jack-in-the-box that will spring up and leer at you when you begin to\r\n  come into really antagonistic contact with the world.  An\r\n  idealization of some such a man as Leonardo da Vinci would be a more\r\n  valuable beacon to you at present.\r\n  You are bound to go up and down, just as I did in my youth, but do\r\n  keep your clarity of mind, and if fools or sages dare to criticise\r\n  don\xe2\x80\x99t blame yourself too much.\r\n  You say that convention is all that really keeps you straight in this\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cwoman proposition\xe2\x80\x9d; but it\xe2\x80\x99s more than that, Amory; it\xe2\x80\x99s the fear\r\n  that what you begin you can\xe2\x80\x99t stop; you would run amuck, and I know\r\n  whereof I speak; it\xe2\x80\x99s that half-miraculous sixth sense by which you\r\n  detect evil, it\xe2\x80\x99s the half-realized fear of God in your heart.\r\n  Whatever your metier proves to be\xe2\x80\x94religion, architecture,\r\n  literature\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m sure you would be much safer anchored to the Church,\r\n  but I won\xe2\x80\x99t risk my influence by arguing with you even though I am\r\n  secretly sure that the \xe2\x80\x9cblack chasm of Romanism\xe2\x80\x9d yawns beneath you. \r\n  Do write me soon.\r\n    With affectionate regards,          THAYER DARCY.\r\n\r\n      Even Amory\xe2\x80\x99s reading paled during this period; he delved further\r\n      into the misty side streets of literature: Huysmans, Walter\r\n      Pater, Theophile Gautier, and the racier sections of Rabelais,\r\n      Boccaccio, Petronius, and Suetonius. One week, through general\r\n      curiosity, he inspected the private libraries of his classmates\r\n      and found Sloane\xe2\x80\x99s as typical as any: sets of Kipling, O. Henry,\r\n      John Fox, Jr., and Richard Harding Davis; \xe2\x80\x9cWhat Every Middle-Aged\r\n      Woman Ought to Know,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cThe Spell of the Yukon\xe2\x80\x9d; a \xe2\x80\x9cgift\xe2\x80\x9d copy of\r\n      James Whitcomb Riley, an assortment of battered, annotated\r\n      schoolbooks, and, finally, to his surprise, one of his own late\r\n      discoveries, the collected poems of Rupert Brooke.\r\n\r\n      Together with Tom D\xe2\x80\x99Invilliers, he sought among the lights of\r\n      Princeton for some one who might found the Great American Poetic\r\n      Tradition.\r\n\r\n      The undergraduate body itself was rather more interesting that\r\n      year than had been the entirely Philistine Princeton of two years\r\n      before. Things had livened surprisingly, though at the sacrifice\r\n      of much of the spontaneous charm of freshman year. In the old\r\n      Princeton they would never have discovered Tanaduke Wylie.\r\n      Tanaduke was a sophomore, with tremendous ears and a way of\r\n      saying, \xe2\x80\x9cThe earth swirls down through the ominous moons of\r\n      preconsidered generations!\xe2\x80\x9d that made them vaguely wonder why it\r\n      did not sound quite clear, but never question that it was the\r\n      utterance of a supersoul. At least so Tom and Amory took him.\r\n      They told him in all earnestness that he had a mind like\r\n      Shelley\xe2\x80\x99s, and featured his ultrafree free verse and prose poetry\r\n      in the Nassau Literary Magazine. But Tanaduke\xe2\x80\x99s genius absorbed\r\n      the many colors of the age, and he took to the Bohemian life, to\r\n      their great disappointment. He talked of Greenwich Village now\r\n      instead of \xe2\x80\x9cnoon-swirled moons,\xe2\x80\x9d and met winter muses,\r\n      unacademic, and cloistered by Forty-second Street and Broadway,\r\n      instead of the Shelleyan dream-children with whom he had regaled\r\n      their expectant appreciation. So they surrendered Tanaduke to the\r\n      futurists, deciding that he and his flaming ties would do better\r\n      there. Tom gave him the final advice that he should stop writing\r\n      for two years and read the complete works of Alexander Pope four\r\n      times, but on Amory\xe2\x80\x99s suggestion that Pope for Tanaduke was like\r\n      foot-ease for stomach trouble, they withdrew in laughter, and\r\n      called it a coin\xe2\x80\x99s toss whether this genius was too big or too\r\n      petty for them.\r\n\r\n      Amory rather scornfully avoided the popular professors who\r\n      dispensed easy epigrams and thimblefuls of Chartreuse to groups\r\n      of admirers every night. He was disappointed, too, at the air of\r\n      general uncertainty on every subject that seemed linked with the\r\n      pedantic temperament; his opinions took shape in a miniature\r\n      satire called \xe2\x80\x9cIn a Lecture-Room,\xe2\x80\x9d which he persuaded Tom to\r\n      print in the Nassau Lit.\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cGood-morning, Fool... Three times a week You hold us helpless while\r\n   you speak, Teasing our thirsty souls with the Sleek \xe2\x80\x98yeas\xe2\x80\x99 of your\r\n   philosophy... Well, here we are, your hundred sheep, Tune up, play\r\n   on, pour forth... we sleep... You are a student, so they say; You\r\n   hammered out the other day A syllabus, from what we know Of some\r\n   forgotten folio; You\xe2\x80\x99d sniffled through an era\xe2\x80\x99s must, Filling your\r\n   nostrils up with dust, And then, arising from your knees, Published,\r\n   in one gigantic sneeze... But here\xe2\x80\x99s a neighbor on my right, An\r\n   Eager Ass, considered bright; Asker of questions....  How he\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\n   stand, With earnest air and fidgy hand, After this hour, telling you\r\n   He sat all night and burrowed through Your book....  Oh, you\xe2\x80\x99ll be\r\n   coy and he Will simulate precosity, And pedants both, you\xe2\x80\x99ll smile\r\n   and smirk, And leer, and hasten back to work....\r\n    \xe2\x80\x99Twas this day week, sir, you returned A theme of mine, from which\r\n    I learned (Through various comment on the side Which you had\r\n    scrawled) that I defied The _highest rules of criticism_ For\r\n    _cheap_ and _careless_ witticism.... \xe2\x80\x98Are you quite sure that this\r\n    could be?\xe2\x80\x99 And \xe2\x80\x98Shaw is no authority!\xe2\x80\x99 But Eager Ass, with what\r\n    he\xe2\x80\x99s sent, Plays havoc with your best per cent.\r\n    Still\xe2\x80\x94still I meet you here and there... When Shakespeare\xe2\x80\x99s played\r\n    you hold a chair, And some defunct, moth-eaten star Enchants the\r\n    mental prig you are... A radical comes down and shocks The\r\n    atheistic orthodox? You\xe2\x80\x99re representing Common Sense, Mouth open,\r\n    in the audience. And, sometimes, even chapel lures That conscious\r\n    tolerance of yours, That broad and beaming view of truth (Including\r\n    Kant and General Booth...) And so from shock to shock you live, A\r\n    hollow, pale affirmative...\r\n    The hour\xe2\x80\x99s up... and roused from rest One hundred children of the\r\n    blest Cheat you a word or two with feet That down the noisy\r\n    aisle-ways beat... Forget on _narrow-minded earth_ The Mighty Yawn\r\n    that gave you birth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      In April, Kerry Holiday left college and sailed for France to\r\n      enroll in the Lafayette Esquadrille. Amory\xe2\x80\x99s envy and admiration\r\n      of this step was drowned in an experience of his own to which he\r\n      never succeeded in giving an appropriate value, but which,\r\n      nevertheless, haunted him for three years afterward.\r\n\r\n\r\n      THE DEVIL\r\n\r\n      Healy\xe2\x80\x99s they left at twelve and taxied to Bistolary\xe2\x80\x99s. There were\r\n      Axia Marlowe and Phoebe Column, from the Summer Garden show, Fred\r\n      Sloane and Amory. The evening was so very young that they felt\r\n      ridiculous with surplus energy, and burst into the cafe like\r\n      Dionysian revellers.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cTable for four in the middle of the floor,\xe2\x80\x9d yelled Phoebe.\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHurry, old dear, tell \xe2\x80\x99em we\xe2\x80\x99re here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cTell \xe2\x80\x99em to play \xe2\x80\x98Admiration\xe2\x80\x99!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Sloane. \xe2\x80\x9cYou two order;\r\n      Phoebe and I are going to shake a wicked calf,\xe2\x80\x9d and they sailed\r\n      off in the muddled crowd. Axia and Amory, acquaintances of an\r\n      hour, jostled behind a waiter to a table at a point of vantage;\r\n      there they took seats and watched.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s Findle Margotson, from New Haven!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried above the\r\n      uproar. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Lo, Findle! Whoo-ee!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Axia!\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted in salutation. \xe2\x80\x9cC\xe2\x80\x99mon over to our table.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo!\xe2\x80\x9d Amory whispered.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t do it, Findle; I\xe2\x80\x99m with somebody else! Call me up\r\n      to-morrow about one o\xe2\x80\x99clock!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Findle, a nondescript man-about-Bisty\xe2\x80\x99s, answered incoherently\r\n      and turned back to the brilliant blonde whom he was endeavoring\r\n      to steer around the room.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s a natural damn fool,\xe2\x80\x9d commented Amory.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, he\xe2\x80\x99s all right. Here\xe2\x80\x99s the old jitney waiter. If you ask me,\r\n      I want a double Daiquiri.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMake it four.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The crowd whirled and changed and shifted. They were mostly from\r\n      the colleges, with a scattering of the male refuse of Broadway,\r\n      and women of two types, the higher of which was the chorus girl.\r\n      On the whole it was a typical crowd, and their party as typical\r\n      as any. About three-fourths of the whole business was for effect\r\n      and therefore harmless, ended at the door of the cafe, soon\r\n      enough for the five-o\xe2\x80\x99clock train back to Yale or Princeton;\r\n      about one-fourth continued on into the dimmer hours and gathered\r\n      strange dust from strange places.
