Their party was scheduled to be\r\n      one of the harmless kind. Fred Sloane and Phoebe Column were old\r\n      friends; Axia and Amory new ones. But strange things are prepared\r\n      even in the dead of night, and the unusual, which lurks least in\r\n      the cafe, home of the prosaic and inevitable, was preparing to\r\n      spoil for him the waning romance of Broadway. The way it took was\r\n      so inexpressibly terrible, so unbelievable, that afterward he\r\n      never thought of it as experience; but it was a scene from a\r\n      misty tragedy, played far behind the veil, and that it meant\r\n      something definite he knew.\r\n\r\n      About one o\xe2\x80\x99clock they moved to Maxim\xe2\x80\x99s, and two found them in\r\n      Deviniere\xe2\x80\x99s. Sloane had been drinking consecutively and was in a\r\n      state of unsteady exhilaration, but Amory was quite tiresomely\r\n      sober; they had run across none of those ancient, corrupt buyers\r\n      of champagne who usually assisted their New York parties. They\r\n      were just through dancing and were making their way back to their\r\n      chairs when Amory became aware that some one at a near-by table\r\n      was looking at him. He turned and glanced casually... a\r\n      middle-aged man dressed in a brown sack suit, it was, sitting a\r\n      little apart at a table by himself and watching their party\r\n      intently. At Amory\xe2\x80\x99s glance he smiled faintly. Amory turned to\r\n      Fred, who was just sitting down.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s that pale fool watching us?\xe2\x80\x9d he complained indignantly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Sloane. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll have him thrown out!\xe2\x80\x9d He rose to\r\n      his feet and swayed back and forth, clinging to his chair. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere\r\n      is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Axia and Phoebe suddenly leaned and whispered to each other\r\n      across the table, and before Amory realized it they found\r\n      themselves on their way to the door.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhere now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cUp to the flat,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Phoebe. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve got brandy and\r\n      fizz\xe2\x80\x94and everything\xe2\x80\x99s slow down here to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory considered quickly. He hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been drinking, and decided\r\n      that if he took no more, it would be reasonably discreet for him\r\n      to trot along in the party. In fact, it would be, perhaps, the\r\n      thing to do in order to keep an eye on Sloane, who was not in a\r\n      state to do his own thinking. So he took Axia\xe2\x80\x99s arm and, piling\r\n      intimately into a taxicab, they drove out over the hundreds and\r\n      drew up at a tall, white-stone apartment-house. ... Never would\r\n      he forget that street.... It was a broad street, lined on both\r\n      sides with just such tall, white-stone buildings, dotted with\r\n      dark windows; they stretched along as far as the eye could see,\r\n      flooded with a bright moonlight that gave them a calcium pallor.\r\n      He imagined each one to have an elevator and a colored hall-boy\r\n      and a key-rack; each one to be eight stories high and full of\r\n      three and four room suites. He was rather glad to walk into the\r\n      cheeriness of Phoebe\xe2\x80\x99s living-room and sink onto a sofa, while\r\n      the girls went rummaging for food.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cPhoebe\xe2\x80\x99s great stuff,\xe2\x80\x9d confided Sloane, sotto voce.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m only going to stay half an hour,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory said sternly. He\r\n      wondered if it sounded priggish.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHell y\xe2\x80\x99 say,\xe2\x80\x9d protested Sloane. \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re here now\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t le\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      rush.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like this place,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory said sulkily, \xe2\x80\x9cand I don\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\n      any food.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Phoebe reappeared with sandwiches, brandy bottle, siphon, and\r\n      four glasses.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAmory, pour \xe2\x80\x99em out,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cand we\xe2\x80\x99ll drink to Fred Sloane,\r\n      who has a rare, distinguished edge.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Axia, coming in, \xe2\x80\x9cand Amory. I like Amory.\xe2\x80\x9d She sat\r\n      down beside him and laid her yellow head on his shoulder.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll pour,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sloane; \xe2\x80\x9cyou use siphon, Phoebe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      They filled the tray with glasses.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cReady, here she goes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory hesitated, glass in hand.\r\n\r\n      There was a minute while temptation crept over him like a warm\r\n      wind, and his imagination turned to fire, and he took the glass\r\n      from Phoebe\xe2\x80\x99s hand. That was all; for at the second that his\r\n      decision came, he looked up and saw, ten yards from him, the man\r\n      who had been in the cafe, and with his jump of astonishment the\r\n      glass fell from his uplifted hand. There the man half sat, half\r\n      leaned against a pile of pillows on the corner divan. His face\r\n      was cast in the same yellow wax as in the cafe, neither the dull,\r\n      pasty color of a dead man\xe2\x80\x94rather a sort of virile pallor\xe2\x80\x94nor\r\n      unhealthy, you\xe2\x80\x99d have called it; but like a strong man who\xe2\x80\x99d\r\n      worked in a mine or done night shifts in a damp climate. Amory\r\n      looked him over carefully and later he could have drawn him after\r\n      a fashion, down to the merest details. His mouth was the kind\r\n      that is called frank, and he had steady gray eyes that moved\r\n      slowly from one to the other of their group, with just the shade\r\n      of a questioning expression. Amory noticed his hands; they\r\n      weren\xe2\x80\x99t fine at all, but they had versatility and a tenuous\r\n      strength... they were nervous hands that sat lightly along the\r\n      cushions and moved constantly with little jerky openings and\r\n      closings. Then, suddenly, Amory perceived the feet, and with a\r\n      rush of blood to the head he realized he was afraid. The feet\r\n      were all wrong ... with a sort of wrongness that he felt rather\r\n      than knew.... It was like weakness in a good woman, or blood on\r\n      satin; one of those terrible incongruities that shake little\r\n      things in the back of the brain. He wore no shoes, but, instead,\r\n      a sort of half moccasin, pointed, though, like the shoes they\r\n      wore in the fourteenth century, and with the little ends curling\r\n      up. They were a darkish brown and his toes seemed to fill them to\r\n      the end.... They were unutterably terrible....\r\n\r\n      He must have said something, or looked something, for Axia\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      voice came out of the void with a strange goodness.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, look at Amory! Poor old Amory\xe2\x80\x99s sick\xe2\x80\x94old head going\r\n      \xe2\x80\x99round?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLook at that man!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Amory, pointing toward the corner\r\n      divan.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou mean that purple zebra!\xe2\x80\x9d shrieked Axia facetiously. \xe2\x80\x9cOoo-ee!\r\n      Amory\xe2\x80\x99s got a purple zebra watching him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Sloane laughed vacantly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOle zebra gotcha, Amory?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      There was a silence.... The man regarded Amory quizzically....\r\n      Then the human voices fell faintly on his ear:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThought you weren\xe2\x80\x99t drinking,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Axia sardonically, but\r\n      her voice was good to hear; the whole divan that held the man was\r\n      alive; alive like heat waves over asphalt, like wriggling\r\n      worms....\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCome back! Come back!\xe2\x80\x9d Axia\xe2\x80\x99s arm fell on his. \xe2\x80\x9cAmory, dear, you\r\n      aren\xe2\x80\x99t going, Amory!\xe2\x80\x9d He was half-way to the door.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCome on, Amory, stick \xe2\x80\x99th us!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSick, are you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSit down a second!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cTake some water.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cTake a little brandy....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The elevator was close, and the colored boy was half asleep,\r\n      paled to a livid bronze... Axia\xe2\x80\x99s beseeching voice floated down\r\n      the shaft. Those feet... those feet...\r\n\r\n      As they settled to the lower floor the feet came into view in the\r\n      sickly electric light of the paved hall.\r\n\r\n\r\n      IN THE ALLEY\r\n\r\n      Down the long street came the moon, and Amory turned his back on\r\n      it and walked. Ten, fifteen steps away sounded the footsteps.\r\n      They were like a slow dripping, with just the slightest\r\n      insistence in their fall. Amory\xe2\x80\x99s shadow lay, perhaps, ten feet\r\n      ahead of him, and soft shoes was presumably that far behind. With\r\n      the instinct of a child Amory edged in under the blue darkness of\r\n      the white buildings, cleaving the moonlight for haggard seconds,\r\n      once bursting into a slow run with clumsy stumblings. After that\r\n      he stopped suddenly; he must keep hold, he thought. His lips were\r\n      dry and he licked them.\r\n\r\n      If he met any one good\xe2\x80\x94were there any good people left in the\r\n      world or did they all live in white apartment-houses now? Was\r\n      every one followed in the moonlight? But if he met some one good\r\n      who\xe2\x80\x99d know what he meant and hear this damned scuffle... then the\r\n      scuffling grew suddenly nearer, and a black cloud settled over\r\n      the moon. When again the pale sheen skimmed the cornices, it was\r\n      almost beside him, and Amory thought he heard a quiet breathing.\r\n      Suddenly he realized that the footsteps were not behind, had\r\n      never been behind, they were ahead and he was not eluding but\r\n      following... following. He began to run, blindly, his heart\r\n      knocking heavily, his hands clinched. Far ahead a black dot\r\n      showed itself, resolved slowly into a human shape. But Amory was\r\n      beyond that now; he turned off the street and darted into an\r\n      alley, narrow and dark and smelling of old rottenness. He twisted\r\n      down a long, sinuous blackness, where the moonlight was shut away\r\n      except for tiny glints and patches... then suddenly sank panting\r\n      into a corner by a fence, exhausted. The steps ahead stopped, and\r\n      he could hear them shift slightly with a continuous motion, like\r\n      waves around a dock.\r\n\r\n      He put his face in his hands and covered eyes and ears as well as\r\n      he could. During all this time it never occurred to him that he\r\n      was delirious or drunk. He had a sense of reality such as\r\n      material things could never give him. His intellectual content\r\n      seemed to submit passively to it, and it fitted like a glove\r\n      everything that had ever preceded it in his life. It did not\r\n      muddle him. It was like a problem whose answer he knew on paper,\r\n      yet whose solution he was unable to grasp. He was far beyond\r\n      horror. He had sunk through the thin surface of that, now moved\r\n      in a region where the feet and the fear of white walls were real,\r\n      living things, things he must accept. Only far inside his soul a\r\n      little fire leaped and cried that something was pulling him down,\r\n      trying to get him inside a door and slam it behind him. After\r\n      that door was slammed there would be only footfalls and white\r\n      buildings in the moonlight, and perhaps he would be one of the\r\n      footfalls.\r\n\r\n      During the five or ten minutes he waited in the shadow of the\r\n      fence, there was somehow this fire... that was as near as he\r\n      could name it afterward. He remembered calling aloud:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI want some one stupid. Oh, send some one stupid!\xe2\x80\x9d This to the\r\n      black fence opposite him, in whose shadows the footsteps shuffled\r\n      ... shuffled. He supposed \xe2\x80\x9cstupid\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cgood\xe2\x80\x9d had become somehow\r\n      intermingled through previous association. When he called thus it\r\n      was not an act of will at all\xe2\x80\x94will had turned him away from the\r\n      moving figure in the street; it was almost instinct that called,\r\n      just the pile on pile of inherent tradition or some wild prayer\r\n      from way over the night. Then something clanged like a low gong\r\n      struck at a distance, and before his eyes a face flashed over the\r\n      two feet, a face pale and distorted with a sort of infinite evil\r\n      that twisted it like flame in the wind; _but he knew, for the\r\n      half instant that the gong tanged and hummed, that it was the\r\n      face of Dick Humbird._\r\n\r\n      Minutes later he sprang to his feet, realizing dimly that there\r\n      was no more sound, and that he was alone in the graying alley. It\r\n      was cold, and he started on a steady run for the light that\r\n      showed the street at the other end.\r\n\r\n\r\n      AT THE WINDOW\r\n\r\n      It was late morning when he woke and found the telephone beside\r\n      his bed in the hotel tolling frantically, and remembered that he\r\n      had left word to be called at eleven. Sloane was snoring heavily,\r\n      his clothes in a pile by his bed. They dressed and ate breakfast\r\n      in silence, and then sauntered out to get some air. Amory\xe2\x80\x99s mind\r\n      was working slowly, trying to assimilate what had happened and\r\n      separate from the chaotic imagery that stacked his memory the\r\n      bare shreds of truth. If the morning had been cold and gray he\r\n      could have grasped the reins of the past in an instant, but it\r\n      was one of those days that New York gets sometimes in May, when\r\n      the air on Fifth Avenue is a soft, light wine. How much or how\r\n      little Sloane remembered Amory did not care to know; he\r\n      apparently had none of the nervous tension that was gripping\r\n      Amory and forcing his mind back and forth like a shrieking saw.\r\n\r\n      Then Broadway broke upon them, and with the babel of noise and\r\n      the painted faces a sudden sickness rushed over Amory.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cFor God\xe2\x80\x99s sake, let\xe2\x80\x99s go back! Let\xe2\x80\x99s get off of this\xe2\x80\x94this\r\n      place!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Sloane looked at him in amazement.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThis street, it\xe2\x80\x99s ghastly! Come on! let\xe2\x80\x99s get back to the\r\n      Avenue!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to say,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sloane stolidly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat \xe2\x80\x99cause you had\r\n      some sort of indigestion that made you act like a maniac last\r\n      night, you\xe2\x80\x99re never coming on Broadway again?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Simultaneously Amory classed him with the crowd, and he seemed no\r\n      longer Sloane of the debonair humor and the happy personality,\r\n      but only one of the evil faces that whirled along the turbid\r\n      stream.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMan!\xe2\x80\x9d he shouted so loud that the people on the corner turned\r\n      and followed them with their eyes, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s filthy, and if you can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      see it, you\xe2\x80\x99re filthy, too!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t help it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sloane doggedly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter with\r\n      you? Old remorse getting you? You\xe2\x80\x99d be in a fine state if you\xe2\x80\x99d\r\n      gone through with our little party.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going, Fred,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory slowly. His knees were shaking\r\n      under him, and he knew that if he stayed another minute on this\r\n      street he would keel over where he stood. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll be at the\r\n      Vanderbilt for lunch.\xe2\x80\x9d And he strode rapidly off and turned over\r\n      to Fifth Avenue. Back at the hotel he felt better, but as he\r\n      walked into the barber-shop, intending to get a head massage, the\r\n      smell of the powders and tonics brought back Axia\xe2\x80\x99s sidelong,\r\n      suggestive smile, and he left hurriedly. In the doorway of his\r\n      room a sudden blackness flowed around him like a divided river.\r\n\r\n      When he came to himself he knew that several hours had passed. He\r\n      pitched onto the bed and rolled over on his face with a deadly\r\n      fear that he was going mad. He wanted people, people, some one\r\n      sane and stupid and good. He lay for he knew not how long without\r\n      moving. He could feel the little hot veins on his forehead\r\n      standing out, and his terror had hardened on him like plaster. He\r\n      felt he was passing up again through the thin crust of horror,\r\n      and now only could he distinguish the shadowy twilight he was\r\n      leaving. He must have fallen asleep again, for when he next\r\n      recollected himself he had paid the hotel bill and was stepping\r\n      into a taxi at the door. It was raining torrents.\r\n\r\n      On the train for Princeton he saw no one he knew, only a crowd of\r\n      fagged-looking Philadelphians. The presence of a painted woman\r\n      across the aisle filled him with a fresh burst of sickness and he\r\n      changed to another car, tried to concentrate on an article in a\r\n      popular magazine. He found himself reading the same paragraphs\r\n      over and over, so he abandoned this attempt and leaning over\r\n      wearily pressed his hot forehead against the damp window-pane.\r\n      The car, a smoker, was hot and stuffy with most of the smells of\r\n      the state\xe2\x80\x99s alien population; he opened a window and shivered\r\n      against the cloud of fog that drifted in over him. The two hours\xe2\x80\x99\r\n      ride were like days, and he nearly cried aloud with joy when the\r\n      towers of Princeton loomed up beside him and the yellow squares\r\n      of light filtered through the blue rain.\r\n\r\n      Tom was standing in the centre of the room, pensively relighting\r\n      a cigar-stub. Amory fancied he looked rather relieved on seeing\r\n      him.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHad a hell of a dream about you last night,\xe2\x80\x9d came in the cracked\r\n      voice through the cigar smoke. \xe2\x80\x9cI had an idea you were in some\r\n      trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t tell me about it!\xe2\x80\x9d Amory almost shrieked. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say a\r\n      word; I\xe2\x80\x99m tired and pepped out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Tom looked at him queerly and then sank into a chair and opened\r\n      his Italian note-book. Amory threw his coat and hat on the floor,\r\n      loosened his collar, and took a Wells novel at random from the\r\n      shelf. \xe2\x80\x9cWells is sane,\xe2\x80\x9d he thought, \xe2\x80\x9cand if he won\xe2\x80\x99t do I\xe2\x80\x99ll read\r\n      Rupert Brooke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Half an hour passed. Outside the wind came up, and Amory started\r\n      as the wet branches moved and clawed with their finger-nails at\r\n      the window-pane. Tom was deep in his work, and inside the room\r\n      only the occasional scratch of a match or the rustle of leather\r\n      as they shifted in their chairs broke the stillness. Then like a\r\n      zigzag of lightning came the change. Amory sat bolt upright,\r\n      frozen cold in his chair. Tom was looking at him with his mouth\r\n      drooping, eyes fixed.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGod help us!\xe2\x80\x9d Amory cried.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, my heavens!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Tom, \xe2\x80\x9clook behind!\xe2\x80\x9d Quick as a flash\r\n      Amory whirled around. He saw nothing but the dark window-pane.\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s gone now,\xe2\x80\x9d came Tom\xe2\x80\x99s voice after a second in a still\r\n      terror. \xe2\x80\x9cSomething was looking at you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Trembling violently, Amory dropped into his chair again.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got to tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve had one hell of an\r\n      experience. I think I\xe2\x80\x99ve\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen the devil or\xe2\x80\x94something like\r\n      him. What face did you just see?\xe2\x80\x94or no,\xe2\x80\x9d he added quickly, \xe2\x80\x9cdon\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      tell me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      And he gave Tom the story. It was midnight when he finished, and\r\n      after that, with all lights burning, two sleepy, shivering boys\r\n      read to each other from \xe2\x80\x9cThe New Machiavelli,\xe2\x80\x9d until dawn came up\r\n      out of Witherspoon Hall, and the Princetonian fell against the\r\n      door, and the May birds hailed the sun on last night\xe2\x80\x99s rain.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n      CHAPTER 4. Narcissus Off Duty\r\n\r\n\r\n      During Princeton\xe2\x80\x99s transition period, that is, during Amory\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      last two years there, while he saw it change and broaden and live\r\n      up to its Gothic beauty by better means than night parades,\r\n      certain individuals arrived who stirred it to its plethoric\r\n      depths. Some of them had been freshmen, and wild freshmen, with\r\n      Amory; some were in the class below; and it was in the beginning\r\n      of his last year and around small tables at the Nassau Inn that\r\n      they began questioning aloud the institutions that Amory and\r\n      countless others before him had questioned so long in secret.\r\n      First, and partly by accident, they struck on certain books, a\r\n      definite type of biographical novel that Amory christened \xe2\x80\x9cquest\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      books. In the \xe2\x80\x9cquest\xe2\x80\x9d book the hero set off in life armed with\r\n      the best weapons and avowedly intending to use them as such\r\n      weapons are usually used, to push their possessors ahead as\r\n      selfishly and blindly as possible, but the heroes of the \xe2\x80\x9cquest\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      books discovered that there might be a more magnificent use for\r\n      them. \xe2\x80\x9cNone Other Gods,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cSinister Street,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cThe Research\r\n      Magnificent\xe2\x80\x9d were examples of such books; it was the latter of\r\n      these three that gripped Burne Holiday and made him wonder in the\r\n      beginning of senior year how much it was worth while being a\r\n      diplomatic autocrat around his club on Prospect Avenue and\r\n      basking in the high lights of class office. It was distinctly\r\n      through the channels of aristocracy that Burne found his way.\r\n      Amory, through Kerry, had had a vague drifting acquaintance with\r\n      him, but not until January of senior year did their friendship\r\n      commence.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHeard the latest?\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom, coming in late one drizzly evening\r\n      with that triumphant air he always wore after a successful\r\n      conversational bout.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo. Somebody flunked out? Or another ship sunk?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWorse than that. About one-third of the junior class are going\r\n      to resign from their clubs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cActual fact!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSpirit of reform and all that. Burne Holiday is behind it. The\r\n      club presidents are holding a meeting to-night to see if they can\r\n      find a joint means of combating it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, what\xe2\x80\x99s the idea of the thing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, clubs injurious to Princeton democracy; cost a lot; draw\r\n      social lines, take time; the regular line you get sometimes from\r\n      disappointed sophomores. Woodrow thought they should be abolished\r\n      and all that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBut this is the real thing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAbsolutely. I think it\xe2\x80\x99ll go through.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cFor Pete\xe2\x80\x99s sake, tell me more about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d began Tom, \xe2\x80\x9cit seems that the idea developed\r\n      simultaneously in several heads. I was talking to Burne awhile\r\n      ago, and he claims that it\xe2\x80\x99s a logical result if an intelligent\r\n      person thinks long enough about the social system. They had a\r\n      \xe2\x80\x98discussion crowd\xe2\x80\x99 and the point of abolishing the clubs was\r\n      brought up by some one\xe2\x80\x94everybody there leaped at it\xe2\x80\x94it had been\r\n      in each one\xe2\x80\x99s mind, more or less, and it just needed a spark to\r\n      bring it out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cFine! I swear I think it\xe2\x80\x99ll be most entertaining. How do they\r\n      feel up at Cap and Gown?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWild, of course. Every one\xe2\x80\x99s been sitting and arguing and\r\n      swearing and getting mad and getting sentimental and getting\r\n      brutal. It\xe2\x80\x99s the same at all the clubs; I\xe2\x80\x99ve been the rounds.\r\n      They get one of the radicals in the corner and fire questions at\r\n      him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHow do the radicals stand up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, moderately well. Burne\xe2\x80\x99s a damn good talker, and so\r\n      obviously sincere that you can\xe2\x80\x99t get anywhere with him. It\xe2\x80\x99s so\r\n      evident that resigning from his club means so much more to him\r\n      than preventing it does to us that I felt futile when I argued;\r\n      finally took a position that was brilliantly neutral. In fact, I\r\n      believe Burne thought for a while that he\xe2\x80\x99d converted me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you say almost a third of the junior class are going to\r\n      resign?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCall it a fourth and be safe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLord\xe2\x80\x94who\xe2\x80\x99d have thought it possible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      There was a brisk knock at the door, and Burne himself came in.\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHello, Amory\xe2\x80\x94hello, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory rose.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Evening, Burne. Don\xe2\x80\x99t mind if I seem to rush; I\xe2\x80\x99m going to\r\n      Renwick\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Burne turned to him quickly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou probably know what I want to talk to Tom about, and it isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      a bit private. I wish you\xe2\x80\x99d stay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d be glad to.\xe2\x80\x9d Amory sat down again, and as Burne perched on a\r\n      table and launched into argument with Tom, he looked at this\r\n      revolutionary more carefully than he ever had before.\r\n      Broad-browed and strong-chinned, with a fineness in the honest\r\n      gray eyes that were like Kerry\xe2\x80\x99s, Burne was a man who gave an\r\n      immediate impression of bigness and security\xe2\x80\x94stubborn, that was\r\n      evident, but his stubbornness wore no stolidity, and when he had\r\n      talked for five minutes Amory knew that this keen enthusiasm had\r\n      in it no quality of dilettantism.\r\n\r\n      The intense power Amory felt later in Burne Holiday differed from\r\n      the admiration he had had for Humbird. This time it began as\r\n      purely a mental interest. With other men of whom he had thought\r\n      as primarily first-class, he had been attracted first by their\r\n      personalities, and in Burne he missed that immediate magnetism to\r\n      which he usually swore allegiance. But that night Amory was\r\n      struck by Burne\xe2\x80\x99s intense earnestness, a quality he was\r\n      accustomed to associate only with the dread stupidity, and by the\r\n      great enthusiasm that struck dead chords in his heart. Burne\r\n      stood vaguely for a land Amory hoped he was drifting toward\xe2\x80\x94and\r\n      it was almost time that land was in sight. Tom and Amory and Alec\r\n      had reached an impasse; never did they seem to have new\r\n      experiences in common, for Tom and Alec had been as blindly busy\r\n      with their committees and boards as Amory had been blindly\r\n      idling, and the things they had for dissection\xe2\x80\x94college,\r\n      contemporary personality and the like\xe2\x80\x94they had hashed and\r\n      rehashed for many a frugal conversational meal.\r\n\r\n      That night they discussed the clubs until twelve, and, in the\r\n      main, they agreed with Burne. To the roommates it did not seem\r\n      such a vital subject as it had in the two years before, but the\r\n      logic of Burne\xe2\x80\x99s objections to the social system dovetailed so\r\n      completely with everything they had thought, that they questioned\r\n      rather than argued, and envied the sanity that enabled this man\r\n      to stand out so against all traditions.\r\n\r\n      Then Amory branched off and found that Burne was deep in other\r\n      things as well. Economics had interested him and he was turning\r\n      socialist. Pacifism played in the back of his mind, and he read\r\n      The Masses and Lyoff Tolstoi faithfully.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHow about religion?\xe2\x80\x9d Amory asked him.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t know. I\xe2\x80\x99m in a muddle about a lot of things\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ve just\r\n      discovered that I\xe2\x80\x99ve a mind, and I\xe2\x80\x99m starting to read.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cRead what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cEverything. I have to pick and choose, of course, but mostly\r\n      things to make me think. I\xe2\x80\x99m reading the four gospels now, and\r\n      the \xe2\x80\x98Varieties of Religious Experience.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat chiefly started you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWells, I guess, and Tolstoi, and a man named Edward Carpenter.\r\n      I\xe2\x80\x99ve been reading for over a year now\xe2\x80\x94on a few lines, on what I\r\n      consider the essential lines.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cPoetry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, frankly, not what you call poetry, or for your reasons\xe2\x80\x94you\r\n      two write, of course, and look at things differently. Whitman is\r\n      the man that attracts me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhitman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes; he\xe2\x80\x99s a definite ethical force.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99m ashamed to say that I\xe2\x80\x99m a blank on the subject of\r\n      Whitman. How about you, Tom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Tom nodded sheepishly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Burne, \xe2\x80\x9cyou may strike a few poems that are\r\n      tiresome, but I mean the mass of his work. He\xe2\x80\x99s tremendous\xe2\x80\x94like\r\n      Tolstoi. They both look things in the face, and, somehow,\r\n      different as they are, stand for somewhat the same things.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou have me stumped, Burne,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory admitted. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve read \xe2\x80\x98Anna\r\n      Karenina\xe2\x80\x99 and the \xe2\x80\x98Kreutzer Sonata\xe2\x80\x99 of course, but Tolstoi is\r\n      mostly in the original Russian as far as I\xe2\x80\x99m concerned.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s the greatest man in hundreds of years,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Burne\r\n      enthusiastically. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you ever see a picture of that shaggy old\r\n      head of his?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      They talked until three, from biology to organized religion, and\r\n      when Amory crept shivering into bed it was with his mind aglow\r\n      with ideas and a sense of shock that some one else had discovered\r\n      the path he might have followed. Burne Holiday was so evidently\r\n      developing\xe2\x80\x94and Amory had considered that he was doing the same.\r\n      He had fallen into a deep cynicism over what had crossed his\r\n      path, plotted the imperfectability of man and read Shaw and\r\n      Chesterton enough to keep his mind from the edges of\r\n      decadence\xe2\x80\x94now suddenly all his mental processes of the last year\r\n      and a half seemed stale and futile\xe2\x80\x94a petty consummation of\r\n      himself... and like a sombre background lay that incident of the\r\n      spring before, that filled half his nights with a dreary terror\r\n      and made him unable to pray. He was not even a Catholic, yet that\r\n      was the only ghost of a code that he had, the gaudy, ritualistic,\r\n      paradoxical Catholicism whose prophet was Chesterton, whose\r\n      claqueurs were such reformed rakes of literature as Huysmans and\r\n      Bourget, whose American sponsor was Ralph Adams Cram, with his\r\n      adulation of thirteenth-century cathedrals\xe2\x80\x94a Catholicism which\r\n      Amory found convenient and ready-made, without priest or\r\n      sacraments or sacrifice.\r\n\r\n      He could not sleep, so he turned on his reading-lamp and, taking\r\n      down the \xe2\x80\x9cKreutzer Sonata,\xe2\x80\x9d searched it carefully for the germs\r\n      of Burne\xe2\x80\x99s enthusiasm. Being Burne was suddenly so much realler\r\n      than being clever. Yet he sighed... here were other possible clay\r\n      feet.\r\n\r\n      He thought back through two years, of Burne as a hurried, nervous\r\n      freshman, quite submerged in his brother\xe2\x80\x99s personality. Then he\r\n      remembered an incident of sophomore year, in which Burne had been\r\n      suspected of the leading role.\r\n\r\n      Dean Hollister had been heard by a large group arguing with a\r\n      taxi-driver, who had driven him from the junction. In the course\r\n      of the altercation the dean remarked that he \xe2\x80\x9cmight as well buy\r\n      the taxicab.\xe2\x80\x9d He paid and walked off, but next morning he entered\r\n      his private office to find the taxicab itself in the space\r\n      usually occupied by his desk, bearing a sign which read \xe2\x80\x9cProperty\r\n      of Dean Hollister. Bought and Paid for.\xe2\x80\x9d... It took two expert\r\n      mechanics half a day to dissemble it into its minutest parts and\r\n      remove it, which only goes to prove the rare energy of sophomore\r\n      humor under efficient leadership.\r\n\r\n      Then again, that very fall, Burne had caused a sensation. A\r\n      certain Phyllis Styles, an intercollegiate prom-trotter, had\r\n      failed to get her yearly invitation to the Harvard-Princeton\r\n      game.\r\n\r\n      Jesse Ferrenby had brought her to a smaller game a few weeks\r\n      before, and had pressed Burne into service\xe2\x80\x94to the ruination of\r\n      the latter\xe2\x80\x99s misogyny.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAre you coming to the Harvard game?\xe2\x80\x9d Burne had asked\r\n      indiscreetly, merely to make conversation.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIf you ask me,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Phyllis quickly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOf course I do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Burne feebly. He was unversed in the arts\r\n      of Phyllis, and was sure that this was merely a vapid form of\r\n      kidding. Before an hour had passed he knew that he was indeed\r\n      involved. Phyllis had pinned him down and served him up, informed\r\n      him the train she was arriving by, and depressed him thoroughly.\r\n      Aside from loathing Phyllis, he had particularly wanted to stag\r\n      that game and entertain some Harvard friends.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99ll see,\xe2\x80\x9d he informed a delegation who arrived in his room to\r\n      josh him. \xe2\x80\x9cThis will be the last game she ever persuades any\r\n      young innocent to take her to!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBut, Burne\xe2\x80\x94why did you _invite_ her if you didn\xe2\x80\x99t want her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBurne, you _know_ you\xe2\x80\x99re secretly mad about her\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\n      _real_ trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat can _you_ do, Burne? What can _you_ do against Phyllis?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      But Burne only shook his head and muttered threats which\r\n      consisted largely of the phrase: \xe2\x80\x9cShe\xe2\x80\x99ll see, she\xe2\x80\x99ll see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The blithesome Phyllis bore her twenty-five summers gayly from\r\n      the train, but on the platform a ghastly sight met her eyes.\r\n      There were Burne and Fred Sloane arrayed to the last dot like the\r\n      lurid figures on college posters. They had bought flaring suits\r\n      with huge peg-top trousers and gigantic padded shoulders. On\r\n      their heads were rakish college hats, pinned up in front and\r\n      sporting bright orange-and-black bands, while from their\r\n      celluloid collars blossomed flaming orange ties. They wore black\r\n      arm-bands with orange \xe2\x80\x9cP\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d and carried canes flying Princeton\r\n      pennants, the effect completed by socks and peeping handkerchiefs\r\n      in the same color motifs. On a clanking chain they led a large,\r\n      angry tom-cat, painted to represent a tiger.\r\n\r\n      A good half of the station crowd was already staring at them,\r\n      torn between horrified pity and riotous mirth, and as Phyllis,\r\n      with her svelte jaw dropping, approached, the pair bent over and\r\n      emitted a college cheer in loud, far-carrying voices,\r\n      thoughtfully adding the name \xe2\x80\x9cPhyllis\xe2\x80\x9d to the end. She was\r\n      vociferously greeted and escorted enthusiastically across the\r\n      campus, followed by half a hundred village urchins\xe2\x80\x94to the stifled\r\n      laughter of hundreds of alumni and visitors, half of whom had no\r\n      idea that this was a practical joke, but thought that Burne and\r\n      Fred were two varsity sports showing their girl a collegiate\r\n      time.\r\n\r\n      Phyllis\xe2\x80\x99s feelings as she was paraded by the Harvard and\r\n      Princeton stands, where sat dozens of her former devotees, can be\r\n      imagined. She tried to walk a little ahead, she tried to walk a\r\n      little behind\xe2\x80\x94but they stayed close, that there should be no\r\n      doubt whom she was with, talking in loud voices of their friends\r\n      on the football team, until she could almost hear her\r\n      acquaintances whispering:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cPhyllis Styles must be _awfully hard up_ to have to come with\r\n      _those two_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      That had been Burne, dynamically humorous, fundamentally serious.\r\n      From that root had blossomed the energy that he was now trying to\r\n      orient with progress....\r\n\r\n      So the weeks passed and March came and the clay feet that Amory\r\n      looked for failed to appear. About a hundred juniors and seniors\r\n      resigned from their clubs in a final fury of righteousness, and\r\n      the clubs in helplessness turned upon Burne their finest weapon:\r\n      ridicule. Every one who knew him liked him\xe2\x80\x94but what he stood for\r\n      (and he began to stand for more all the time) came under the lash\r\n      of many tongues, until a frailer man than he would have been\r\n      snowed under.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you mind losing prestige?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Amory one night. They had\r\n      taken to exchanging calls several times a week.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOf course I don\xe2\x80\x99t. What\xe2\x80\x99s prestige, at best?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSome people say that you\xe2\x80\x99re just a rather original politician.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He roared with laughter.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what Fred Sloane told me to-day. I suppose I have it\r\n      coming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      One afternoon they dipped into a subject that had interested\r\n      Amory for a long time\xe2\x80\x94the matter of the bearing of physical\r\n      attributes on a man\xe2\x80\x99s make-up. Burne had gone into the biology of\r\n      this, and then:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOf course health counts\xe2\x80\x94a healthy man has twice the chance of\r\n      being good,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t agree with you\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in \xe2\x80\x98muscular\r\n      Christianity.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI do\xe2\x80\x94I believe Christ had great physical vigor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, no,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory protested. \xe2\x80\x9cHe worked too hard for that. I\r\n      imagine that when he died he was a broken-down man\xe2\x80\x94and the great\r\n      saints haven\xe2\x80\x99t been strong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHalf of them have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, even granting that, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think health has anything to\r\n      do with goodness; of course, it\xe2\x80\x99s valuable to a great saint to be\r\n      able to stand enormous strains, but this fad of popular preachers\r\n      rising on their toes in simulated virility, bellowing that\r\n      calisthenics will save the world\xe2\x80\x94no, Burne, I can\xe2\x80\x99t go that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, let\xe2\x80\x99s waive it\xe2\x80\x94we won\xe2\x80\x99t get anywhere, and besides I\r\n      haven\xe2\x80\x99t quite made up my mind about it myself. Now, here\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      something I _do_ know\xe2\x80\x94personal appearance has a lot to do with\r\n      it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cColoring?\xe2\x80\x9d Amory asked eagerly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what Tom and I figured,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory agreed. \xe2\x80\x9cWe took the\r\n      year-books for the last ten years and looked at the pictures of\r\n      the senior council. I know you don\xe2\x80\x99t think much of that august\r\n      body, but it does represent success here in a general way. Well,\r\n      I suppose only about thirty-five per cent of every class here are\r\n      blonds, are really light\xe2\x80\x94yet _two-thirds_ of every senior council\r\n      are light. We looked at pictures of ten years of them, mind you;\r\n      that means that out of every _fifteen_ light-haired men in the\r\n      senior class _one_ is on the senior council, and of the\r\n      dark-haired men it\xe2\x80\x99s only one in _fifty_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s true,\xe2\x80\x9d Burne agreed. \xe2\x80\x9cThe light-haired man _is_ a higher\r\n      type, generally speaking. I worked the thing out with the\r\n      Presidents of the United States once, and found that way over\r\n      half of them were light-haired\xe2\x80\x94yet think of the preponderant\r\n      number of brunettes in the race.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cPeople unconsciously admit it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll notice a\r\n      blond person is _expected_ to talk. If a blond girl doesn\xe2\x80\x99t talk\r\n      we call her a \xe2\x80\x98doll\xe2\x80\x99; if a light-haired man is silent he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      considered stupid. Yet the world is full of \xe2\x80\x98dark silent men\xe2\x80\x99 and\r\n      \xe2\x80\x98languorous brunettes\xe2\x80\x99 who haven\xe2\x80\x99t a brain in their heads, but\r\n      somehow are never accused of the dearth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd the large mouth and broad chin and rather big nose\r\n      undoubtedly make the superior face.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not so sure.\xe2\x80\x9d Amory was all for classical features.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99ll show you,\xe2\x80\x9d and Burne pulled out of his desk a\r\n      photographic collection of heavily bearded, shaggy\r\n      celebrities\xe2\x80\x94Tolstoi, Whitman, Carpenter, and others.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAren\xe2\x80\x99t they wonderful?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory tried politely to appreciate them, and gave up laughingly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBurne, I think they\xe2\x80\x99re the ugliest-looking crowd I ever came\r\n      across. They look like an old man\xe2\x80\x99s home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Amory, look at that forehead on Emerson; look at Tolstoi\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      eyes.\xe2\x80\x9d His tone was reproachful.\r\n\r\n      Amory shook his head.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo! Call them remarkable-looking or anything you want\xe2\x80\x94but ugly\r\n      they certainly are.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Unabashed, Burne ran his hand lovingly across the spacious\r\n      foreheads, and piling up the pictures put them back in his desk.\r\n\r\n      Walking at night was one of his favorite pursuits, and one night\r\n      he persuaded Amory to accompany him.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI hate the dark,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory objected. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t use to\xe2\x80\x94except when I\r\n      was particularly imaginative, but now, I really do\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m a regular\r\n      fool about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s useless, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cQuite possibly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll go east,\xe2\x80\x9d Burne suggested, \xe2\x80\x9cand down that string of roads\r\n      through the woods.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDoesn\xe2\x80\x99t sound very appealing to me,\xe2\x80\x9d admitted Amory reluctantly,\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cbut let\xe2\x80\x99s go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      They set off at a good gait, and for an hour swung along in a\r\n      brisk argument until the lights of Princeton were luminous white\r\n      blots behind them.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAny person with any imagination is bound to be afraid,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\n      Burne earnestly. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd this very walking at night is one of the\r\n      things I was afraid about. I\xe2\x80\x99m going to tell you why I can walk\r\n      anywhere now and not be afraid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGo on,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory urged eagerly. They were striding toward the\r\n      woods, Burne\xe2\x80\x99s nervous, enthusiastic voice warming to his\r\n      subject.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI used to come out here alone at night, oh, three months ago,\r\n      and I always stopped at that cross-road we just passed. There\r\n      were the woods looming up ahead, just as they do now, there were\r\n      dogs howling and the shadows and no human sound. Of course, I\r\n      peopled the woods with everything ghastly, just like you do;\r\n      don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI do,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory admitted.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I began analyzing it\xe2\x80\x94my imagination persisted in sticking\r\n      horrors into the dark\xe2\x80\x94so I stuck my imagination into the dark\r\n      instead, and let it look out at me\xe2\x80\x94I let it play stray dog or\r\n      escaped convict or ghost, and then saw myself coming along the\r\n      road. That made it all right\xe2\x80\x94as it always makes everything all\r\n      right to project yourself completely into another\xe2\x80\x99s place. I knew\r\n      that if I were the dog or the convict or the ghost I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\n      a menace to Burne Holiday any more than he was a menace to me.\r\n      Then I thought of my watch. I\xe2\x80\x99d better go back and leave it and\r\n      then essay the woods. No; I decided, it\xe2\x80\x99s better on the whole\r\n      that I should lose a watch than that I should turn back\xe2\x80\x94and I did\r\n      go into them\xe2\x80\x94not only followed the road through them, but walked\r\n      into them until I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t frightened any more\xe2\x80\x94did it until one\r\n      night I sat down and dozed off in there; then I knew I was\r\n      through being afraid of the dark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLordy,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory breathed. \xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t have done that. I\xe2\x80\x99d have\r\n      come out half-way, and the first time an automobile passed and\r\n      made the dark thicker when its lamps disappeared, I\xe2\x80\x99d have come\r\n      in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d Burne said suddenly, after a few moments\xe2\x80\x99 silence, \xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x99re\r\n      half-way through, let\xe2\x80\x99s turn back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      On the return he launched into a discussion of will.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the whole thing,\xe2\x80\x9d he asserted. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the one dividing line\r\n      between good and evil. I\xe2\x80\x99ve never met a man who led a rotten life\r\n      and didn\xe2\x80\x99t have a weak will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHow about great criminals?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re usually insane. If not, they\xe2\x80\x99re weak. There is no such\r\n      thing as a strong, sane criminal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBurne, I disagree with you altogether; how about the superman?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s evil, I think, yet he\xe2\x80\x99s strong and sane.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve never met him. I\xe2\x80\x99ll bet, though, that he\xe2\x80\x99s stupid or\r\n      insane.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve met him over and over and he\xe2\x80\x99s neither. That\xe2\x80\x99s why I think\r\n      you\xe2\x80\x99re wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure I\xe2\x80\x99m not\xe2\x80\x94and so I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in imprisonment except\r\n      for the insane.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      On this point Amory could not agree. It seemed to him that life\r\n      and history were rife with the strong criminal, keen, but often\r\n      self-deluding; in politics and business one found him and among\r\n      the old statesmen and kings and generals; but Burne never agreed\r\n      and their courses began to split on that point.\r\n\r\n      Burne was drawing farther and farther away from the world about\r\n      him. He resigned the vice-presidency of the senior class and took\r\n      to reading and walking as almost his only pursuits. He\r\n      voluntarily attended graduate lectures in philosophy and biology,\r\n      and sat in all of them with a rather pathetically intent look in\r\n      his eyes, as if waiting for something the lecturer would never\r\n      quite come to. Sometimes Amory would see him squirm in his seat;\r\n      and his face would light up; he was on fire to debate a point.\r\n\r\n      He grew more abstracted on the street and was even accused of\r\n      becoming a snob, but Amory knew it was nothing of the sort, and\r\n      once when Burne passed him four feet off, absolutely unseeingly,\r\n      his mind a thousand miles away, Amory almost choked with the\r\n      romantic joy of watching him. Burne seemed to be climbing heights\r\n      where others would be forever unable to get a foothold.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory declared to Tom, \xe2\x80\x9che\xe2\x80\x99s the first contemporary\r\n      I\xe2\x80\x99ve ever met whom I\xe2\x80\x99ll admit is my superior in mental capacity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a bad time to admit it\xe2\x80\x94people are beginning to think he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      odd.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s way over their heads\xe2\x80\x94you know you think so yourself when\r\n      you talk to him\xe2\x80\x94Good Lord, Tom, you _used_ to stand out against\r\n      \xe2\x80\x98people.\xe2\x80\x99 Success has completely conventionalized you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Tom grew rather annoyed.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s he trying to do\xe2\x80\x94be excessively holy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo! not like anybody you\xe2\x80\x99ve ever seen. Never enters the\r\n      Philadelphian Society. He has no faith in that rot. He doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      believe that public swimming-pools and a kind word in time will\r\n      right the wrongs of the world; moreover, he takes a drink\r\n      whenever he feels like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHe certainly is getting in wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHave you talked to him lately?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThen you haven\xe2\x80\x99t any conception of him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The argument ended nowhere, but Amory noticed more than ever how\r\n      the sentiment toward Burne had changed on the campus.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s odd,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory said to Tom one night when they had grown more\r\n      amicable on the subject, \xe2\x80\x9cthat the people who violently\r\n      disapprove of Burne\xe2\x80\x99s radicalism are distinctly the Pharisee\r\n      class\xe2\x80\x94I mean they\xe2\x80\x99re the best-educated men in college\xe2\x80\x94the editors\r\n      of the papers, like yourself and Ferrenby, the younger\r\n      professors.... The illiterate athletes like Langueduc think he\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      getting eccentric, but they just say, \xe2\x80\x98Good old Burne has got\r\n      some queer ideas in his head,\xe2\x80\x99 and pass on\xe2\x80\x94the Pharisee\r\n      class\xe2\x80\x94Gee! they ridicule him unmercifully.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The next morning he met Burne hurrying along McCosh walk after a\r\n      recitation.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhither bound, Tsar?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOver to the Prince office to see Ferrenby,\xe2\x80\x9d he waved a copy of\r\n      the morning\xe2\x80\x99s Princetonian at Amory. \xe2\x80\x9cHe wrote this editorial.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGoing to flay him alive?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94but he\xe2\x80\x99s got me all balled up. Either I\xe2\x80\x99ve misjudged him or\r\n      he\xe2\x80\x99s suddenly become the world\xe2\x80\x99s worst radical.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Burne hurried on, and it was several days before Amory heard an\r\n      account of the ensuing conversation. Burne had come into the\r\n      editor\xe2\x80\x99s sanctum displaying the paper cheerfully.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHello, Jesse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHello there, Savonarola.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI just read your editorial.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGood boy\xe2\x80\x94didn\xe2\x80\x99t know you stooped that low.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cJesse, you startled me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHow so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAren\xe2\x80\x99t you afraid the faculty\xe2\x80\x99ll get after you if you pull this\r\n      irreligious stuff?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLike this morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat the devil\xe2\x80\x94that editorial was on the coaching system.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes, but that quotation\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Jesse sat up.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat quotation?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou know: \xe2\x80\x98He who is not with me is against me.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94what about it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Jesse was puzzled but not alarmed.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, you say here\xe2\x80\x94let me see.\xe2\x80\x9d Burne opened the paper and read:\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98_He who is not with me is against me_, as that gentleman said\r\n      who was notoriously capable of only coarse distinctions and\r\n      puerile generalities.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat of it?\xe2\x80\x9d Ferrenby began to look alarmed. \xe2\x80\x9cOliver Cromwell\r\n      said it, didn\xe2\x80\x99t he? or was it Washington, or one of the saints?\r\n      Good Lord, I\xe2\x80\x99ve forgotten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Burne roared with laughter.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Jesse, oh, good, kind Jesse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWho said it, for Pete\xe2\x80\x99s sake?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Burne, recovering his voice, \xe2\x80\x9cSt. Matthew attributes\r\n      it to Christ.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMy God!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Jesse, and collapsed backward into the\r\n      waste-basket.\r\n\r\n\r\n      AMORY WRITES A POEM\r\n\r\n      The weeks tore by. Amory wandered occasionally to New York on the\r\n      chance of finding a new shining green auto-bus, that its\r\n      stick-of-candy glamour might penetrate his disposition. One day\r\n      he ventured into a stock-company revival of a play whose name was\r\n      faintly familiar. The curtain rose\xe2\x80\x94he watched casually as a girl\r\n      entered. A few phrases rang in his ear and touched a faint chord\r\n      of memory. Where\xe2\x80\x94? When\xe2\x80\x94?\r\n\r\n      Then he seemed to hear a voice whispering beside him, a very\r\n      soft, vibrant voice: \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m such a poor little fool; _do_ tell\r\n      me when I do wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The solution came in a flash and he had a quick, glad memory of\r\n      Isabelle.\r\n\r\n      He found a blank space on his programme, and began to scribble\r\n      rapidly:\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cHere in the figured dark I watch once more, There, with the\r\n   curtain, roll the years away; Two years of years\xe2\x80\x94there was an idle\r\n   day Of ours, when happy endings didn\xe2\x80\x99t bore Our unfermented souls; I\r\n   could adore Your eager face beside me, wide-eyed, gay, Smiling a\r\n   repertoire while the poor play Reached me as a faint ripple reaches\r\n   shore.\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cYawning and wondering an evening through, I watch alone... and\r\n   chatterings, of course, Spoil the one scene which, somehow, _did_\r\n   have charms; You wept a bit, and I grew sad for you Right here! \r\n   Where Mr. X defends divorce And What\xe2\x80\x99s-Her-Name falls fainting in\r\n   his arms.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n      STILL CALM\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGhosts are such dumb things,\xe2\x80\x9d said Alec, \xe2\x80\x9cthey\xe2\x80\x99re slow-witted. I\r\n      can always outguess a ghost.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHow?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Tom.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, it depends where. Take a bedroom, for example. If you use\r\n      _any_ discretion a ghost can never get you in a bedroom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGo on, s\xe2\x80\x99pose you think there\xe2\x80\x99s maybe a ghost in your\r\n      bedroom\xe2\x80\x94what measures do you take on getting home at night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      demanded Amory, interested.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cTake a stick\xe2\x80\x9d answered Alec, with ponderous reverence, \xe2\x80\x9cone\r\n      about the length of a broom-handle. Now, the first thing to do is\r\n      to get the room _cleared_\xe2\x80\x94to do this you rush with your eyes\r\n      closed into your study and turn on the lights\xe2\x80\x94next, approaching\r\n      the closet, carefully run the stick in the door three or four\r\n      times. Then, if nothing happens, you can look in. _Always,\r\n      always_ run the stick in viciously first\xe2\x80\x94_never_ look first!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOf course, that\xe2\x80\x99s the ancient Celtic school,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom gravely.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94but they usually pray first. Anyway, you use this method to\r\n      clear the closets and also for behind all doors\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd the bed,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory suggested.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Amory, no!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Alec in horror. \xe2\x80\x9cThat isn\xe2\x80\x99t the way\xe2\x80\x94the\r\n      bed requires different tactics\xe2\x80\x94let the bed alone, as you value\r\n      your reason\xe2\x80\x94if there is a ghost in the room and that\xe2\x80\x99s only about\r\n      a third of the time, it is _almost always_ under the bed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x9d Amory began.\r\n\r\n      Alec waved him into silence.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOf _course_ you never look. You stand in the middle of the floor\r\n      and before he knows what you\xe2\x80\x99re going to do make a sudden leap\r\n      for the bed\xe2\x80\x94never walk near the bed; to a ghost your ankle is\r\n      your most vulnerable part\xe2\x80\x94once in bed, you\xe2\x80\x99re safe; he may lie\r\n      around under the bed all night, but you\xe2\x80\x99re safe as daylight. If\r\n      you still have doubts pull the blanket over your head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAll that\xe2\x80\x99s very interesting, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d Alec beamed proudly. \xe2\x80\x9cAll my own, too\xe2\x80\x94the Sir Oliver\r\n      Lodge of the new world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory was enjoying college immensely again. The sense of going\r\n      forward in a direct, determined line had come back; youth was\r\n      stirring and shaking out a few new feathers. He had even stored\r\n      enough surplus energy to sally into a new pose.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the idea of all this \xe2\x80\x98distracted\xe2\x80\x99 stuff, Amory?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\n      Alec one day, and then as Amory pretended to be cramped over his\r\n      book in a daze: \xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t try to act Burne, the mystic, to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory looked up innocently.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d mimicked Alec. \xe2\x80\x9cAre you trying to read yourself into a\r\n      rhapsody with\xe2\x80\x94let\xe2\x80\x99s see the book.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He snatched it; regarded it derisively.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory a little stiffly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98The Life of St. Teresa,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d read Alec aloud. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, my gosh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSay, Alec.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDoes it bother you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDoes what bother me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMy acting dazed and all that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, no\xe2\x80\x94of course it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t _bother_ me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, then, don\xe2\x80\x99t spoil it. If I enjoy going around telling\r\n      people guilelessly that I think I\xe2\x80\x99m a genius, let me do it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re getting a reputation for being eccentric,\xe2\x80\x9d said Alec,\r\n      laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cif that\xe2\x80\x99s what you mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory finally prevailed, and Alec agreed to accept his face value\r\n      in the presence of others if he was allowed rest periods when\r\n      they were alone; so Amory \xe2\x80\x9cran it out\xe2\x80\x9d at a great rate, bringing\r\n      the most eccentric characters to dinner, wild-eyed grad students,\r\n      preceptors with strange theories of God and government, to the\r\n      cynical amazement of the supercilious Cottage Club.\r\n\r\n      As February became slashed by sun and moved cheerfully into\r\n      March, Amory went several times to spend week-ends with\r\n      Monsignor; once he took Burne, with great success, for he took\r\n      equal pride and delight in displaying them to each other.\r\n      Monsignor took him several times to see Thornton Hancock, and\r\n      once or twice to the house of a Mrs. Lawrence, a type of\r\n      Rome-haunting American whom Amory liked immediately.\r\n\r\n      Then one day came a letter from Monsignor, which appended an\r\n      interesting P. S.:\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cDo you know,\xe2\x80\x9d it ran, \xe2\x80\x9cthat your third cousin, Clara Page, widowed\r\n  six months and very poor, is living in Philadelphia? I don\xe2\x80\x99t think\r\n  you\xe2\x80\x99ve ever met her, but I wish, as a favor to me, you\xe2\x80\x99d go to see\r\n  her.  To my mind, she\xe2\x80\x99s rather a remarkable woman, and just about\r\n  your age.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory sighed and decided to go, as a favor....\r\n\r\n\r\n      CLARA\r\n\r\n      She was immemorial.... Amory wasn\xe2\x80\x99t good enough for Clara, Clara\r\n      of ripply golden hair, but then no man was. Her goodness was\r\n      above the prosy morals of the husband-seeker, apart from the dull\r\n      literature of female virtue.\r\n\r\n      Sorrow lay lightly around her, and when Amory found her in\r\n      Philadelphia he thought her steely blue eyes held only happiness;\r\n      a latent strength, a realism, was brought to its fullest\r\n      development by the facts that she was compelled to face. She was\r\n      alone in the world, with two small children, little money, and,\r\n      worst of all, a host of friends. He saw her that winter in\r\n      Philadelphia entertaining a houseful of men for an evening, when\r\n      he knew she had not a servant in the house except the little\r\n      colored girl guarding the babies overhead. He saw one of the\r\n      greatest libertines in that city, a man who was habitually drunk\r\n      and notorious at home and abroad, sitting opposite her for an\r\n      evening, discussing _girls\xe2\x80\x99 boarding-schools_ with a sort of\r\n      innocent excitement. What a twist Clara had to her mind! She\r\n      could make fascinating and almost brilliant conversation out of\r\n      the thinnest air that ever floated through a drawing-room.\r\n\r\n      The idea that the girl was poverty-stricken had appealed to\r\n      Amory\xe2\x80\x99s sense of situation. He arrived in Philadelphia expecting\r\n      to be told that 921 Ark Street was in a miserable lane of hovels.\r\n      He was even disappointed when it proved to be nothing of the\r\n      sort. It was an old house that had been in her husband\xe2\x80\x99s family\r\n      for years. An elderly aunt, who objected to having it sold, had\r\n      put ten years\xe2\x80\x99 taxes with a lawyer and pranced off to Honolulu,\r\n      leaving Clara to struggle with the heating-problem as best she\r\n      could. So no wild-haired woman with a hungry baby at her breast\r\n      and a sad Amelia-like look greeted him. Instead, Amory would have\r\n      thought from his reception that she had not a care in the world.\r\n\r\n      A calm virility and a dreamy humor, marked contrasts to her\r\n      level-headedness\xe2\x80\x94into these moods she slipped sometimes as a\r\n      refuge. She could do the most prosy things (though she was wise\r\n      enough never to stultify herself with such \xe2\x80\x9chousehold arts\xe2\x80\x9d as\r\n      _knitting_ and _embroidery_), yet immediately afterward pick up a\r\n      book and let her imagination rove as a formless cloud with the\r\n      wind. Deepest of all in her personality was the golden radiance\r\n      that she diffused around her. As an open fire in a dark room\r\n      throws romance and pathos into the quiet faces at its edge, so\r\n      she cast her lights and shadows around the rooms that held her,\r\n      until she made of her prosy old uncle a man of quaint and\r\n      meditative charm, metamorphosed the stray telegraph boy into a\r\n      Puck-like creature of delightful originality. At first this\r\n      quality of hers somehow irritated Amory. He considered his own\r\n      uniqueness sufficient, and it rather embarrassed him when she\r\n      tried to read new interests into him for the benefit of what\r\n      other adorers were present. He felt as if a polite but insistent\r\n      stage-manager were attempting to make him give a new\r\n      interpretation of a part he had conned for years.\r\n\r\n      But Clara talking, Clara telling a slender tale of a hatpin and\r\n      an inebriated man and herself.... People tried afterward to\r\n      repeat her anecdotes but for the life of them they could make\r\n      them sound like nothing whatever. They gave her a sort of\r\n      innocent attention and the best smiles many of them had smiled\r\n      for long; there were few tears in Clara, but people smiled\r\n      misty-eyed at her.\r\n\r\n      Very occasionally Amory stayed for little half-hours after the\r\n      rest of the court had gone, and they would have bread and jam and\r\n      tea late in the afternoon or \xe2\x80\x9cmaple-sugar lunches,\xe2\x80\x9d as she called\r\n      them, at night.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou _are_ remarkable, aren\xe2\x80\x99t you!\xe2\x80\x9d Amory was becoming trite from\r\n      where he perched in the centre of the dining-room table one six\r\n      o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNot a bit,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered. She was searching out napkins in the\r\n      sideboard. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m really most humdrum and commonplace. One of those\r\n      people who have no interest in anything but their children.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cTell that to somebody else,\xe2\x80\x9d scoffed Amory. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\n      perfectly effulgent.\xe2\x80\x9d He asked her the one thing that he knew\r\n      might embarrass her. It was the remark that the first bore made\r\n      to Adam.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cTell me about yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d And she gave the answer that Adam must\r\n      have given.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing to tell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      But eventually Adam probably told the bore all the things he\r\n      thought about at night when the locusts sang in the sandy grass,\r\n      and he must have remarked patronizingly how _different_ he was\r\n      from Eve, forgetting how different she was from him... at any\r\n      rate, Clara told Amory much about herself that evening. She had\r\n      had a harried life from sixteen on, and her education had stopped\r\n      sharply with her leisure. Browsing in her library, Amory found a\r\n      tattered gray book out of which fell a yellow sheet that he\r\n      impudently opened. It was a poem that she had written at school\r\n      about a gray convent wall on a gray day, and a girl with her\r\n      cloak blown by the wind sitting atop of it and thinking about the\r\n      many-colored world. As a rule such sentiment bored him, but this\r\n      was done with so much simplicity and atmosphere, that it brought\r\n      a picture of Clara to his mind, of Clara on such a cool, gray day\r\n      with her keen blue eyes staring out, trying to see her tragedies\r\n      come marching over the gardens outside. He envied that poem. How\r\n      he would have loved to have come along and seen her on the wall\r\n      and talked nonsense or romance to her, perched above him in the\r\n      air. He began to be frightfully jealous of everything about\r\n      Clara: of her past, of her babies, of the men and women who\r\n      flocked to drink deep of her cool kindness and rest their tired\r\n      minds as at an absorbing play.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9c_Nobody_ seems to bore you,\xe2\x80\x9d he objected.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAbout half the world do,\xe2\x80\x9d she admitted, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I think that\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\n      pretty good average, don\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d and she turned to find something\r\n      in Browning that bore on the subject. She was the only person he\r\n      ever met who could look up passages and quotations to show him in\r\n      the middle of the conversation, and yet not be irritating to\r\n      distraction. She did it constantly, with such a serious\r\n      enthusiasm that he grew fond of watching her golden hair bent\r\n      over a book, brow wrinkled ever so little at hunting her\r\n      sentence.\r\n\r\n      Through early March he took to going to Philadelphia for\r\n      week-ends. Almost always there was some one else there and she\r\n      seemed not anxious to see him alone, for many occasions presented\r\n      themselves when a word from her would have given him another\r\n      delicious half-hour of adoration. But he fell gradually in love\r\n      and began to speculate wildly on marriage. Though this design\r\n      flowed through his brain even to his lips, still he knew\r\n      afterward that the desire had not been deeply rooted. Once he\r\n      dreamt that it had come true and woke up in a cold panic, for in\r\n      his dream she had been a silly, flaxen Clara, with the gold gone\r\n      out of her hair and platitudes falling insipidly from her\r\n      changeling tongue. But she was the first fine woman he ever knew\r\n      and one of the few good people who ever interested him. She made\r\n      her goodness such an asset. Amory had decided that most good\r\n      people either dragged theirs after them as a liability, or else\r\n      distorted it to artificial geniality, and of course there were\r\n      the ever-present prig and Pharisee\xe2\x80\x94(but Amory never included\r\n      _them_ as being among the saved).\r\n\r\n\r\n      ST. CECILIA\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cOver her gray and velvet dress, Under her molten, beaten hair,\r\n   Color of rose in mock distress Flushes and fades and makes her fair;\r\n   Fills the air from her to him With light and languor and little\r\n   sighs, Just so subtly he scarcely knows... Laughing lightning, color\r\n   of rose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDo you like me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOf course I do,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clara seriously.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, we have some qualities in common. Things that are\r\n      spontaneous in each of us\xe2\x80\x94or were originally.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re implying that I haven\xe2\x80\x99t used myself very well?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Clara hesitated.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I can\xe2\x80\x99t judge. A man, of course, has to go through a lot\r\n      more, and I\xe2\x80\x99ve been sheltered.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, don\xe2\x80\x99t stall, please, Clara,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory interrupted; \xe2\x80\x9cbut do talk\r\n      about me a little, won\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSurely, I\xe2\x80\x99d adore to.\xe2\x80\x9d She didn\xe2\x80\x99t smile.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s sweet of you. First answer some questions. Am I painfully\r\n      conceited?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94no, you have tremendous vanity, but it\xe2\x80\x99ll amuse the people\r\n      who notice its preponderance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re really humble at heart. You sink to the third hell of\r\n      depression when you think you\xe2\x80\x99ve been slighted. In fact, you\r\n      haven\xe2\x80\x99t much self-respect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCentre of target twice, Clara. How do you do it? You never let\r\n      me say a word.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOf course not\xe2\x80\x94I can never judge a man while he\xe2\x80\x99s talking. But\r\n      I\xe2\x80\x99m not through; the reason you have so little real\r\n      self-confidence, even though you gravely announce to the\r\n      occasional philistine that you think you\xe2\x80\x99re a genius, is that\r\n      you\xe2\x80\x99ve attributed all sorts of atrocious faults to yourself and\r\n      are trying to live up to them. For instance, you\xe2\x80\x99re always saying\r\n      that you are a slave to high-balls.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBut I am, potentially.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you say you\xe2\x80\x99re a weak character, that you\xe2\x80\x99ve no will.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNot a bit of will\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m a slave to my emotions, to my likes, to my\r\n      hatred of boredom, to most of my desires\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou are not!\xe2\x80\x9d She brought one little fist down onto the other.\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re a slave, a bound helpless slave to one thing in the\r\n      world, your imagination.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou certainly interest me. If this isn\xe2\x80\x99t boring you, go on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI notice that when you want to stay over an extra day from\r\n      college you go about it in a sure way. You never decide at first\r\n      while the merits of going or staying are fairly clear in your\r\n      mind. You let your imagination shinny on the side of your desires\r\n      for a few hours, and then you decide. Naturally your imagination,\r\n      after a little freedom, thinks up a million reasons why you\r\n      should stay, so your decision when it comes isn\xe2\x80\x99t true. It\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      biassed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d objected Amory, \xe2\x80\x9cbut isn\xe2\x80\x99t it lack of will-power to let my\r\n      imagination shinny on the wrong side?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear boy, there\xe2\x80\x99s your big mistake. This has nothing to do\r\n      with will-power; that\xe2\x80\x99s a crazy, useless word, anyway; you lack\r\n      judgment\xe2\x80\x94the judgment to decide at once when you know your\r\n      imagination will play you false, given half a chance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I\xe2\x80\x99ll be darned!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Amory in surprise, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\n      last thing I expected.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Clara didn\xe2\x80\x99t gloat. She changed the subject immediately. But she\r\n      had started him thinking and he believed she was partly right. He\r\n      felt like a factory-owner who after accusing a clerk of\r\n      dishonesty finds that his own son, in the office, is changing the\r\n      books once a week. His poor, mistreated will that he had been\r\n      holding up to the scorn of himself and his friends, stood before\r\n      him innocent, and his judgment walked off to prison with the\r\n      unconfinable imp, imagination, dancing in mocking glee beside\r\n      him. Clara\xe2\x80\x99s was the only advice he ever asked without dictating\r\n      the answer himself\xe2\x80\x94except, perhaps, in his talks with Monsignor\r\n      Darcy.\r\n\r\n      How he loved to do any sort of thing with Clara! Shopping with\r\n      her was a rare, epicurean dream. In every store where she had\r\n      ever traded she was whispered about as the beautiful Mrs. Page.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll bet she won\xe2\x80\x99t stay single long.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, don\xe2\x80\x99t scream it out.
