She ain\xe2\x80\x99t lookin\xe2\x80\x99 for no advice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9c_Ain\xe2\x80\x99t_ she beautiful!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n   (Enter a floor-walker\xe2\x80\x94silence till he moves forward, smirking.)\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSociety person, ain\xe2\x80\x99t she?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYeah, but poor now, I guess; so they say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGee! girls, _ain\xe2\x80\x99t_ she some kid!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      And Clara beamed on all alike. Amory believed that tradespeople\r\n      gave her discounts, sometimes to her knowledge and sometimes\r\n      without it. He knew she dressed very well, had always the best of\r\n      everything in the house, and was inevitably waited upon by the\r\n      head floor-walker at the very least.\r\n\r\n      Sometimes they would go to church together on Sunday and he would\r\n      walk beside her and revel in her cheeks moist from the soft water\r\n      in the new air. She was very devout, always had been, and God\r\n      knows what heights she attained and what strength she drew down\r\n      to herself when she knelt and bent her golden hair into the\r\n      stained-glass light.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSt. Cecelia,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried aloud one day, quite involuntarily, and\r\n      the people turned and peered, and the priest paused in his sermon\r\n      and Clara and Amory turned to fiery red.\r\n\r\n      That was the last Sunday they had, for he spoiled it all that\r\n      night. He couldn\xe2\x80\x99t help it.\r\n\r\n      They were walking through the March twilight where it was as warm\r\n      as June, and the joy of youth filled his soul so that he felt he\r\n      must speak.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI think,\xe2\x80\x9d he said and his voice trembled, \xe2\x80\x9cthat if I lost faith\r\n      in you I\xe2\x80\x99d lose faith in God.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      She looked at him with such a startled face that he asked her the\r\n      matter.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNothing,\xe2\x80\x9d she said slowly, \xe2\x80\x9conly this: five men have said that\r\n      to me before, and it frightens me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Clara, is that your fate!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      She did not answer.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose love to you is\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he began.\r\n\r\n      She turned like a flash.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI have never been in love.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      They walked along, and he realized slowly how much she had told\r\n      him... never in love.... She seemed suddenly a daughter of light\r\n      alone. His entity dropped out of her plane and he longed only to\r\n      touch her dress with almost the realization that Joseph must have\r\n      had of Mary\xe2\x80\x99s eternal significance. But quite mechanically he\r\n      heard himself saying:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I love you\xe2\x80\x94any latent greatness that I\xe2\x80\x99ve got is... oh, I\r\n      can\xe2\x80\x99t talk, but Clara, if I come back in two years in a position\r\n      to marry you\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      She shook her head.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99d never marry again. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got my two children\r\n      and I want myself for them. I like you\xe2\x80\x94I like all clever men, you\r\n      more than any\xe2\x80\x94but you know me well enough to know that I\xe2\x80\x99d never\r\n      marry a clever man\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d She broke off suddenly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAmory.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not in love with me. You never wanted to marry me, did\r\n      you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt was the twilight,\xe2\x80\x9d he said wonderingly. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t feel as\r\n      though I were speaking aloud. But I love you\xe2\x80\x94or adore you\xe2\x80\x94or\r\n      worship you\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThere you go\xe2\x80\x94running through your catalogue of emotions in five\r\n      seconds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He smiled unwillingly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t make me out such a light-weight, Clara; you _are_\r\n      depressing sometimes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re not a light-weight, of all things,\xe2\x80\x9d she said intently,\r\n      taking his arm and opening wide her eyes\xe2\x80\x94he could see their\r\n      kindliness in the fading dusk. \xe2\x80\x9cA light-weight is an eternal\r\n      nay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s so much spring in the air\xe2\x80\x94there\xe2\x80\x99s so much lazy sweetness\r\n      in your heart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      She dropped his arm.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re all fine now, and I feel glorious. Give me a cigarette.\r\n      You\xe2\x80\x99ve never seen me smoke, have you? Well, I do, about once a\r\n      month.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      And then that wonderful girl and Amory raced to the corner like\r\n      two mad children gone wild with pale-blue twilight.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going to the country for to-morrow,\xe2\x80\x9d she announced, as she\r\n      stood panting, safe beyond the flare of the corner lamp-post.\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThese days are too magnificent to miss, though perhaps I feel\r\n      them more in the city.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Clara!\xe2\x80\x9d Amory said; \xe2\x80\x9cwhat a devil you could have been if the\r\n      Lord had just bent your soul a little the other way!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMaybe,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I think not. I\xe2\x80\x99m never really wild\r\n      and never have been. That little outburst was pure spring.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you are, too,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n      They were walking along now.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99re wrong again, how can a person of your own self-reputed\r\n      brains be so constantly wrong about me? I\xe2\x80\x99m the opposite of\r\n      everything spring ever stood for. It\xe2\x80\x99s unfortunate, if I happen\r\n      to look like what pleased some soppy old Greek sculptor, but I\r\n      assure you that if it weren\xe2\x80\x99t for my face I\xe2\x80\x99d be a quiet nun in\r\n      the convent without\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94then she broke into a run and her raised\r\n      voice floated back to him as he followed\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cmy precious babies,\r\n      which I must go back and see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      She was the only girl he ever knew with whom he could understand\r\n      how another man might be preferred. Often Amory met wives whom he\r\n      had known as debutantes, and looking intently at them imagined\r\n      that he found something in their faces which said:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, if I could only have gotten _you!_\xe2\x80\x9d Oh, the enormous conceit\r\n      of the man!\r\n\r\n      But that night seemed a night of stars and singing and Clara\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      bright soul still gleamed on the ways they had trod.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGolden, golden is the air\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he chanted to the little pools of\r\n      water. ... \xe2\x80\x9cGolden is the air, golden notes from golden\r\n      mandolins, golden frets of golden violins, fair, oh, wearily\r\n      fair.... Skeins from braided basket, mortals may not hold; oh,\r\n      what young extravagant God, who would know or ask it?... who\r\n      could give such gold...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n      AMORY IS RESENTFUL\r\n\r\n      Slowly and inevitably, yet with a sudden surge at the last, while\r\n      Amory talked and dreamed, war rolled swiftly up the beach and\r\n      washed the sands where Princeton played. Every night the\r\n      gymnasium echoed as platoon after platoon swept over the floor\r\n      and shuffled out the basket-ball markings. When Amory went to\r\n      Washington the next week-end he caught some of the spirit of\r\n      crisis which changed to repulsion in the Pullman car coming back,\r\n      for the berths across from him were occupied by stinking\r\n      aliens\xe2\x80\x94Greeks, he guessed, or Russians. He thought how much\r\n      easier patriotism had been to a homogeneous race, how much easier\r\n      it would have been to fight as the Colonies fought, or as the\r\n      Confederacy fought. And he did no sleeping that night, but\r\n      listened to the aliens guffaw and snore while they filled the car\r\n      with the heavy scent of latest America.\r\n\r\n      In Princeton every one bantered in public and told themselves\r\n      privately that their deaths at least would be heroic. The\r\n      literary students read Rupert Brooke passionately; the\r\n      lounge-lizards worried over whether the government would permit\r\n      the English-cut uniform for officers; a few of the hopelessly\r\n      lazy wrote to the obscure branches of the War Department, seeking\r\n      an easy commission and a soft berth.\r\n\r\n      Then, after a week, Amory saw Burne and knew at once that\r\n      argument would be futile\xe2\x80\x94Burne had come out as a pacifist. The\r\n      socialist magazines, a great smattering of Tolstoi, and his own\r\n      intense longing for a cause that would bring out whatever\r\n      strength lay in him, had finally decided him to preach peace as a\r\n      subjective ideal.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhen the German army entered Belgium,\xe2\x80\x9d he began, \xe2\x80\x9cif the\r\n      inhabitants had gone peaceably about their business, the German\r\n      army would have been disorganized in\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI know,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory interrupted, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve heard it all. But I\xe2\x80\x99m not\r\n      going to talk propaganda with you. There\xe2\x80\x99s a chance that you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\n      right\xe2\x80\x94but even so we\xe2\x80\x99re hundreds of years before the time when\r\n      non-resistance can touch us as a reality.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBut, Amory, listen\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBurne, we\xe2\x80\x99d just argue\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cVery well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cJust one thing\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t ask you to think of your family or\r\n      friends, because I know they don\xe2\x80\x99t count a picayune with you\r\n      beside your sense of duty\xe2\x80\x94but, Burne, how do you know that the\r\n      magazines you read and the societies you join and these idealists\r\n      you meet aren\xe2\x80\x99t just plain _German?_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSome of them are, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHow do you know they aren\xe2\x80\x99t _all_ pro-German\xe2\x80\x94just a lot of weak\r\n      ones\xe2\x80\x94with German-Jewish names.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the chance, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d he said slowly. \xe2\x80\x9cHow much or how\r\n      little I\xe2\x80\x99m taking this stand because of propaganda I\xe2\x80\x99ve heard, I\r\n      don\xe2\x80\x99t know; naturally I think that it\xe2\x80\x99s my most innermost\r\n      conviction\xe2\x80\x94it seems a path spread before me just now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory\xe2\x80\x99s heart sank.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBut think of the cheapness of it\xe2\x80\x94no one\xe2\x80\x99s really going to martyr\r\n      you for being a pacifist\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s just going to throw you in with the\r\n      worst\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI doubt it,\xe2\x80\x9d he interrupted.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, it all smells of Bohemian New York to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI know what you mean, and that\xe2\x80\x99s why I\xe2\x80\x99m not sure I\xe2\x80\x99ll agitate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re one man, Burne\xe2\x80\x94going to talk to people who won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      listen\xe2\x80\x94with all God\xe2\x80\x99s given you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what Stephen must have thought many years ago. But he\r\n      preached his sermon and they killed him. He probably thought as\r\n      he was dying what a waste it all was. But you see, I\xe2\x80\x99ve always\r\n      felt that Stephen\xe2\x80\x99s death was the thing that occurred to Paul on\r\n      the road to Damascus, and sent him to preach the word of Christ\r\n      all over the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGo on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all\xe2\x80\x94this is my particular duty. Even if right now I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\n      just a pawn\xe2\x80\x94just sacrificed. God! Amory\xe2\x80\x94you don\xe2\x80\x99t think I like\r\n      the Germans!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I can\xe2\x80\x99t say anything else\xe2\x80\x94I get to the end of all the\r\n      logic about non-resistance, and there, like an excluded middle,\r\n      stands the huge spectre of man as he is and always will be. And\r\n      this spectre stands right beside the one logical necessity of\r\n      Tolstoi\xe2\x80\x99s, and the other logical necessity of Nietzsche\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d Amory\r\n      broke off suddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cWhen are you going?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going next week.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll see you, of course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      As he walked away it seemed to Amory that the look in his face\r\n      bore a great resemblance to that in Kerry\xe2\x80\x99s when he had said\r\n      good-by under Blair Arch two years before. Amory wondered\r\n      unhappily why he could never go into anything with the primal\r\n      honesty of those two.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBurne\xe2\x80\x99s a fanatic,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Tom, \xe2\x80\x9cand he\xe2\x80\x99s dead wrong and,\r\n      I\xe2\x80\x99m inclined to think, just an unconscious pawn in the hands of\r\n      anarchistic publishers and German-paid rag wavers\xe2\x80\x94but he haunts\r\n      me\xe2\x80\x94just leaving everything worth while\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Burne left in a quietly dramatic manner a week later. He sold all\r\n      his possessions and came down to the room to say good-by, with a\r\n      battered old bicycle, on which he intended to ride to his home in\r\n      Pennsylvania.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cPeter the Hermit bidding farewell to Cardinal Richelieu,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      suggested Alec, who was lounging in the window-seat as Burne and\r\n      Amory shook hands.\r\n\r\n      But Amory was not in a mood for that, and as he saw Burne\xe2\x80\x99s long\r\n      legs propel his ridiculous bicycle out of sight beyond Alexander\r\n      Hall, he knew he was going to have a bad week. Not that he\r\n      doubted the war\xe2\x80\x94Germany stood for everything repugnant to him;\r\n      for materialism and the direction of tremendous licentious force;\r\n      it was just that Burne\xe2\x80\x99s face stayed in his memory and he was\r\n      sick of the hysteria he was beginning to hear.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat on earth is the use of suddenly running down Goethe,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\n      declared to Alec and Tom. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy write books to prove he started\r\n      the war\xe2\x80\x94or that that stupid, overestimated Schiller is a demon in\r\n      disguise?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHave you ever read anything of theirs?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Tom shrewdly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory admitted.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNeither have I,\xe2\x80\x9d he said laughing.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cPeople will shout,\xe2\x80\x9d said Alec quietly, \xe2\x80\x9cbut Goethe\xe2\x80\x99s on his same\r\n      old shelf in the library\xe2\x80\x94to bore any one that wants to read him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory subsided, and the subject dropped.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you going to do, Amory?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cInfantry or aviation, I can\xe2\x80\x99t make up my mind\xe2\x80\x94I hate mechanics,\r\n      but then of course aviation\xe2\x80\x99s the thing for me\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI feel as Amory does,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom. \xe2\x80\x9cInfantry or aviation\xe2\x80\x94aviation\r\n      sounds like the romantic side of the war, of course\xe2\x80\x94like cavalry\r\n      used to be, you know; but like Amory I don\xe2\x80\x99t know a horse-power\r\n      from a piston-rod.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Somehow Amory\xe2\x80\x99s dissatisfaction with his lack of enthusiasm\r\n      culminated in an attempt to put the blame for the whole war on\r\n      the ancestors of his generation... all the people who cheered for\r\n      Germany in 1870.... All the materialists rampant, all the\r\n      idolizers of German science and efficiency. So he sat one day in\r\n      an English lecture and heard \xe2\x80\x9cLocksley Hall\xe2\x80\x9d quoted and fell into\r\n      a brown study with contempt for Tennyson and all he stood for\xe2\x80\x94for\r\n      he took him as a representative of the Victorians.\r\n\r\n  Victorians, Victorians, who never learned to weep Who sowed the\r\n  bitter harvest that your children go to reap\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n      scribbled Amory in his note-book. The lecturer was saying\r\n      something about Tennyson\xe2\x80\x99s solidity and fifty heads were bent to\r\n      take notes. Amory turned over to a fresh page and began scrawling\r\n      again.\r\n\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cThey shuddered when they found what Mr. Darwin was about, They\r\n shuddered when the waltz came in and Newman hurried out\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      But the waltz came in much earlier; he crossed that out.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd entitled A Song in the Time of Order,\xe2\x80\x9d came the professor\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      voice, droning far away. \xe2\x80\x9cTime of Order\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Good Lord! Everything\r\n      crammed in the box and the Victorians sitting on the lid smiling\r\n      serenely.... With Browning in his Italian villa crying bravely:\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAll\xe2\x80\x99s for the best.\xe2\x80\x9d Amory scribbled again.\r\n\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cYou knelt up in the temple and he bent to hear you pray, You thanked\r\n him for your \xe2\x80\x98glorious gains\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94reproached him for \xe2\x80\x98Cathay.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Why could he never get more than a couplet at a time? Now he\r\n      needed something to rhyme with:\r\n\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cYou would keep Him straight with science, tho He had gone wrong\r\n before...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Well, anyway....\r\n\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cYou met your children in your home\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98I\xe2\x80\x99ve fixed it up!\xe2\x80\x99 you cried,\r\n Took your fifty years of Europe, and then virtuously\xe2\x80\x94died.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat was to a great extent Tennyson\xe2\x80\x99s idea,\xe2\x80\x9d came the lecturer\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      voice. \xe2\x80\x9cSwinburne\xe2\x80\x99s Song in the Time of Order might well have\r\n      been Tennyson\xe2\x80\x99s title. He idealized order against chaos, against\r\n      waste.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      At last Amory had it. He turned over another page and scrawled\r\n      vigorously for the twenty minutes that was left of the hour. Then\r\n      he walked up to the desk and deposited a page torn out of his\r\n      note-book.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHere\xe2\x80\x99s a poem to the Victorians, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d he said coldly.\r\n\r\n      The professor picked it up curiously while Amory backed rapidly\r\n      through the door.\r\n\r\n      Here is what he had written:\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cSongs in the time of order You left for us to sing, Proofs with\r\n   excluded middles, Answers to life in rhyme, Keys of the prison\r\n   warder And ancient bells to ring, Time was the end of riddles, We\r\n   were the end of time...\r\n    Here were domestic oceans And a sky that we might reach, Guns and a\r\n    guarded border, Gantlets\xe2\x80\x94but not to fling, Thousands of old\r\n    emotions And a platitude for each, Songs in the time of order\xe2\x80\x94 And\r\n    tongues, that we might sing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n      THE END OF MANY THINGS\r\n\r\n      Early April slipped by in a haze\xe2\x80\x94a haze of long evenings on the\r\n      club veranda with the graphophone playing \xe2\x80\x9cPoor Butterfly\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      inside... for \xe2\x80\x9cPoor Butterfly\xe2\x80\x9d had been the song of that last\r\n      year. The war seemed scarcely to touch them and it might have\r\n      been one of the senior springs of the past, except for the\r\n      drilling every other afternoon, yet Amory realized poignantly\r\n      that this was the last spring under the old regime.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThis is the great protest against the superman,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose so,\xe2\x80\x9d Alec agreed.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s absolutely irreconcilable with any Utopia. As long as he\r\n      occurs, there\xe2\x80\x99s trouble and all the latent evil that makes a\r\n      crowd list and sway when he talks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd of course all that he is is a gifted man without a moral\r\n      sense.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s all. I think the worst thing to contemplate is this\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      all happened before, how soon will it happen again? Fifty years\r\n      after Waterloo Napoleon was as much a hero to English school\r\n      children as Wellington. How do we know our grandchildren won\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      idolize Von Hindenburg the same way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat brings it about?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cTime, damn it, and the historian. If we could only learn to look\r\n      on evil as evil, whether it\xe2\x80\x99s clothed in filth or monotony or\r\n      magnificence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGod! Haven\xe2\x80\x99t we raked the universe over the coals for four\r\n      years?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Then the night came that was to be the last. Tom and Amory, bound\r\n      in the morning for different training-camps, paced the shadowy\r\n      walks as usual and seemed still to see around them the faces of\r\n      the men they knew.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThe grass is full of ghosts to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThe whole campus is alive with them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      They paused by Little and watched the moon rise, to make silver\r\n      of the slate roof of Dodd and blue the rustling trees.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou know,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Tom, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat we feel now is the sense of all\r\n      the gorgeous youth that has rioted through here in two hundred\r\n      years.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      A last burst of singing flooded up from Blair Arch\xe2\x80\x94broken voices\r\n      for some long parting.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd what we leave here is more than this class; it\xe2\x80\x99s the whole\r\n      heritage of youth. We\xe2\x80\x99re just one generation\xe2\x80\x94we\xe2\x80\x99re breaking all\r\n      the links that seemed to bind us here to top-booted and\r\n      high-stocked generations. We\xe2\x80\x99ve walked arm and arm with Burr and\r\n      Light-Horse Harry Lee through half these deep-blue nights.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what they are,\xe2\x80\x9d Tom tangented off, \xe2\x80\x9cdeep blue\xe2\x80\x94a bit of\r\n      color would spoil them, make them exotic. Spires, against a sky\r\n      that\xe2\x80\x99s a promise of dawn, and blue light on the slate roofs\xe2\x80\x94it\r\n      hurts... rather\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGood-by, Aaron Burr,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory called toward deserted Nassau Hall,\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cyou and I knew strange corners of life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      His voice echoed in the stillness.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThe torches are out,\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Tom. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, Messalina, the long\r\n      shadows are building minarets on the stadium\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      For an instant the voices of freshman year surged around them and\r\n      then they looked at each other with faint tears in their eyes.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDamn!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDamn!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The last light fades and drifts across the land\xe2\x80\x94the low, long\r\n      land, the sunny land of spires; the ghosts of evening tune again\r\n      their lyres and wander singing in a plaintive band down the long\r\n      corridors of trees; pale fires echo the night from tower top to\r\n      tower: Oh, sleep that dreams, and dream that never tires, press\r\n      from the petals of the lotus flower something of this to keep,\r\n      the essence of an hour.\r\n\r\n      No more to wait the twilight of the moon in this sequestered vale\r\n      of star and spire, for one eternal morning of desire passes to\r\n      time and earthy afternoon. Here, Heraclitus, did you find in fire\r\n      and shifting things the prophecy you hurled down the dead years;\r\n      this midnight my desire will see, shadowed among the embers,\r\n      furled in flame, the splendor and the sadness of the world.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n      INTERLUDE\r\n\r\n      May, 1917-February, 1919\r\n\r\n      A letter dated January, 1918, written by Monsignor Darcy to\r\n      Amory, who is a second lieutenant in the 171st Infantry, Port of\r\n      Embarkation, Camp Mills, Long Island.\r\n\r\n      MY DEAR BOY:\r\n\r\n      All you need tell me of yourself is that you still are; for the\r\n      rest I merely search back in a restive memory, a thermometer that\r\n      records only fevers, and match you with what I was at your age.\r\n      But men will chatter and you and I will still shout our\r\n      futilities to each other across the stage until the last silly\r\n      curtain falls _plump!_ upon our bobbing heads. But you are\r\n      starting the spluttering magic-lantern show of life with much the\r\n      same array of slides as I had, so I need to write you if only to\r\n      shriek the colossal stupidity of people....\r\n\r\n      This is the end of one thing: for better or worse you will never\r\n      again be quite the Amory Blaine that I knew, never again will we\r\n      meet as we have met, because your generation is growing hard,\r\n      much harder than mine ever grew, nourished as they were on the\r\n      stuff of the nineties.\r\n\r\n      Amory, lately I reread Aeschylus and there in the divine irony of\r\n      the \xe2\x80\x9cAgamemnon\xe2\x80\x9d I find the only answer to this bitter age\xe2\x80\x94all the\r\n      world tumbled about our ears, and the closest parallel ages back\r\n      in that hopeless resignation. There are times when I think of the\r\n      men out there as Roman legionaries, miles from their corrupt\r\n      city, stemming back the hordes... hordes a little more menacing,\r\n      after all, than the corrupt city... another blind blow at the\r\n      race, furies that we passed with ovations years ago, over whose\r\n      corpses we bleated triumphantly all through the Victorian era....\r\n\r\n      And afterward an out-and-out materialistic world\xe2\x80\x94and the Catholic\r\n      Church. I wonder where you\xe2\x80\x99ll fit in. Of one thing I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\n      sure\xe2\x80\x94Celtic you\xe2\x80\x99ll live and Celtic you\xe2\x80\x99ll die; so if you don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      use heaven as a continual referendum for your ideas you\xe2\x80\x99ll find\r\n      earth a continual recall to your ambitions.\r\n\r\n      Amory, I\xe2\x80\x99ve discovered suddenly that I\xe2\x80\x99m an old man. Like all old\r\n      men, I\xe2\x80\x99ve had dreams sometimes and I\xe2\x80\x99m going to tell you of them.\r\n      I\xe2\x80\x99ve enjoyed imagining that you were my son, that perhaps when I\r\n      was young I went into a state of coma and begat you, and when I\r\n      came to, had no recollection of it... it\xe2\x80\x99s the paternal instinct,\r\n      Amory\xe2\x80\x94celibacy goes deeper than the flesh....\r\n\r\n      Sometimes I think that the explanation of our deep resemblance is\r\n      some common ancestor, and I find that the only blood that the\r\n      Darcys and the O\xe2\x80\x99Haras have in common is that of the\r\n      O\xe2\x80\x99Donahues... Stephen was his name, I think....\r\n\r\n      When the lightning strikes one of us it strikes both: you had\r\n      hardly arrived at the port of embarkation when I got my papers to\r\n      start for Rome, and I am waiting every moment to be told where to\r\n      take ship. Even before you get this letter I shall be on the\r\n      ocean; then will come your turn. You went to war as a gentleman\r\n      should, just as you went to school and college, because it was\r\n      the thing to do. It\xe2\x80\x99s better to leave the blustering and\r\n      tremulo-heroism to the middle classes; they do it so much better.\r\n\r\n      Do you remember that week-end last March when you brought Burne\r\n      Holiday from Princeton to see me? What a magnificent boy he is!\r\n      It gave me a frightful shock afterward when you wrote that he\r\n      thought me splendid; how could he be so deceived? Splendid is the\r\n      one thing that neither you nor I are. We are many other\r\n      things\xe2\x80\x94we\xe2\x80\x99re extraordinary, we\xe2\x80\x99re clever, we could be said, I\r\n      suppose, to be brilliant. We can attract people, we can make\r\n      atmosphere, we can almost lose our Celtic souls in Celtic\r\n      subtleties, we can almost always have our own way; but\r\n      splendid\xe2\x80\x94rather not!\r\n\r\n      I am going to Rome with a wonderful dossier and letters of\r\n      introduction that cover every capital in Europe, and there will\r\n      be \xe2\x80\x9cno small stir\xe2\x80\x9d when I get there. How I wish you were with me!\r\n      This sounds like a rather cynical paragraph, not at all the sort\r\n      of thing that a middle-aged clergyman should write to a youth\r\n      about to depart for the war; the only excuse is that the\r\n      middle-aged clergyman is talking to himself. There are deep\r\n      things in us and you know what they are as well as I do. We have\r\n      great faith, though yours at present is uncrystallized; we have a\r\n      terrible honesty that all our sophistry cannot destroy and, above\r\n      all, a childlike simplicity that keeps us from ever being really\r\n      malicious.\r\n\r\n      I have written a keen for you which follows. I am sorry your\r\n      cheeks are not up to the description I have written of them, but\r\n      you _will_ smoke and read all night\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n      At any rate here it is:\r\n\r\n      A Lament for a Foster Son, and He going to the War Against the\r\n      King of Foreign.\r\n\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cOchone He is gone from me the son of my mind And he in his golden\r\n youth like Angus Oge Angus of the bright birds And his mind strong and\r\n subtle like the mind of Cuchulin on Muirtheme.\r\n  Awirra sthrue His brow is as white as the milk of the cows of Maeve\r\n  And his cheeks like the cherries of the tree And it bending down to\r\n  Mary and she feeding the Son of God.\r\n  Aveelia Vrone His hair is like the golden collar of the Kings at Tara\r\n  And his eyes like the four gray seas of Erin. And they swept with the\r\n  mists of rain.\r\n  Mavrone go Gudyo He to be in the joyful and red battle Amongst the\r\n  chieftains and they doing great deeds of valor His life to go from\r\n  him It is the chords of my own soul would be loosed.\r\n  A Vich Deelish My heart is in the heart of my son And my life is in\r\n  his life surely A man can be twice young In the life of his sons\r\n  only.\r\n  Jia du Vaha Alanav May the Son of God be above him and beneath him,\r\n  before him and behind him May the King of the elements cast a mist\r\n  over the eyes of the King of Foreign, May the Queen of the Graces\r\n  lead him by the hand the way he can go through the midst of his\r\n  enemies and they not seeing him\r\n  May Patrick of the Gael and Collumb of the Churches and the five\r\n  thousand Saints of Erin be better than a shield to him And he got\r\n  into the fight. Och Ochone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory\xe2\x80\x94Amory\xe2\x80\x94I feel, somehow, that this is all; one or both of us\r\n      is not going to last out this war.... I\xe2\x80\x99ve been trying to tell\r\n      you how much this reincarnation of myself in you has meant in the\r\n      last few years... curiously alike we are... curiously unlike.\r\n      Good-by, dear boy, and God be with you. THAYER DARCY.\r\n\r\n\r\n      EMBARKING AT NIGHT\r\n\r\n      Amory moved forward on the deck until he found a stool under an\r\n      electric light. He searched in his pocket for note-book and\r\n      pencil and then began to write, slowly, laboriously:\r\n\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cWe leave to-night... Silent, we filled the still, deserted street, A\r\n column of dim gray, And ghosts rose startled at the muffled beat Along\r\n the moonless way; The shadowy shipyards echoed to the feet That turned\r\n from night and day.\r\n    And so we linger on the windless decks, See on the spectre shore\r\n    Shades of a thousand days, poor gray-ribbed wrecks... Oh, shall we\r\n    then deplore Those futile years! See how the sea is white! The\r\n    clouds have broken and the heavens burn To hollow highways, paved\r\n    with gravelled light The churning of the waves about the stern\r\n    Rises to one voluminous nocturne, ... We leave to-night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      A letter from Amory, headed \xe2\x80\x9cBrest, March 11th, 1919,\xe2\x80\x9d to\r\n      Lieutenant T. P. D\xe2\x80\x99Invilliers, Camp Gordon, Ga.\r\n\r\n      DEAR BAUDELAIRE:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n      We meet in Manhattan on the 30th of this very mo.; we then\r\n      proceed to take a very sporty apartment, you and I and Alec, who\r\n      is at me elbow as I write. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what I\xe2\x80\x99m going to do but\r\n      I have a vague dream of going into politics. Why is it that the\r\n      pick of the young Englishmen from Oxford and Cambridge go into\r\n      politics and in the U. S. A. we leave it to the muckers?\xe2\x80\x94raised\r\n      in the ward, educated in the assembly and sent to Congress,\r\n      fat-paunched bundles of corruption, devoid of \xe2\x80\x9cboth ideas and\r\n      ideals\xe2\x80\x9d as the debaters used to say. Even forty years ago we had\r\n      good men in politics, but we, we are brought up to pile up a\r\n      million and \xe2\x80\x9cshow what we are made of.\xe2\x80\x9d Sometimes I wish I\xe2\x80\x99d been\r\n      an Englishman; American life is so damned dumb and stupid and\r\n      healthy.\r\n\r\n      Since poor Beatrice died I\xe2\x80\x99ll probably have a little money, but\r\n      very darn little. I can forgive mother almost everything except\r\n      the fact that in a sudden burst of religiosity toward the end,\r\n      she left half of what remained to be spent in stained-glass\r\n      windows and seminary endowments. Mr. Barton, my lawyer, writes me\r\n      that my thousands are mostly in street railways and that the said\r\n      Street R.R. s are losing money because of the five-cent fares.\r\n      Imagine a salary list that gives $350 a month to a man that can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      read and write!\xe2\x80\x94yet I believe in it, even though I\xe2\x80\x99ve seen what\r\n      was once a sizable fortune melt away between speculation,\r\n      extravagance, the democratic administration, and the income\r\n      tax\xe2\x80\x94modern, that\xe2\x80\x99s me all over, Mabel.\r\n\r\n      At any rate we\xe2\x80\x99ll have really knock-out rooms\xe2\x80\x94you can get a job\r\n      on some fashion magazine, and Alec can go into the Zinc Company\r\n      or whatever it is that his people own\xe2\x80\x94he\xe2\x80\x99s looking over my\r\n      shoulder and he says it\xe2\x80\x99s a brass company, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t think it\r\n      matters much, do you? There\xe2\x80\x99s probably as much corruption in\r\n      zinc-made money as brass-made money. As for the well-known Amory,\r\n      he would write immortal literature if he were sure enough about\r\n      anything to risk telling any one else about it. There is no more\r\n      dangerous gift to posterity than a few cleverly turned\r\n      platitudes.\r\n\r\n      Tom, why don\xe2\x80\x99t you become a Catholic? Of course to be a good one\r\n      you\xe2\x80\x99d have to give up those violent intrigues you used to tell me\r\n      about, but you\xe2\x80\x99d write better poetry if you were linked up to\r\n      tall golden candlesticks and long, even chants, and even if the\r\n      American priests are rather burgeois, as Beatrice used to say,\r\n      still you need only go to the sporty churches, and I\xe2\x80\x99ll introduce\r\n      you to Monsignor Darcy who really is a wonder.\r\n\r\n      Kerry\xe2\x80\x99s death was a blow, so was Jesse\xe2\x80\x99s to a certain extent. And\r\n      I have a great curiosity to know what queer corner of the world\r\n      has swallowed Burne. Do you suppose he\xe2\x80\x99s in prison under some\r\n      false name? I confess that the war instead of making me orthodox,\r\n      which is the correct reaction, has made me a passionate agnostic.\r\n      The Catholic Church has had its wings clipped so often lately\r\n      that its part was timidly negligible, and they haven\xe2\x80\x99t any good\r\n      writers any more. I\xe2\x80\x99m sick of Chesterton.\r\n\r\n      I\xe2\x80\x99ve only discovered one soldier who passed through the\r\n      much-advertised spiritual crisis, like this fellow, Donald\r\n      Hankey, and the one I knew was already studying for the ministry,\r\n      so he was ripe for it. I honestly think that\xe2\x80\x99s all pretty much\r\n      rot, though it seemed to give sentimental comfort to those at\r\n      home; and may make fathers and mothers appreciate their children.\r\n      This crisis-inspired religion is rather valueless and fleeting at\r\n      best. I think four men have discovered Paris to one that\r\n      discovered God.\r\n\r\n      But us\xe2\x80\x94you and me and Alec\xe2\x80\x94oh, we\xe2\x80\x99ll get a Jap butler and dress\r\n      for dinner and have wine on the table and lead a contemplative,\r\n      emotionless life until we decide to use machine-guns with the\r\n      property owners\xe2\x80\x94or throw bombs with the Bolshevik God! Tom, I\r\n      hope something happens. I\xe2\x80\x99m restless as the devil and have a\r\n      horror of getting fat or falling in love and growing domestic.\r\n\r\n      The place at Lake Geneva is now for rent but when I land I\xe2\x80\x99m\r\n      going West to see Mr. Barton and get some details. Write me care\r\n      of the Blackstone, Chicago.\r\n\r\n              S\xe2\x80\x99ever, dear Boswell,\r\n                                  SAMUEL JOHNSON.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n      BOOK TWO\xe2\x80\x94The Education of a Personage\r\n\r\n      CHAPTER 1. The Debutante\r\n\r\n\r\n      The time is February. The place is a large, dainty bedroom in the\r\n      Connage house on Sixty-eighth Street, New York. A girl\xe2\x80\x99s room:\r\n      pink walls and curtains and a pink bedspread on a cream-colored\r\n      bed. Pink and cream are the motifs of the room, but the only\r\n      article of furniture in full view is a luxurious dressing-table\r\n      with a glass top and a three-sided mirror. On the walls there is\r\n      an expensive print of \xe2\x80\x9cCherry Ripe,\xe2\x80\x9d a few polite dogs by\r\n      Landseer, and the \xe2\x80\x9cKing of the Black Isles,\xe2\x80\x9d by Maxfield Parrish.\r\n\r\n      Great disorder consisting of the following items: (1) seven or\r\n      eight empty cardboard boxes, with tissue-paper tongues hanging\r\n      panting from their mouths; (2) an assortment of street dresses\r\n      mingled with their sisters of the evening, all upon the table,\r\n      all evidently new; (3) a roll of tulle, which has lost its\r\n      dignity and wound itself tortuously around everything in sight,\r\n      and (4) upon the two small chairs, a collection of lingerie that\r\n      beggars description. One would enjoy seeing the bill called forth\r\n      by the finery displayed and one is possessed by a desire to see\r\n      the princess for whose benefit\xe2\x80\x94Look! There\xe2\x80\x99s some one!\r\n      Disappointment! This is only a maid hunting for something\xe2\x80\x94she\r\n      lifts a heap from a chair\xe2\x80\x94Not there; another heap, the\r\n      dressing-table, the chiffonier drawers. She brings to light\r\n      several beautiful chemises and an amazing pajama but this does\r\n      not satisfy her\xe2\x80\x94she goes out.\r\n\r\n      An indistinguishable mumble from the next room.\r\n\r\n      Now, we are getting warm. This is Alec\xe2\x80\x99s mother, Mrs. Connage,\r\n      ample, dignified, rouged to the dowager point and quite worn out.\r\n      Her lips move significantly as she looks for IT. Her search is\r\n      less thorough than the maid\xe2\x80\x99s but there is a touch of fury in it,\r\n      that quite makes up for its sketchiness. She stumbles on the\r\n      tulle and her \xe2\x80\x9cdamn\xe2\x80\x9d is quite audible. She retires, empty-handed.\r\n\r\n      More chatter outside and a girl\xe2\x80\x99s voice, a very spoiled voice,\r\n      says: \xe2\x80\x9cOf all the stupid people\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      After a pause a third seeker enters, not she of the spoiled\r\n      voice, but a younger edition. This is Cecelia Connage, sixteen,\r\n      pretty, shrewd, and constitutionally good-humored. She is dressed\r\n      for the evening in a gown the obvious simplicity of which\r\n      probably bores her. She goes to the nearest pile, selects a small\r\n      pink garment and holds it up appraisingly.\r\n\r\n      CECELIA: Pink?\r\n\r\n      ROSALIND: (Outside) Yes!\r\n\r\n      CECELIA: _Very_ snappy?\r\n\r\n      ROSALIND: Yes!\r\n\r\n      CECELIA: I\xe2\x80\x99ve got it!\r\n\r\n      (She sees herself in the mirror of the dressing-table and\r\n      commences to shimmy enthusiastically.)\r\n\r\n      ROSALIND: (Outside) What are you doing\xe2\x80\x94trying it on?\r\n\r\n      (CECELIA ceases and goes out carrying the garment at the right\r\n      shoulder.\r\n\r\n      From the other door, enters ALEC CONNAGE. There used to be two kinds of kisses:\r\n      First when girls were kissed and deserted; second, when they were\r\n      engaged. Now there\xe2\x80\x99s a third kind, where the man is kissed and\r\n      deserted. Oh\xe2\x80\x94I am very youthful,\r\n      thank God\xe2\x80\x94and rather beautiful, thank God\xe2\x80\x94and happy, thank God,\r\n      thank God\xe2\x80\x94(She pauses and then, in an odd burst of prophecy,\r\n      adds) Poor Amory!\r\n\r\n      (He kisses her again.)\r\n\r\n\r\n      KISMET\r\n\r\n      Within two weeks Amory and Rosalind were deeply and passionately\r\n      in love. The critical qualities which had spoiled for each of\r\n      them a dozen romances were dulled by the great wave of emotion\r\n      that washed over them.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt may be an insane love-affair,\xe2\x80\x9d she told her anxious mother,\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cbut it\xe2\x80\x99s not inane.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The wave swept Amory into an advertising agency early in March,\r\n      where he alternated between astonishing bursts of rather\r\n      exceptional work and wild dreams of becoming suddenly rich and\r\n      touring Italy with Rosalind.\r\n\r\n      They were together constantly, for lunch, for dinner, and nearly\r\n      every evening\xe2\x80\x94always in a sort of breathless hush, as if they\r\n      feared that any minute the spell would break and drop them out of\r\n      this paradise of rose and flame. But the spell became a trance,\r\n      seemed to increase from day to day; they began to talk of\r\n      marrying in July\xe2\x80\x94in June. All life was transmitted into terms of\r\n      their love, all experience, all desires, all ambitions, were\r\n      nullified\xe2\x80\x94their senses of humor crawled into corners to sleep;\r\n      their former love-affairs seemed faintly laughable and scarcely\r\n      regretted juvenalia.\r\n\r\n      For the second time in his life Amory had had a complete\r\n      bouleversement and was hurrying into line with his generation.\r\n\r\n\r\n      A LITTLE INTERLUDE\r\n\r\n      Amory wandered slowly up the avenue and thought of the night as\r\n      inevitably his\xe2\x80\x94the pageantry and carnival of rich dusk and dim\r\n      streets ... it seemed that he had closed the book of fading\r\n      harmonies at last and stepped into the sensuous vibrant walks of\r\n      life. Everywhere these countless lights, this promise of a night\r\n      of streets and singing\xe2\x80\x94he moved in a half-dream through the crowd\r\n      as if expecting to meet Rosalind hurrying toward him with eager\r\n      feet from every corner.... How the unforgettable faces of dusk\r\n      would blend to her, the myriad footsteps, a thousand overtures,\r\n      would blend to her footsteps; and there would be more drunkenness\r\n      than wine in the softness of her eyes on his. Even his dreams now\r\n      were faint violins drifting like summer sounds upon the summer\r\n      air.\r\n\r\n      The room was in darkness except for the faint glow of Tom\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      cigarette where he lounged by the open window. As the door shut\r\n      behind him, Amory stood a moment with his back against it.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHello, Benvenuto Blaine. How went the advertising business\r\n      to-day?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory sprawled on a couch.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI loathed it as usual!\xe2\x80\x9d The momentary vision of the bustling\r\n      agency was displaced quickly by another picture.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMy God! She\xe2\x80\x99s wonderful!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Tom sighed.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Amory, \xe2\x80\x9cjust how wonderful she is. I\r\n      don\xe2\x80\x99t want you to know. Immediately Rosalind insisted that\r\n      Howard should climb up with her to see what it looked like.\r\n\r\n      A minute later, as he sat and dangled his feet on the edge, a\r\n      form shot by him; Rosalind, her arms spread in a beautiful swan\r\n      dive, had sailed through the air into the clear water.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOf course _I_ had to go, after that\xe2\x80\x94and I nearly killed myself.\r\n      I thought I was pretty good to even try it. Nobody else in the\r\n      party tried it. Well, afterward Rosalind had the nerve to ask me\r\n      why I stooped over when I dove. \xe2\x80\x98It didn\xe2\x80\x99t make it any easier,\xe2\x80\x99\r\n      she said, \xe2\x80\x98it just took all the courage out of it.\xe2\x80\x99 I ask you,\r\n      what can a man do with a girl like that? Unnecessary, I call it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Gillespie failed to understand why Amory was smiling delightedly\r\n      all through lunch. He thought perhaps he was one of these hollow\r\n      optimists.\r\n\r\n\r\n      FIVE WEEKS LATER\r\n\r\n      Again the library of the Connage house. I can\xe2\x80\x99t stand it\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n      AMORY: (His face drawn, his voice strained) Do you know what\r\n      you\xe2\x80\x99re saying? You\xe2\x80\x99re afraid of taking\r\n      two years\xe2\x80\x99 knocks with me.\r\n\r\n      ROSALIND: I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be the Rosalind you love.\r\n\r\n      AMORY: (A little hysterically) I can\xe2\x80\x99t give you up! I can\xe2\x80\x99t,\r\n      that\xe2\x80\x99s all! I\xe2\x80\x99ve got to have you!\r\n\r\n      ROSALIND: (A hard note in her voice) You\xe2\x80\x99re being a baby now.\r\n\r\n      AMORY: (Wildly) I don\xe2\x80\x99t care! You\xe2\x80\x99re spoiling our lives!\r\n\r\n      ROSALIND: I\xe2\x80\x99m doing the wise thing, the only thing.\r\n\r\n      AMORY: Are you going to marry Dawson Ryder?\r\n\r\n      ROSALIND: Oh, don\xe2\x80\x99t ask me. You know I\xe2\x80\x99m old in some ways\xe2\x80\x94in\r\n      others\xe2\x80\x94well, I\xe2\x80\x99m just a little girl. I like sunshine and pretty\r\n      things and cheerfulness\xe2\x80\x94and I dread responsibility. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\n      to think about pots and kitchens and brooms. I want to worry\r\n      whether my legs will get slick and brown when I swim in the\r\n      summer.\r\n\r\n      AMORY: And you love me.\r\n\r\n      ROSALIND: That\xe2\x80\x99s just why it has to end. Drifting hurts too much.\r\n      We can\xe2\x80\x99t have any more scenes like this.\r\n\r\n      (She draws his ring from her finger and hands it to him. Their\r\n      eyes blind again with tears.)\r\n\r\n      AMORY: (His lips against her wet cheek) Don\xe2\x80\x99t! Keep it,\r\n      please\xe2\x80\x94oh, don\xe2\x80\x99t break my heart!\r\n\r\n      (She presses the ring softly into his hand.)\r\n\r\n      ROSALIND: (Brokenly) You\xe2\x80\x99d better go.\r\n\r\n      AMORY: Good-by\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n      (She looks at him once more, with infinite longing, infinite\r\n      sadness.)\r\n\r\n      ROSALIND: Don\xe2\x80\x99t ever forget me, Amory\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n      AMORY: Good-by\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n      (He goes to the door, fumbles for the knob, finds it\xe2\x80\x94she sees him\r\n      throw back his head\xe2\x80\x94and he is gone. Gone\xe2\x80\x94she half starts from the\r\n      lounge and then sinks forward on her face into the pillows.)\r\n\r\n      ROSALIND: Oh, God, I want to die! (After a moment she rises and\r\n      with her eyes closed feels her way to the door. Then she turns\r\n      and looks once more at the room. Here they had sat and dreamed:\r\n      that tray she had so often filled with matches for him; that\r\n      shade that they had discreetly lowered one long Sunday afternoon.\r\n      Misty-eyed she stands and remembers; she speaks aloud.) Oh,\r\n      Amory, what have I done to you?\r\n\r\n      (And deep under the aching sadness that will pass in time,\r\n      Rosalind feels that she has lost something, she knows not what,\r\n      she knows not why.)\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n      CHAPTER 2. Experiments in Convalescence\r\n\r\n\r\n      The Knickerbocker Bar, beamed upon by Maxfield Parrish\xe2\x80\x99s jovial,\r\n      colorful \xe2\x80\x9cOld King Cole,\xe2\x80\x9d was well crowded. Amory stopped in the\r\n      entrance and looked at his wrist-watch; he wanted particularly to\r\n      know the time, for something in his mind that catalogued and\r\n      classified liked to chip things off cleanly. Later it would\r\n      satisfy him in a vague way to be able to think \xe2\x80\x9cthat thing ended\r\n      at exactly twenty minutes after eight on Thursday, June 10,\r\n      1919.\xe2\x80\x9d This was allowing for the walk from her house\xe2\x80\x94a walk\r\n      concerning which he had afterward not the faintest recollection.\r\n\r\n      He was in rather grotesque condition: two days of worry and\r\n      nervousness, of sleepless nights, of untouched meals, culminating\r\n      in the emotional crisis and Rosalind\xe2\x80\x99s abrupt decision\xe2\x80\x94the strain\r\n      of it had drugged the foreground of his mind into a merciful\r\n      coma. As he fumbled clumsily with the olives at the free-lunch\r\n      table, a man approached and spoke to him, and the olives dropped\r\n      from his nervous hands.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, Amory...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      It was some one he had known at Princeton; he had no idea of the\r\n      name.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHello, old boy\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he heard himself saying.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cName\xe2\x80\x99s Jim Wilson\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99ve forgotten.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSure, you bet, Jim. I remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGoing to reunion?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou know!\xe2\x80\x9d Simultaneously he realized that he was not going to\r\n      reunion.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGet overseas?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory nodded, his eyes staring oddly. Stepping back to let some\r\n      one pass, he knocked the dish of olives to a crash on the floor.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cToo bad,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered. \xe2\x80\x9cHave a drink?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Wilson, ponderously diplomatic, reached over and slapped him on\r\n      the back.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve had plenty, old boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory eyed him dumbly until Wilson grew embarrassed under the\r\n      scrutiny.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cPlenty, hell!\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory finally. \xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t had a drink\r\n      to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Wilson looked incredulous.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHave a drink or not?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Amory rudely.\r\n\r\n      Together they sought the bar.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cRye high.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll just take a Bronx.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Wilson had another; Amory had several more. They decided to sit\r\n      down. At ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock Wilson was displaced by Carling, class of\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9915. Amory, his head spinning gorgeously, layer upon layer of\r\n      soft satisfaction setting over the bruised spots of his spirit,\r\n      was discoursing volubly on the war.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99S a mental was\xe2\x80\x99e,\xe2\x80\x9d he insisted with owl-like wisdom. \xe2\x80\x9cTwo years\r\n      my life spent inalleshual vacuity. Los\xe2\x80\x99 idealism, got be physcal\r\n      anmal,\xe2\x80\x9d he shook his fist expressively at Old King Cole, \xe2\x80\x9cgot be\r\n      Prussian \xe2\x80\x99bout ev\xe2\x80\x99thing, women \xe2\x80\x99specially. Use\xe2\x80\x99 be straight \xe2\x80\x99bout\r\n      women college. Now don\xe2\x80\x99givadam.\xe2\x80\x9d He expressed his lack of\r\n      principle by sweeping a seltzer bottle with a broad gesture to\r\n      noisy extinction on the floor, but this did not interrupt his\r\n      speech. \xe2\x80\x9cSeek pleasure where find it for to-morrow die. \xe2\x80\x99At\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      philos\xe2\x80\x99phy for me now on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Carling yawned, but Amory, waxing brilliant, continued:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cUse\xe2\x80\x99 wonder \xe2\x80\x99bout things\xe2\x80\x94people satisfied compromise,\r\n      fif\xe2\x80\x99y-fif\xe2\x80\x99y att\xe2\x80\x99tude on life. Now don\xe2\x80\x99 wonder, don\xe2\x80\x99 wonder\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\n      became so emphatic in impressing on Carling the fact that he\r\n      didn\xe2\x80\x99t wonder that he lost the thread of his discourse and\r\n      concluded by announcing to the bar at large that he was a\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cphyscal anmal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you celebrating, Amory?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory leaned forward confidentially.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCel\xe2\x80\x99brating blowmylife. Great moment blow my life. Can\xe2\x80\x99t tell\r\n      you \xe2\x80\x99bout it\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He heard Carling addressing a remark to the bartender:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGive him a bromo-seltzer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory shook his head indignantly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNone that stuff!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBut listen, Amory, you\xe2\x80\x99re making yourself sick. You\xe2\x80\x99re white as\r\n      a ghost.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory considered the question. He tried to look at himself in the\r\n      mirror but even by squinting up one eye could only see as far as\r\n      the row of bottles behind the bar.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLike som\xe2\x80\x99n solid. We go get some\xe2\x80\x94some salad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He settled his coat with an attempt at nonchalance, but letting\r\n      go of the bar was too much for him, and he slumped against a\r\n      chair.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll go over to Shanley\xe2\x80\x99s,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Carling, offering an\r\n      elbow.\r\n\r\n      With this assistance Amory managed to get his legs in motion\r\n      enough to propel him across Forty-second Street.\r\n\r\n      Shanley\xe2\x80\x99s was very dim. He was conscious that he was talking in a\r\n      loud voice, very succinctly and convincingly, he thought, about a\r\n      desire to crush people under his heel. He consumed three club\r\n      sandwiches, devouring each as though it were no larger than a\r\n      chocolate-drop. Then Rosalind began popping into his mind again,\r\n      and he found his lips forming her name over and over. Next he was\r\n      sleepy, and he had a hazy, listless sense of people in dress\r\n      suits, probably waiters, gathering around the table....\r\n\r\n      ... He was in a room and Carling was saying something about a\r\n      knot in his shoe-lace.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNemmine,\xe2\x80\x9d he managed to articulate drowsily. \xe2\x80\x9cSleep in \xe2\x80\x99em....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n      STILL ALCOHOLIC\r\n\r\n      He awoke laughing and his eyes lazily roamed his surroundings,\r\n      evidently a bedroom and bath in a good hotel. His head was\r\n      whirring and picture after picture was forming and blurring and\r\n      melting before his eyes, but beyond the desire to laugh he had no\r\n      entirely conscious reaction. He reached for the \xe2\x80\x99phone beside his\r\n      bed.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHello\xe2\x80\x94what hotel is this\xe2\x80\x94?\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cKnickerbocker? All right, send up two rye high-balls\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He lay for a moment and wondered idly whether they\xe2\x80\x99d send up a\r\n      bottle or just two of those little glass containers. Then, with\r\n      an effort, he struggled out of bed and ambled into the bathroom.\r\n\r\n      When he emerged, rubbing himself lazily with a towel, he found\r\n      the bar boy with the drinks and had a sudden desire to kid him.\r\n      On reflection he decided that this would be undignified, so he\r\n      waved him away.\r\n\r\n      As the new alcohol tumbled into his stomach and warmed him, the\r\n      isolated pictures began slowly to form a cinema reel of the day\r\n      before. Again he saw Rosalind curled weeping among the pillows,\r\n      again he felt her tears against his cheek. Her words began\r\n      ringing in his ears: \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t ever forget me, Amory\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t ever\r\n      forget me\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHell!\xe2\x80\x9d he faltered aloud, and then he choked and collapsed on\r\n      the bed in a shaken spasm of grief. After a minute he opened his\r\n      eyes and regarded the ceiling.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDamned fool!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed in disgust, and with a voluminous\r\n      sigh rose and approached the bottle. After another glass he gave\r\n      way loosely to the luxury of tears. Purposely he called up into\r\n      his mind little incidents of the vanished spring, phrased to\r\n      himself emotions that would make him react even more strongly to\r\n      sorrow.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWe were so happy,\xe2\x80\x9d he intoned dramatically, \xe2\x80\x9cso very happy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      Then he gave way again and knelt beside the bed, his head\r\n      half-buried in the pillow.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMy own girl\xe2\x80\x94my own\xe2\x80\x94Oh\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He clinched his teeth so that the tears streamed in a flood from\r\n      his eyes.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh... my baby girl, all I had, all I wanted!... Oh, my girl,\r\n      come back, come back! I need you... need you... we\xe2\x80\x99re so pitiful\r\n      ... just misery we brought each other.... She\xe2\x80\x99ll be shut away\r\n      from me.... I can\xe2\x80\x99t see her; I can\xe2\x80\x99t be her friend. It\xe2\x80\x99s got to\r\n      be that way\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s got to be\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      And then again:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ve been so happy, so very happy....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He rose to his feet and threw himself on the bed in an ecstasy of\r\n      sentiment, and then lay exhausted while he realized slowly that\r\n      he had been very drunk the night before, and that his head was\r\n      spinning again wildly. He laughed, rose, and crossed again to\r\n      Lethe....\r\n\r\n      At noon he ran into a crowd in the Biltmore bar, and the riot\r\n      began again. He had a vague recollection afterward of discussing\r\n      French poetry with a British officer who was introduced to him as\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCaptain Corn, of his Majesty\xe2\x80\x99s Foot,\xe2\x80\x9d and he remembered\r\n      attempting to recite \xe2\x80\x9cClair de Lune\xe2\x80\x9d at luncheon; then he slept\r\n      in a big, soft chair until almost five o\xe2\x80\x99clock when another crowd\r\n      found and woke him; there followed an alcoholic dressing of\r\n      several temperaments for the ordeal of dinner. They selected\r\n      theatre tickets at Tyson\xe2\x80\x99s for a play that had a four-drink\r\n      programme\xe2\x80\x94a play with two monotonous voices, with turbid, gloomy\r\n      scenes, and lighting effects that were hard to follow when his\r\n      eyes behaved so amazingly. He imagined afterward that it must\r\n      have been \xe2\x80\x9cThe Jest.\xe2\x80\x9d...\r\n\r\n      ... Then the Cocoanut Grove, where Amory slept again on a little\r\n      balcony outside. Out in Shanley\xe2\x80\x99s, Yonkers, he became almost\r\n      logical, and by a careful control of the number of high-balls he\r\n      drank, grew quite lucid and garrulous. He found that the party\r\n      consisted of five men, two of whom he knew slightly; he became\r\n      righteous about paying his share of the expense and insisted in a\r\n      loud voice on arranging everything then and there to the\r\n      amusement of the tables around him....\r\n\r\n      Some one mentioned that a famous cabaret star was at the next\r\n      table, so Amory rose and, approaching gallantly, introduced\r\n      himself... this involved him in an argument, first with her\r\n      escort and then with the headwaiter\xe2\x80\x94Amory\xe2\x80\x99s attitude being a\r\n      lofty and exaggerated courtesy... he consented, after being\r\n      confronted with irrefutable logic, to being led back to his own\r\n      table.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDecided to commit suicide,\xe2\x80\x9d he announced suddenly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhen? Next year?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNow. To-morrow morning. Going to take a room at the Commodore,\r\n      get into a hot bath and open a vein.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s getting morbid!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou need another rye, old boy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll all talk it over to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      But Amory was not to be dissuaded, from argument at least.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDid you ever get that way?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded confidentially\r\n      fortaccio.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSure!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOften?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMy chronic state.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      This provoked discussion. One man said that he got so depressed\r\n      sometimes that he seriously considered it. Another agreed that\r\n      there was nothing to live for. \xe2\x80\x9cCaptain Corn,\xe2\x80\x9d who had somehow\r\n      rejoined the party, said that in his opinion it was when one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      health was bad that one felt that way most. Amory\xe2\x80\x99s suggestion\r\n      was that they should each order a Bronx, mix broken glass in it,\r\n      and drink it off. To his relief no one applauded the idea, so\r\n      having finished his high-ball, he balanced his chin in his hand\r\n      and his elbow on the table\xe2\x80\x94a most delicate, scarcely noticeable\r\n      sleeping position, he assured himself\xe2\x80\x94and went into a deep\r\n      stupor....\r\n\r\n      He was awakened by a woman clinging to him, a pretty woman, with\r\n      brown, disarranged hair and dark blue eyes.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cTake me home!\xe2\x80\x9d she cried.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHello!\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory, blinking.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI like you,\xe2\x80\x9d she announced tenderly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI like you too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He noticed that there was a noisy man in the background and that\r\n      one of his party was arguing with him.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cFella I was with\xe2\x80\x99s a damn fool,\xe2\x80\x9d confided the blue-eyed woman.\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI hate him. I want to go home with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou drunk?\xe2\x80\x9d queried Amory with intense wisdom.\r\n\r\n      She nodded coyly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGo home with him,\xe2\x80\x9d he advised gravely. \xe2\x80\x9cHe brought you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      At this point the noisy man in the background broke away from his\r\n      detainers and approached.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSay!\xe2\x80\x9d he said fiercely. \xe2\x80\x9cI brought this girl out here and you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\n      butting in!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory regarded him coldly, while the girl clung to him closer.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou let go that girl!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the noisy man.\r\n\r\n      Amory tried to make his eyes threatening.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou go to hell!\xe2\x80\x9d he directed finally, and turned his attention\r\n      to the girl.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLove first sight,\xe2\x80\x9d he suggested.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI love you,\xe2\x80\x9d she breathed and nestled close to him. For an instant a brawl\r\n      threatened, but a sleek waiter bent back Margaret Diamond\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      fingers until she released her hold on Amory, whereupon she\r\n      slapped the waiter furiously in the face and flung her arms about\r\n      her raging original escort.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Lord!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Amory.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s go!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCome on, the taxis are getting scarce!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCheck, waiter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cC\xe2\x80\x99mon, Amory. Your romance is over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory laughed.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t know how true you spoke. No idea. \xe2\x80\x99At\xe2\x80\x99s the whole\r\n      trouble.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n      AMORY ON THE LABOR QUESTION\r\n\r\n      Two mornings later he knocked at the president\xe2\x80\x99s door at Bascome\r\n      and Barlow\xe2\x80\x99s advertising agency.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCome in!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory entered unsteadily.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x99Morning, Mr. Barlow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Mr. Barlow brought his glasses to the inspection and set his\r\n      mouth slightly ajar that he might better listen.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, Mr. Blaine. We haven\xe2\x80\x99t seen you for several days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m quitting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94well\xe2\x80\x94this is\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like it here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sorry. I thought our relations had been quite\xe2\x80\x94ah\xe2\x80\x94pleasant.\r\n      You seemed to be a hard worker\xe2\x80\x94a little inclined perhaps to write\r\n      fancy copy\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI just got tired of it,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Amory rudely. \xe2\x80\x9cIt didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      matter a damn to me whether Harebell\xe2\x80\x99s flour was any better than\r\n      any one else\xe2\x80\x99s. In fact, I never ate any of it. So I got tired of\r\n      telling people about it\xe2\x80\x94oh, I know I\xe2\x80\x99ve been drinking\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Mr. Barlow\xe2\x80\x99s face steeled by several ingots of expression.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou asked for a position\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory waved him to silence.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I think I was rottenly underpaid. Thirty-five dollars a\r\n      week\xe2\x80\x94less than a good carpenter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou had just started. You\xe2\x80\x99d never worked before,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\n      Barlow coolly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBut it took about ten thousand dollars to educate me where I\r\n      could write your darned stuff for you. I just wanted to tell you I\xe2\x80\x99m quitting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      They stood for a moment looking at each other impassively and\r\n      then Amory turned and left the office.\r\n\r\n\r\n      A LITTLE LULL\r\n\r\n      Four days after that he returned at last to the apartment. Tom\r\n      was engaged on a book review for The New Democracy on the staff\r\n      of which he was employed. They regarded each other for a moment\r\n      in silence.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGood Lord, Amory, where\xe2\x80\x99d you get the black eye\xe2\x80\x94and the jaw?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory laughed.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a mere nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He peeled off his coat and bared his shoulders.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLook here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Tom emitted a low whistle.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat hit you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory laughed again.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, a lot of people. I got beaten up. Fact.\xe2\x80\x9d He slowly replaced\r\n      his shirt. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was bound to come sooner or later and I wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      have missed it for anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWho was it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, there were some waiters and a couple of sailors and a few\r\n      stray pedestrians, I guess. It\xe2\x80\x99s the strangest feeling. You ought\r\n      to get beaten up just for the experience of it. You fall down\r\n      after a while and everybody sort of slashes in at you before you\r\n      hit the ground\xe2\x80\x94then they kick you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Tom lighted a cigarette.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI spent a day chasing you all over town, Amory. But you always\r\n      kept a little ahead of me. I\xe2\x80\x99d say you\xe2\x80\x99ve been on some party.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory tumbled into a chair and asked for a cigarette.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou sober now?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Tom quizzically.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cPretty sober. Why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, Alec has left. His family had been after him to go home\r\n      and live, so he\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      A spasm of pain shook Amory.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cToo bad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes, it is too bad. We\xe2\x80\x99ll have to get some one else if we\xe2\x80\x99re\r\n      going to stay here. The rent\xe2\x80\x99s going up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSure. Get anybody. I\xe2\x80\x99ll leave it to you, Tom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory walked into his bedroom. The first thing that met his\r\n      glance was a photograph of Rosalind that he had intended to have\r\n      framed, propped up against a mirror on his dresser. He looked at\r\n      it unmoved. After the vivid mental pictures of her that were his\r\n      portion at present, the portrait was curiously unreal. He went\r\n      back into the study.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGot a cardboard box?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Tom, puzzled. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy should I have? Oh, yes\xe2\x80\x94there\r\n      may be one in Alec\xe2\x80\x99s room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Eventually Amory found what he was looking for and, returning to\r\n      his dresser, opened a drawer full of letters, notes, part of a\r\n      chain, two little handkerchiefs, and some snap-shots. As he\r\n      transferred them carefully to the box his mind wandered to some\r\n      place in a book where the hero, after preserving for a year a\r\n      cake of his lost love\xe2\x80\x99s soap, finally washed his hands with it.\r\n      He laughed and began to hum \xe2\x80\x9cAfter you\xe2\x80\x99ve gone\xe2\x80\x9d ... ceased\r\n      abruptly...\r\n\r\n      The string broke twice, and then he managed to secure it, dropped\r\n      the package into the bottom of his trunk, and having slammed the\r\n      lid returned to the study.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGoing out?\xe2\x80\x9d Tom\xe2\x80\x99s voice held an undertone of anxiety.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cUh-huh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say, old keed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s have dinner together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSorry. I told Sukey Brett I\xe2\x80\x99d eat with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBy-by.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory crossed the street and had a high-ball; then he walked to\r\n      Washington Square and found a top seat on a bus. He disembarked\r\n      at Forty-third Street and strolled to the Biltmore bar.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHi, Amory!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99ll you have?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYo-ho! Waiter!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n      TEMPERATURE NORMAL\r\n\r\n      The advent of prohibition with the \xe2\x80\x9cthirsty-first\xe2\x80\x9d put a sudden\r\n      stop to the submerging of Amory\xe2\x80\x99s sorrows, and when he awoke one\r\n      morning to find that the old bar-to-bar days were over, he had\r\n      neither remorse for the past three weeks nor regret that their\r\n      repetition was impossible. He had taken the most violent, if the\r\n      weakest, method to shield himself from the stabs of memory, and\r\n      while it was not a course he would have prescribed for others, he\r\n      found in the end that it had done its business: he was over the\r\n      first flush of pain.\r\n\r\n      Don\xe2\x80\x99t misunderstand! Amory had loved Rosalind as he would never\r\n      love another living person. She had taken the first flush of his\r\n      youth and brought from his unplumbed depths tenderness that had\r\n      surprised him, gentleness and unselfishness that he had never\r\n      given to another creature. He had later love-affairs, but of a\r\n      different sort: in those he went back to that, perhaps, more\r\n      typical frame of mind, in which the girl became the mirror of a\r\n      mood in him. Rosalind had drawn out what was more than passionate\r\n      admiration; he had a deep, undying affection for Rosalind.\r\n\r\n      But there had been, near the end, so much dramatic tragedy,\r\n      culminating in the arabesque nightmare of his three weeks\xe2\x80\x99 spree,\r\n      that he was emotionally worn out. The people and surroundings\r\n      that he remembered as being cool or delicately artificial, seemed\r\n      to promise him a refuge. He wrote a cynical story which featured\r\n      his father\xe2\x80\x99s funeral and despatched it to a magazine, receiving\r\n      in return a check for sixty dollars and a request for more of the\r\n      same tone. This tickled his vanity, but inspired him to no\r\n      further effort.\r\n\r\n      He read enormously. He was puzzled and depressed by \xe2\x80\x9cA Portrait\r\n      of the Artist as a Young Man\xe2\x80\x9d; intensely interested by \xe2\x80\x9cJoan and\r\n      Peter\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cThe Undying Fire,\xe2\x80\x9d and rather surprised by his\r\n      discovery through a critic named Mencken of several excellent\r\n      American novels: \xe2\x80\x9cVandover and the Brute,\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cThe Damnation of\r\n      Theron Ware,\xe2\x80\x9d and \xe2\x80\x9cJennie Gerhardt.\xe2\x80\x9d Mackenzie, Chesterton,\r\n      Galsworthy, Bennett, had sunk in his appreciation from sagacious,\r\n      life-saturated geniuses to merely diverting contemporaries.\r\n      Shaw\xe2\x80\x99s aloof clarity and brilliant consistency and the gloriously\r\n      intoxicated efforts of H. G. Wells to fit the key of romantic\r\n      symmetry into the elusive lock of truth, alone won his rapt\r\n      attention.\r\n\r\n      He wanted to see Monsignor Darcy, to whom he had written when he\r\n      landed, but he had not heard from him; besides he knew that a\r\n      visit to Monsignor would entail the story of Rosalind, and the\r\n      thought of repeating it turned him cold with horror.\r\n\r\n      In his search for cool people he remembered Mrs. Lawrence, a very\r\n      intelligent, very dignified lady, a convert to the church, and a\r\n      great devotee of Monsignor\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\n      He called her on the \xe2\x80\x99phone one day. Yes, she remembered him\r\n      perfectly; no, Monsignor wasn\xe2\x80\x99t in town, was in Boston she\r\n      thought; he\xe2\x80\x99d promised to come to dinner when he returned.\r\n      Couldn\xe2\x80\x99t Amory take luncheon with her?\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI thought I\xe2\x80\x99d better catch up, Mrs. Lawrence,\xe2\x80\x9d he said rather\r\n      ambiguously when he arrived.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMonsignor was here just last week,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Lawrence\r\n      regretfully. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was very anxious to see you, but he\xe2\x80\x99d left your\r\n      address at home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDid he think I\xe2\x80\x99d plunged into Bolshevism?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Amory,\r\n      interested.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, he\xe2\x80\x99s having a frightful time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAbout the Irish Republic. He thinks it lacks dignity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSo?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHe went to Boston when the Irish President arrived and he was\r\n      greatly distressed because the receiving committee, when they\r\n      rode in an automobile, _would_ put their arms around the\r\n      President.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t blame him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, what impressed you more than anything while you were in\r\n      the army? You look a great deal older.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s from another, more disastrous battle,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered,\r\n      smiling in spite of himself. \xe2\x80\x9cBut the army\xe2\x80\x94let me see\xe2\x80\x94well, I\r\n      discovered that physical courage depends to a great extent on the\r\n      physical shape a man is in. I found that I was as brave as the\r\n      next man\xe2\x80\x94it used to worry me before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat else?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, the idea that men can stand anything if they get used to\r\n      it, and the fact that I got a high mark in the psychological\r\n      examination.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Mrs. Lawrence laughed. Amory was finding it a great relief to be\r\n      in this cool house on Riverside Drive, away from more condensed\r\n      New York and the sense of people expelling great quantities of\r\n      breath into a little space.
