Mrs. Lawrence reminded him vaguely of\r\n      Beatrice, not in temperament, but in her perfect grace and\r\n      dignity. The house, its furnishings, the manner in which dinner\r\n      was served, were in immense contrast to what he had met in the\r\n      great places on Long Island, where the servants were so obtrusive\r\n      that they had positively to be bumped out of the way, or even in\r\n      the houses of more conservative \xe2\x80\x9cUnion Club\xe2\x80\x9d families. He\r\n      wondered if this air of symmetrical restraint, this grace, which\r\n      he felt was continental, was distilled through Mrs. Lawrence\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      New England ancestry or acquired in long residence in Italy and\r\n      Spain.\r\n\r\n      Two glasses of sauterne at luncheon loosened his tongue, and he\r\n      talked, with what he felt was something of his old charm, of\r\n      religion and literature and the menacing phenomena of the social\r\n      order. Mrs. Lawrence was ostensibly pleased with him, and her\r\n      interest was especially in his mind; he wanted people to like his\r\n      mind again\xe2\x80\x94after a while it might be such a nice place in which\r\n      to live.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMonsignor Darcy still thinks that you\xe2\x80\x99re his reincarnation, that\r\n      your faith will eventually clarify.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d he assented. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m rather pagan at present. It\xe2\x80\x99s just\r\n      that religion doesn\xe2\x80\x99t seem to have the slightest bearing on life\r\n      at my age.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      When he left her house he walked down Riverside Drive with a\r\n      feeling of satisfaction. It was amusing to discuss again such\r\n      subjects as this young poet, Stephen Vincent Benet, or the Irish\r\n      Republic. Between the rancid accusations of Edward Carson and\r\n      Justice Cohalan he had completely tired of the Irish question;\r\n      yet there had been a time when his own Celtic traits were pillars\r\n      of his personal philosophy.\r\n\r\n      There seemed suddenly to be much left in life, if only this\r\n      revival of old interests did not mean that he was backing away\r\n      from it again\xe2\x80\x94backing away from life itself.\r\n\r\n\r\n      RESTLESSNESS\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m tres old and tres bored, Tom,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory one day,\r\n      stretching himself at ease in the comfortable window-seat. He\r\n      always felt most natural in a recumbent position.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou used to be entertaining before you started to write,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\n      continued. \xe2\x80\x9cNow you save any idea that you think would do to\r\n      print.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Existence had settled back to an ambitionless normality. They had\r\n      decided that with economy they could still afford the apartment,\r\n      which Tom, with the domesticity of an elderly cat, had grown fond\r\n      of. The old English hunting prints on the wall were Tom\xe2\x80\x99s, and\r\n      the large tapestry by courtesy, a relic of decadent days in\r\n      college, and the great profusion of orphaned candlesticks and the\r\n      carved Louis XV chair in which no one could sit more than a\r\n      minute without acute spinal disorders\xe2\x80\x94Tom claimed that this was\r\n      because one was sitting in the lap of Montespan\xe2\x80\x99s wraith\xe2\x80\x94at any\r\n      rate, it was Tom\xe2\x80\x99s furniture that decided them to stay.\r\n\r\n      They went out very little: to an occasional play, or to dinner at\r\n      the Ritz or the Princeton Club. With prohibition the great\r\n      rendezvous had received their death wounds; no longer could one\r\n      wander to the Biltmore bar at twelve or five and find congenial\r\n      spirits, and both Tom and Amory had outgrown the passion for\r\n      dancing with mid-Western or New Jersey debbies at the\r\n      Club-de-Vingt (surnamed the \xe2\x80\x9cClub de Gink\xe2\x80\x9d) or the Plaza Rose\r\n      Room\xe2\x80\x94besides even that required several cocktails \xe2\x80\x9cto come down\r\n      to the intellectual level of the women present,\xe2\x80\x9d as Amory had\r\n      once put it to a horrified matron.\r\n\r\n      Amory had lately received several alarming letters from Mr.\r\n      Barton\xe2\x80\x94the Lake Geneva house was too large to be easily rented;\r\n      the best rent obtainable at present would serve this year to\r\n      little more than pay for the taxes and necessary improvements; in\r\n      fact, the lawyer suggested that the whole property was simply a\r\n      white elephant on Amory\xe2\x80\x99s hands. Nevertheless, even though it\r\n      might not yield a cent for the next three years, Amory decided\r\n      with a vague sentimentality that for the present, at any rate, he\r\n      would not sell the house.\r\n\r\n      This particular day on which he announced his ennui to Tom had\r\n      been quite typical. He had risen at noon, lunched with Mrs.\r\n      Lawrence, and then ridden abstractedly homeward atop one of his\r\n      beloved buses.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhy shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you be bored,\xe2\x80\x9d yawned Tom. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t that the\r\n      conventional frame of mind for the young man of your age and\r\n      condition?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory speculatively, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I\xe2\x80\x99m more than bored; I am\r\n      restless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLove and war did for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory considered, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not sure that the war itself had\r\n      any great effect on either you or me\xe2\x80\x94but it certainly ruined the\r\n      old backgrounds, sort of killed individualism out of our\r\n      generation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Tom looked up in surprise.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes it did,\xe2\x80\x9d insisted Amory. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not sure it didn\xe2\x80\x99t kill it out\r\n      of the whole world. Oh, Lord, what a pleasure it used to be to\r\n      dream I might be a really great dictator or writer or religious\r\n      or political leader\xe2\x80\x94and now even a Leonardo da Vinci or Lorenzo\r\n      de Medici couldn\xe2\x80\x99t be a real old-fashioned bolt in the world.\r\n      Life is too huge and complex. The world is so overgrown that it\r\n      can\xe2\x80\x99t lift its own fingers, and I was planning to be such an\r\n      important finger\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t agree with you,\xe2\x80\x9d Tom interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cThere never were men\r\n      placed in such egotistic positions since\xe2\x80\x94oh, since the French\r\n      Revolution.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory disagreed violently.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re mistaking this period when every nut is an individualist\r\n      for a period of individualism. Wilson has only been powerful when\r\n      he has represented; he\xe2\x80\x99s had to compromise over and over again.\r\n      Just as soon as Trotsky and Lenin take a definite, consistent\r\n      stand they\xe2\x80\x99ll become merely two-minute figures like Kerensky.\r\n      Even Foch hasn\xe2\x80\x99t half the significance of Stonewall Jackson. War\r\n      used to be the most individualistic pursuit of man, and yet the\r\n      popular heroes of the war had neither authority nor\r\n      responsibility: Guynemer and Sergeant York. How could a schoolboy\r\n      make a hero of Pershing? A big man has no time really to do\r\n      anything but just sit and be big.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThen you don\xe2\x80\x99t think there will be any more permanent world\r\n      heroes?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94in history\xe2\x80\x94not in life. Carlyle would have difficulty\r\n      getting material for a new chapter on \xe2\x80\x98The Hero as a Big Man.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGo on. I\xe2\x80\x99m a good listener to-day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cPeople try so hard to believe in leaders now, pitifully hard.\r\n      But we no sooner get a popular reformer or politician or soldier\r\n      or writer or philosopher\xe2\x80\x94a Roosevelt, a Tolstoi, a Wood, a Shaw,\r\n      a Nietzsche, than the cross-currents of criticism wash him away.\r\n      My Lord, no man can stand prominence these days. It\xe2\x80\x99s the surest\r\n      path to obscurity. People get sick of hearing the same name over\r\n      and over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThen you blame it on the press?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAbsolutely. Look at you; you\xe2\x80\x99re on The New Democracy, considered\r\n      the most brilliant weekly in the country, read by the men who do\r\n      things and all that. What\xe2\x80\x99s your business? Why, to be as clever,\r\n      as interesting, and as brilliantly cynical as possible about\r\n      every man, doctrine, book, or policy that is assigned you to deal\r\n      with. The more strong lights, the more spiritual scandal you can\r\n      throw on the matter, the more money they pay you, the more the\r\n      people buy the issue. You, Tom d\xe2\x80\x99Invilliers, a blighted Shelley,\r\n      changing, shifting, clever, unscrupulous, represent the critical\r\n      consciousness of the race\xe2\x80\x94Oh, don\xe2\x80\x99t protest, I know the stuff. I\r\n      used to write book reviews in college; I considered it rare sport\r\n      to refer to the latest honest, conscientious effort to propound a\r\n      theory or a remedy as a \xe2\x80\x98welcome addition to our light summer\r\n      reading.\xe2\x80\x99 Come on now, admit it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Tom laughed, and Amory continued triumphantly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWe _want_ to believe. Young students try to believe in older\r\n      authors, constituents try to believe in their Congressmen,\r\n      countries try to believe in their statesmen, but they _can\xe2\x80\x99t_.\r\n      Too many voices, too much scattered, illogical, ill-considered\r\n      criticism. It\xe2\x80\x99s worse in the case of newspapers. Any rich,\r\n      unprogressive old party with that particularly grasping,\r\n      acquisitive form of mentality known as financial genius can own a\r\n      paper that is the intellectual meat and drink of thousands of\r\n      tired, hurried men, men too involved in the business of modern\r\n      living to swallow anything but predigested food. For two cents\r\n      the voter buys his politics, prejudices, and philosophy. A year\r\n      later there is a new political ring or a change in the paper\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      ownership, consequence: more confusion, more contradiction, a\r\n      sudden inrush of new ideas, their tempering, their distillation,\r\n      the reaction against them\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He paused only to get his breath.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd that is why I have sworn not to put pen to paper until my\r\n      ideas either clarify or depart entirely; I have quite enough sins\r\n      on my soul without putting dangerous, shallow epigrams into\r\n      people\xe2\x80\x99s heads; I might cause a poor, inoffensive capitalist to\r\n      have a vulgar liaison with a bomb, or get some innocent little\r\n      Bolshevik tangled up with a machine-gun bullet\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Tom was growing restless under this lampooning of his connection\r\n      with The New Democracy.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s all this got to do with your being bored?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory considered that it had much to do with it.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHow\xe2\x80\x99ll I fit in?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat am I for? To propagate the\r\n      race? According to the American novels we are led to believe that\r\n      the \xe2\x80\x98healthy American boy\xe2\x80\x99 from nineteen to twenty-five is an\r\n      entirely sexless animal. As a matter of fact, the healthier he is\r\n      the less that\xe2\x80\x99s true. The only alternative to letting it get you\r\n      is some violent interest. Well, the war is over; I believe too\r\n      much in the responsibilities of authorship to write just now; and\r\n      business, well, business speaks for itself. It has no connection\r\n      with anything in the world that I\xe2\x80\x99ve ever been interested in,\r\n      except a slim, utilitarian connection with economics. What I\xe2\x80\x99d\r\n      see of it, lost in a clerkship, for the next and best ten years\r\n      of my life would have the intellectual content of an industrial\r\n      movie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cTry fiction,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Tom.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cTrouble is I get distracted when I start to write stories\xe2\x80\x94get\r\n      afraid I\xe2\x80\x99m doing it instead of living\xe2\x80\x94get thinking maybe life is\r\n      waiting for me in the Japanese gardens at the Ritz or at Atlantic\r\n      City or on the lower East Side.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnyway,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, \xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t the vital urge. I wanted to be\r\n      a regular human being but the girl couldn\xe2\x80\x99t see it that way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll find another.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGod! Banish the thought. Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you tell me that \xe2\x80\x98if the girl\r\n      had been worth having she\xe2\x80\x99d have waited for you\xe2\x80\x99? No, sir, the\r\n      girl really worth having won\xe2\x80\x99t wait for anybody. If I thought\r\n      there\xe2\x80\x99d be another I\xe2\x80\x99d lose my remaining faith in human nature.\r\n      Maybe I\xe2\x80\x99ll play\xe2\x80\x94but Rosalind was the only girl in the wide world\r\n      that could have held me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d yawned Tom, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve played confidant a good hour by the\r\n      clock. Still, I\xe2\x80\x99m glad to see you\xe2\x80\x99re beginning to have violent\r\n      views again on something.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI am,\xe2\x80\x9d agreed Amory reluctantly. \xe2\x80\x9cYet when I see a happy family\r\n      it makes me sick at my stomach\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHappy families try to make people feel that way,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom\r\n      cynically.\r\n\r\n\r\n      TOM THE CENSOR\r\n\r\n      There were days when Amory listened. These were when Tom,\r\n      wreathed in smoke, indulged in the slaughter of American\r\n      literature. Words failed him.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cFifty thousand dollars a year,\xe2\x80\x9d he would cry. \xe2\x80\x9cMy God! Look at\r\n      them, look at them\xe2\x80\x94Edna Ferber, Gouverneur Morris, Fanny Hurst,\r\n      Mary Roberts Rinehart\xe2\x80\x94not producing among \xe2\x80\x99em one story or novel\r\n      that will last ten years. This man Cobb\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t tink he\xe2\x80\x99s either\r\n      clever or amusing\xe2\x80\x94and what\xe2\x80\x99s more, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think very many people\r\n      do, except the editors. He\xe2\x80\x99s just groggy with advertising. And\xe2\x80\x94oh\r\n      Harold Bell Wright oh Zane Grey\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThey try.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo, they don\xe2\x80\x99t even try. Some of them _can_ write, but they\r\n      won\xe2\x80\x99t sit down and do one honest novel. Most of them _can\xe2\x80\x99t_\r\n      write, I\xe2\x80\x99ll admit. I believe Rupert Hughes tries to give a real,\r\n      comprehensive picture of American life, but his style and\r\n      perspective are barbarous. Ernest Poole and Dorothy Canfield try\r\n      but they\xe2\x80\x99re hindered by their absolute lack of any sense of\r\n      humor; but at least they crowd their work instead of spreading it\r\n      thin. Every author ought to write every book as if he were going\r\n      to be beheaded the day he finished it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIs that double entente?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t slow me up! Now there\xe2\x80\x99s a few of \xe2\x80\x99em that seem to have\r\n      some cultural background, some intelligence and a good deal of\r\n      literary felicity but they just simply won\xe2\x80\x99t write honestly;\r\n      they\xe2\x80\x99d all claim there was no public for good stuff. Then why the\r\n      devil is it that Wells, Conrad, Galsworthy, Shaw, Bennett, and\r\n      the rest depend on America for over half their sales?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHow does little Tommy like the poets?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Tom was overcome. He dropped his arms until they swung loosely\r\n      beside the chair and emitted faint grunts.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m writing a satire on \xe2\x80\x99em now, calling it \xe2\x80\x98Boston Bards and\r\n      Hearst Reviewers.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s hear it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory eagerly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve only got the last few lines done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s very modern. Let\xe2\x80\x99s hear \xe2\x80\x99em, if they\xe2\x80\x99re funny.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Tom produced a folded paper from his pocket and read aloud,\r\n      pausing at intervals so that Amory could see that it was free\r\n      verse:\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cSo Walter Arensberg, Alfred Kreymborg, Carl Sandburg, Louis\r\n   Untermeyer, Eunice Tietjens, Clara Shanafelt, James Oppenheim,\r\n   Maxwell Bodenheim, Richard Glaenzer, Scharmel Iris, Conrad Aiken, I\r\n   place your names here So that you may live If only as names,\r\n   Sinuous, mauve-colored names, In the Juvenalia Of my collected\r\n   editions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory roared.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou win the iron pansy. I\xe2\x80\x99ll buy you a meal on the arrogance of\r\n      the last two lines.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory did not entirely agree with Tom\xe2\x80\x99s sweeping damnation of\r\n      American novelists and poets. He enjoyed both Vachel Lindsay and\r\n      Booth Tarkington, and admired the conscientious, if slender,\r\n      artistry of Edgar Lee Masters.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat I hate is this idiotic drivel about \xe2\x80\x98I am God\xe2\x80\x94I am man\xe2\x80\x94I\r\n      ride the winds\xe2\x80\x94I look through the smoke\xe2\x80\x94I am the life sense.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s ghastly!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I wish American novelists would give up trying to make\r\n      business romantically interesting. Nobody wants to read about it,\r\n      unless it\xe2\x80\x99s crooked business. If it was an entertaining subject\r\n      they\xe2\x80\x99d buy the life of James J. Hill and not one of these long\r\n      office tragedies that harp along on the significance of smoke\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd gloom,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s another favorite, though I\xe2\x80\x99ll\r\n      admit the Russians have the monopoly. Our specialty is stories\r\n      about little girls who break their spines and get adopted by\r\n      grouchy old men because they smile so much. You\xe2\x80\x99d think we were a\r\n      race of cheerful cripples and that the common end of the Russian\r\n      peasant was suicide\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSix o\xe2\x80\x99clock,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory, glancing at his wrist-watch. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll buy\r\n      you a grea\xe2\x80\x99 big dinner on the strength of the Juvenalia of your\r\n      collected editions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n      LOOKING BACKWARD\r\n\r\n      July sweltered out with a last hot week, and Amory in another\r\n      surge of unrest realized that it was just five months since he\r\n      and Rosalind had met. Yet it was already hard for him to\r\n      visualize the heart-whole boy who had stepped off the transport,\r\n      passionately desiring the adventure of life. One night while the\r\n      heat, overpowering and enervating, poured into the windows of his\r\n      room he struggled for several hours in a vague effort to\r\n      immortalize the poignancy of that time.\r\n\r\n  The February streets, wind-washed by night, blow full of strange\r\n  half-intermittent damps, bearing on wasted walks in shining sight wet\r\n  snow plashed into gleams under the lamps, like golden oil from some\r\n  divine machine, in an hour of thaw and stars.\r\n  Strange damps\xe2\x80\x94full of the eyes of many men, crowded with life borne\r\n  in upon a lull....  Oh, I was young, for I could turn again to you,\r\n  most finite and most beautiful, and taste the stuff of\r\n  half-remembered dreams, sweet and new on your mouth.\r\n ... There was a tanging in the midnight air\xe2\x80\x94silence was dead and sound\r\n not yet awoken\xe2\x80\x94Life cracked like ice!\xe2\x80\x94one brilliant note and there,\r\n radiant and pale, you stood... and spring had broken. (The icicles\r\n were short upon the roofs and the changeling city swooned.)\r\n  Our thoughts were frosty mist along the eaves; our two ghosts kissed,\r\n  high on the long, mazed wires\xe2\x80\x94eerie half-laughter echoes here and\r\n  leaves only a fatuous sigh for young desires; regret has followed\r\n  after things she loved, leaving the great husk.\r\n\r\n\r\n      ANOTHER ENDING\r\n\r\n      In mid-August came a letter from Monsignor Darcy, who had\r\n      evidently just stumbled on his address:\r\n\r\n      MY DEAR BOY:\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n      Your last letter was quite enough to make me worry about you. It\r\n      was not a bit like yourself. Reading between the lines I should\r\n      imagine that your engagement to this girl is making you rather\r\n      unhappy, and I see you have lost all the feeling of romance that\r\n      you had before the war. You make a great mistake if you think you\r\n      can be romantic without religion. Sometimes I think that with\r\n      both of us the secret of success, when we find it, is the\r\n      mystical element in us: something flows into us that enlarges our\r\n      personalities, and when it ebbs out our personalities shrink; I\r\n      should call your last two letters rather shrivelled. Beware of\r\n      losing yourself in the personality of another being, man or\r\n      woman.\r\n\r\n      His Eminence Cardinal O\xe2\x80\x99Neill and the Bishop of Boston are\r\n      staying with me at present, so it is hard for me to get a moment\r\n      to write, but I wish you would come up here later if only for a\r\n      week-end. I go to Washington this week.\r\n\r\n      What I shall do in the future is hanging in the balance.\r\n      Absolutely between ourselves I should not be surprised to see the\r\n      red hat of a cardinal descend upon my unworthy head within the\r\n      next eight months. In any event, I should like to have a house in\r\n      New York or Washington where you could drop in for week-ends.\r\n\r\n      Amory, I\xe2\x80\x99m very glad we\xe2\x80\x99re both alive; this war could easily have\r\n      been the end of a brilliant family. But in regard to matrimony,\r\n      you are now at the most dangerous period of your life. You might\r\n      marry in haste and repent at leisure, but I think you won\xe2\x80\x99t. From\r\n      what you write me about the present calamitous state of your\r\n      finances, what you want is naturally impossible. However, if I\r\n      judge you by the means I usually choose, I should say that there\r\n      will be something of an emotional crisis within the next year.\r\n\r\n      Do write me. I feel annoyingly out of date on you.\r\n\r\n          With greatest affection,\r\n                              THAYER DARCY.\r\n\r\n      Within a week after the receipt of this letter their little\r\n      household fell precipitously to pieces. The immediate cause was\r\n      the serious and probably chronic illness of Tom\xe2\x80\x99s mother. So they\r\n      stored the furniture, gave instructions to sublet and shook hands\r\n      gloomily in the Pennsylvania Station. Amory and Tom seemed always\r\n      to be saying good-by.\r\n\r\n      Feeling very much alone, Amory yielded to an impulse and set off\r\n      southward, intending to join Monsignor in Washington. They missed\r\n      connections by two hours, and, deciding to spend a few days with\r\n      an ancient, remembered uncle, Amory journeyed up through the\r\n      luxuriant fields of Maryland into Ramilly County. But instead of\r\n      two days his stay lasted from mid-August nearly through\r\n      September, for in Maryland he met Eleanor.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n      CHAPTER 3. Young Irony\r\n\r\n\r\n      For years afterward when Amory thought of Eleanor he seemed still\r\n      to hear the wind sobbing around him and sending little chills\r\n      into the places beside his heart. The night when they rode up the\r\n      slope and watched the cold moon float through the clouds, he lost\r\n      a further part of him that nothing could restore; and when he\r\n      lost it he lost also the power of regretting it. Eleanor was,\r\n      say, the last time that evil crept close to Amory under the mask\r\n      of beauty, the last weird mystery that held him with wild\r\n      fascination and pounded his soul to flakes.\r\n\r\n      With her his imagination ran riot and that is why they rode to\r\n      the highest hill and watched an evil moon ride high, for they\r\n      knew then that they could see the devil in each other. But\r\n      Eleanor\xe2\x80\x94did Amory dream her? Afterward their ghosts played, yet\r\n      both of them hoped from their souls never to meet. Was it the\r\n      infinite sadness of her eyes that drew him or the mirror of\r\n      himself that he found in the gorgeous clarity of her mind? She\r\n      will have no other adventure like Amory, and if she reads this\r\n      she will say:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd Amory will have no other adventure like me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Nor will she sigh, any more than he would sigh.\r\n\r\n      Eleanor tried to put it on paper once:\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cThe fading things we only know We\xe2\x80\x99ll have forgotten... Put away...\r\n   Desires that melted with the snow, And dreams begotten This to-day:\r\n   The sudden dawns we laughed to greet, That all could see, that none\r\n   could share, Will be but dawns... and if we meet We shall not care.\r\n    Dear... not one tear will rise for this... A little while hence No\r\n    regret Will stir for a remembered kiss\xe2\x80\x94 Not even silence, When\r\n    we\xe2\x80\x99ve met, Will give old ghosts a waste to roam, Or stir the\r\n    surface of the sea... If gray shapes drift beneath the foam We\r\n    shall not see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      They quarrelled dangerously because Amory maintained that _sea_\r\n      and _see_ couldn\xe2\x80\x99t possibly be used as a rhyme. And then Eleanor\r\n      had part of another verse that she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t find a beginning for:\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9c... But wisdom passes... still the years Will feed us wisdom.... \r\n   Age will go Back to the old\xe2\x80\x94 For all our tears We shall not know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Eleanor hated Maryland passionately. She belonged to the oldest\r\n      of the old families of Ramilly County and lived in a big, gloomy\r\n      house with her grandfather. She had been born and brought up in\r\n      France.... I see I am starting wrong. Let me begin again.\r\n\r\n      Amory was bored, as he usually was in the country. He used to go\r\n      for far walks by himself\xe2\x80\x94and wander along reciting \xe2\x80\x9cUlalume\xe2\x80\x9d to\r\n      the corn-fields, and congratulating Poe for drinking himself to\r\n      death in that atmosphere of smiling complacency. One afternoon he\r\n      had strolled for several miles along a road that was new to him,\r\n      and then through a wood on bad advice from a colored woman...\r\n      losing himself entirely. A passing storm decided to break out,\r\n      and to his great impatience the sky grew black as pitch and the\r\n      rain began to splatter down through the trees, become suddenly\r\n      furtive and ghostly. Thunder rolled with menacing crashes up the\r\n      valley and scattered through the woods in intermittent batteries.\r\n      He stumbled blindly on, hunting for a way out, and finally,\r\n      through webs of twisted branches, caught sight of a rift in the\r\n      trees where the unbroken lightning showed open country. He rushed\r\n      to the edge of the woods and then hesitated whether or not to\r\n      cross the fields and try to reach the shelter of the little house\r\n      marked by a light far down the valley. It was only half past\r\n      five, but he could see scarcely ten steps before him, except when\r\n      the lightning made everything vivid and grotesque for great\r\n      sweeps around.\r\n\r\n      Suddenly a strange sound fell on his ears. It was a song, in a\r\n      low, husky voice, a girl\xe2\x80\x99s voice, and whoever was singing was\r\n      very close to him. A year before he might have laughed, or\r\n      trembled; but in his restless mood he only stood and listened\r\n      while the words sank into his consciousness:\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cLes sanglots longs Des violons De l\xe2\x80\x99automne Blessent mon coeur\r\n   D\xe2\x80\x99une langueur Monotone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The lightning split the sky, but the song went on without a\r\n      quaver. The girl was evidently in the field and the voice seemed\r\n      to come vaguely from a haystack about twenty feet in front of\r\n      him.\r\n\r\n      Then it ceased: ceased and began again in a weird chant that\r\n      soared and hung and fell and blended with the rain:\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cTout suffocant Et bleme quand Sonne l\xe2\x80\x99heure Je me souviens Des\r\n   jours anciens Et je pleure....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWho the devil is there in Ramilly County,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Amory aloud,\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cwho would deliver Verlaine in an extemporaneous tune to a\r\n      soaking haystack?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSomebody\xe2\x80\x99s there!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the voice unalarmed. \xe2\x80\x9cWho are\r\n      you?\xe2\x80\x94Manfred, St. Christopher, or Queen Victoria?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m Don Juan!\xe2\x80\x9d Amory shouted on impulse, raising his voice above\r\n      the noise of the rain and the wind.\r\n\r\n      A delighted shriek came from the haystack.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI know who you are\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99re the blond boy that likes \xe2\x80\x98Ulalume\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94I\r\n      recognize your voice.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHow do I get up?\xe2\x80\x9d he cried from the foot of the haystack,\r\n      whither he had arrived, dripping wet. A head appeared over the\r\n      edge\xe2\x80\x94it was so dark that Amory could just make out a patch of\r\n      damp hair and two eyes that gleamed like a cat\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cRun back!\xe2\x80\x9d came the voice, \xe2\x80\x9cand jump and I\xe2\x80\x99ll catch your\r\n      hand\xe2\x80\x94no, not there\xe2\x80\x94on the other side.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He followed directions and as he sprawled up the side, knee-deep\r\n      in hay, a small, white hand reached out, gripped his, and helped\r\n      him onto the top.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHere you are, Juan,\xe2\x80\x9d cried she of the damp hair. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you mind if\r\n      I drop the Don?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve got a thumb like mine!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you\xe2\x80\x99re holding my hand, which is dangerous without seeing my\r\n      face.\xe2\x80\x9d He dropped it quickly.\r\n\r\n      As if in answer to his prayers came a flash of lightning and he\r\n      looked eagerly at her who stood beside him on the soggy haystack,\r\n      ten feet above the ground. But she had covered her face and he\r\n      saw nothing but a slender figure, dark, damp, bobbed hair, and\r\n      the small white hands with the thumbs that bent back like his.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSit down,\xe2\x80\x9d she suggested politely, as the dark closed in on\r\n      them. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you\xe2\x80\x99ll sit opposite me in this hollow you can have half\r\n      of the raincoat, which I was using as a water-proof tent until\r\n      you so rudely interrupted me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI was asked,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory said joyfully; \xe2\x80\x9cyou asked me\xe2\x80\x94you know you\r\n      did.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon Juan always manages that,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cbut I shan\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      call you that any more, because you\xe2\x80\x99ve got reddish hair. Instead\r\n      you can recite \xe2\x80\x98Ulalume\xe2\x80\x99 and I\xe2\x80\x99ll be Psyche, your soul.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory flushed, happily invisible under the curtain of wind and\r\n      rain. They were sitting opposite each other in a slight hollow in\r\n      the hay with the raincoat spread over most of them, and the rain\r\n      doing for the rest. Amory was trying desperately to see Psyche,\r\n      but the lightning refused to flash again, and he waited\r\n      impatiently. Good Lord! supposing she wasn\xe2\x80\x99t beautiful\xe2\x80\x94supposing\r\n      she was forty and pedantic\xe2\x80\x94heavens! Suppose, only suppose, she\r\n      was mad. But he knew the last was unworthy. Here had Providence\r\n      sent a girl to amuse him just as it sent Benvenuto Cellini men to\r\n      murder, and he was wondering if she was mad, just because she\r\n      exactly filled his mood.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNot what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNot mad. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think you were mad when I first saw you, so it\r\n      isn\xe2\x80\x99t fair that you should think so of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHow on earth\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      As long as they knew each other Eleanor and Amory could be \xe2\x80\x9con a\r\n      subject\xe2\x80\x9d and stop talking with the definite thought of it in\r\n      their heads, yet ten minutes later speak aloud and find that\r\n      their minds had followed the same channels and led them each to a\r\n      parallel idea, an idea that others would have found absolutely\r\n      unconnected with the first.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cTell me,\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded, leaning forward eagerly, \xe2\x80\x9chow do you know\r\n      about \xe2\x80\x98Ulalume\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94how did you know the color of my hair? What\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      your name? What were you doing here? Tell me all at once!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Suddenly the lightning flashed in with a leap of overreaching\r\n      light and he saw Eleanor, and looked for the first time into\r\n      those eyes of hers. Oh, she was magnificent\xe2\x80\x94pale skin, the color\r\n      of marble in starlight, slender brows, and eyes that glittered\r\n      green as emeralds in the blinding glare. She was a witch, of\r\n      perhaps nineteen, he judged, alert and dreamy and with the\r\n      tell-tale white line over her upper lip that was a weakness and a\r\n      delight. He sank back with a gasp against the wall of hay.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNow you\xe2\x80\x99ve seen me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said calmly, \xe2\x80\x9cand I suppose you\xe2\x80\x99re\r\n      about to say that my green eyes are burning into your brain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat color is your hair?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked intently. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s bobbed, isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s bobbed. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what color it is,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered,\r\n      musing, \xe2\x80\x9cso many men have asked me. It\xe2\x80\x99s medium, I suppose\xe2\x80\x94No one\r\n      ever looks long at my hair. I\xe2\x80\x99ve got beautiful eyes, though,\r\n      haven\xe2\x80\x99t I. I don\xe2\x80\x99t care what you say, I have beautiful eyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnswer my question, Madeline.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t remember them all\xe2\x80\x94besides my name isn\xe2\x80\x99t Madeline, it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      Eleanor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI might have guessed it. You _look_ like Eleanor\xe2\x80\x94you have that\r\n      Eleanor look. You know what I mean.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      There was a silence as they listened to the rain.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s going down my neck, fellow lunatic,\xe2\x80\x9d she offered finally.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnswer my questions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94name of Savage, Eleanor; live in big old house mile down\r\n      road; nearest living relation to be notified, grandfather\xe2\x80\x94Ramilly\r\n      Savage; height, five feet four inches; number on watch-case, 3077\r\n      W; nose, delicate aquiline; temperament, uncanny\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd me,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory interrupted, \xe2\x80\x9cwhere did you see me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, you\xe2\x80\x99re one of _those_ men,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered haughtily, \xe2\x80\x9cmust\r\n      lug old self into conversation. Well, my boy, I was behind a\r\n      hedge sunning myself one day last week, and along comes a man\r\n      saying in a pleasant, conceited way of talking:\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98And now when the night was senescent\xe2\x80\x99 (says he) \xe2\x80\x98And the star\r\n    dials pointed to morn At the end of the path a liquescent\xe2\x80\x99 (says\r\n    he) \xe2\x80\x98And nebulous lustre was born.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSo I poked my eyes up over the hedge, but you had started to\r\n      run, for some unknown reason, and so I saw but the back of your\r\n      beautiful head. \xe2\x80\x98Oh!\xe2\x80\x99 says I, \xe2\x80\x98there\xe2\x80\x99s a man for whom many of us\r\n      might sigh,\xe2\x80\x99 and I continued in my best Irish\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAll right,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory interrupted. \xe2\x80\x9cNow go back to yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I will. I\xe2\x80\x99m one of those people who go through the world\r\n      giving other people thrills, but getting few myself except those\r\n      I read into men on such nights as these. I have the social\r\n      courage to go on the stage, but not the energy; I haven\xe2\x80\x99t the\r\n      patience to write books; and I never met a man I\xe2\x80\x99d marry.\r\n      However, I\xe2\x80\x99m only eighteen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The storm was dying down softly and only the wind kept up its\r\n      ghostly surge and made the stack lean and gravely settle from\r\n      side to side. Amory was in a trance. He felt that every moment\r\n      was precious. He had never met a girl like this before\xe2\x80\x94she would\r\n      never seem quite the same again. He didn\xe2\x80\x99t at all feel like a\r\n      character in a play, the appropriate feeling in an unconventional\r\n      situation\xe2\x80\x94instead, he had a sense of coming home.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI have just made a great decision,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eleanor after another\r\n      pause, \xe2\x80\x9cand that is why I\xe2\x80\x99m here, to answer another of your\r\n      questions. I have just decided that I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe in\r\n      immortality.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cReally! how banal!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cFrightfully so,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, \xe2\x80\x9cbut depressing with a stale,\r\n      sickly depression, nevertheless. I came out here to get wet\xe2\x80\x94like\r\n      a wet hen; wet hens always have great clarity of mind,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\n      concluded.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGo on,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory said politely.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m not afraid of the dark, so I put on my slicker and\r\n      rubber boots and came out. You see I was always afraid, before,\r\n      to say I didn\xe2\x80\x99t believe in God\xe2\x80\x94because the lightning might strike\r\n      me\xe2\x80\x94but here I am and it hasn\xe2\x80\x99t, of course, but the main point is\r\n      that this time I wasn\xe2\x80\x99t any more afraid of it than I had been\r\n      when I was a Christian Scientist, like I was last year. So now I\r\n      know I\xe2\x80\x99m a materialist and I was fraternizing with the hay when\r\n      you came out and stood by the woods, scared to death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, you little wretch\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d cried Amory indignantly. \xe2\x80\x9cScared of\r\n      what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9c_Yourself!_\xe2\x80\x9d she shouted, and he jumped. She clapped her hands\r\n      and laughed. \xe2\x80\x9cSee\xe2\x80\x94see! Conscience\xe2\x80\x94kill it like me! Eleanor\r\n      Savage, materiologist\xe2\x80\x94no jumping, no starting, come early\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBut I _have_ to have a soul,\xe2\x80\x9d he objected. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\n      rational\xe2\x80\x94and I won\xe2\x80\x99t be molecular.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      She leaned toward him, her burning eyes never leaving his own and\r\n      whispered with a sort of romantic finality:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI thought so, Juan, I feared so\xe2\x80\x94you\xe2\x80\x99re sentimental. You\xe2\x80\x99re not\r\n      like me. I\xe2\x80\x99m a romantic little materialist.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not sentimental\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m as romantic as you are. The idea, you\r\n      know, is that the sentimental person thinks things will last\xe2\x80\x94the\r\n      romantic person has a desperate confidence that they won\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      (This was an ancient distinction of Amory\xe2\x80\x99s.)\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cEpigrams. I\xe2\x80\x99m going home,\xe2\x80\x9d she said sadly. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s get off the\r\n      haystack and walk to the cross-roads.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      They slowly descended from their perch. She would not let him\r\n      help her down and motioning him away arrived in a graceful lump\r\n      in the soft mud where she sat for an instant, laughing at\r\n      herself. Then she jumped to her feet and slipped her hand into\r\n      his, and they tiptoed across the fields, jumping and swinging\r\n      from dry spot to dry spot. A transcendent delight seemed to\r\n      sparkle in every pool of water, for the moon had risen and the\r\n      storm had scurried away into western Maryland. When Eleanor\xe2\x80\x99s arm\r\n      touched his he felt his hands grow cold with deadly fear lest he\r\n      should lose the shadow brush with which his imagination was\r\n      painting wonders of her. He watched her from the corners of his\r\n      eyes as ever he did when he walked with her\xe2\x80\x94she was a feast and a\r\n      folly and he wished it had been his destiny to sit forever on a\r\n      haystack and see life through her green eyes. His paganism soared\r\n      that night and when she faded out like a gray ghost down the\r\n      road, a deep singing came out of the fields and filled his way\r\n      homeward. All night the summer moths flitted in and out of\r\n      Amory\xe2\x80\x99s window; all night large looming sounds swayed in mystic\r\n      revery through the silver grain\xe2\x80\x94and he lay awake in the clear\r\n      darkness.\r\n\r\n\r\n      SEPTEMBER\r\n\r\n      Amory selected a blade of grass and nibbled at it scientifically.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI never fall in love in August or September,\xe2\x80\x9d he proffered.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhen then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cChristmas or Easter. I\xe2\x80\x99m a liturgist.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cEaster!\xe2\x80\x9d She turned up her nose. \xe2\x80\x9cHuh! Spring in corsets!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cEaster _would_ bore spring, wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t she? Easter has her hair\r\n      braided, wears a tailored suit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cBind on thy sandals, oh, thou most fleet. Over the splendor and\r\n  speed of thy feet\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      quoted Eleanor softly, and then added: \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose Hallowe\xe2\x80\x99en is a\r\n      better day for autumn than Thanksgiving.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMuch better\xe2\x80\x94and Christmas eve does very well for winter, but\r\n      summer...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSummer has no day,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cWe can\xe2\x80\x99t possibly have a summer\r\n      love. So many people have tried that the name\xe2\x80\x99s become\r\n      proverbial. Summer is only the unfulfilled promise of spring, a\r\n      charlatan in place of the warm balmy nights I dream of in April.\r\n      It\xe2\x80\x99s a sad season of life without growth.... It has no day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cFourth of July,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory suggested facetiously.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be funny!\xe2\x80\x9d she said, raking him with her eyes.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, what could fulfil the promise of spring?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      She thought a moment.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I suppose heaven would, if there was one,\xe2\x80\x9d she said finally,\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9ca sort of pagan heaven\xe2\x80\x94you ought to be a materialist,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\n      continued irrelevantly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBecause you look a good deal like the pictures of Rupert\r\n      Brooke.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      To some extent Amory tried to play Rupert Brooke as long as he\r\n      knew Eleanor. What he said, his attitude toward life, toward her,\r\n      toward himself, were all reflexes of the dead Englishman\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      literary moods. Often she sat in the grass, a lazy wind playing\r\n      with her short hair, her voice husky as she ran up and down the\r\n      scale from Grantchester to Waikiki. There was something most\r\n      passionate in Eleanor\xe2\x80\x99s reading aloud. They seemed nearer, not\r\n      only mentally, but physically, when they read, than when she was\r\n      in his arms, and this was often, for they fell half into love\r\n      almost from the first. Yet was Amory capable of love now? He\r\n      could, as always, run through the emotions in a half hour, but\r\n      even while they revelled in their imaginations, he knew that\r\n      neither of them could care as he had cared once before\xe2\x80\x94I suppose\r\n      that was why they turned to Brooke, and Swinburne, and Shelley.\r\n      Their chance was to make everything fine and finished and rich\r\n      and imaginative; they must bend tiny golden tentacles from his\r\n      imagination to hers, that would take the place of the great, deep\r\n      love that was never so near, yet never so much of a dream.\r\n\r\n      One poem they read over and over; Swinburne\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cTriumph of Time,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      and four lines of it rang in his memory afterward on warm nights\r\n      when he saw the fireflies among dusky tree trunks and heard the\r\n      low drone of many frogs. Then Eleanor seemed to come out of the\r\n      night and stand by him, and he heard her throaty voice, with its\r\n      tone of a fleecy-headed drum, repeating:\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cIs it worth a tear, is it worth an hour, To think of things that\r\n   are well outworn; Of fruitless husk and fugitive flower, The dream\r\n   foregone and the deed foreborne?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      They were formally introduced two days later, and his aunt told\r\n      him her history. The Ramillys were two: old Mr. Ramilly and his\r\n      granddaughter, Eleanor. She had lived in France with a restless\r\n      mother whom Amory imagined to have been very like his own, on\r\n      whose death she had come to America, to live in Maryland. She had\r\n      gone to Baltimore first to stay with a bachelor uncle, and there\r\n      she insisted on being a debutante at the age of seventeen. She\r\n      had a wild winter and arrived in the country in March, having\r\n      quarrelled frantically with all her Baltimore relatives, and\r\n      shocked them into fiery protest. A rather fast crowd had come\r\n      out, who drank cocktails in limousines and were promiscuously\r\n      condescending and patronizing toward older people, and Eleanor\r\n      with an esprit that hinted strongly of the boulevards, led many\r\n      innocents still redolent of St. Timothy\xe2\x80\x99s and Farmington, into\r\n      paths of Bohemian naughtiness. When the story came to her uncle,\r\n      a forgetful cavalier of a more hypocritical era, there was a\r\n      scene, from which Eleanor emerged, subdued but rebellious and\r\n      indignant, to seek haven with her grandfather who hovered in the\r\n      country on the near side of senility. That\xe2\x80\x99s as far as her story\r\n      went; she told him the rest herself, but that was later.\r\n\r\n      Often they swam and as Amory floated lazily in the water he shut\r\n      his mind to all thoughts except those of hazy soap-bubble lands\r\n      where the sun splattered through wind-drunk trees. How could any\r\n      one possibly think or worry, or do anything except splash and\r\n      dive and loll there on the edge of time while the flower months\r\n      failed. Let the days move over\xe2\x80\x94sadness and memory and pain\r\n      recurred outside, and here, once more, before he went on to meet\r\n      them he wanted to drift and be young.\r\n\r\n      There were days when Amory resented that life had changed from an\r\n      even progress along a road stretching ever in sight, with the\r\n      scenery merging and blending, into a succession of quick,\r\n      unrelated scenes\xe2\x80\x94two years of sweat and blood, that sudden absurd\r\n      instinct for paternity that Rosalind had stirred; the\r\n      half-sensual, half-neurotic quality of this autumn with Eleanor.\r\n      He felt that it would take all time, more than he could ever\r\n      spare, to glue these strange cumbersome pictures into the\r\n      scrap-book of his life. It was all like a banquet where he sat\r\n      for this half-hour of his youth and tried to enjoy brilliant\r\n      epicurean courses.\r\n\r\n      Dimly he promised himself a time where all should be welded\r\n      together. For months it seemed that he had alternated between\r\n      being borne along a stream of love or fascination, or left in an\r\n      eddy, and in the eddies he had not desired to think, rather to be\r\n      picked up on a wave\xe2\x80\x99s top and swept along again.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThe despairing, dying autumn and our love\xe2\x80\x94how well they\r\n      harmonize!\xe2\x80\x9d said Eleanor sadly one day as they lay dripping by\r\n      the water.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThe Indian summer of our hearts\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he ceased.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cTell me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said finally, \xe2\x80\x9cwas she light or dark?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLight.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWas she more beautiful than I am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory shortly.\r\n\r\n      One night they walked while the moon rose and poured a great\r\n      burden of glory over the garden until it seemed fairyland with\r\n      Amory and Eleanor, dim phantasmal shapes, expressing eternal\r\n      beauty in curious elfin love moods. Then they turned out of the\r\n      moonlight into the trellised darkness of a vine-hung pagoda,\r\n      where there were scents so plaintive as to be nearly musical.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLight a match,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered. \xe2\x80\x9cI want to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Scratch! Flare!\r\n\r\n      The night and the scarred trees were like scenery in a play, and\r\n      to be there with Eleanor, shadowy and unreal, seemed somehow\r\n      oddly familiar. Amory thought how it was only the past that ever\r\n      seemed strange and unbelievable. The match went out.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s black as pitch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99re just voices now,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Eleanor, \xe2\x80\x9clittle lonesome\r\n      voices. Light another.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat was my last match.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Suddenly he caught her in his arms.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou _are_ mine\xe2\x80\x94you know you\xe2\x80\x99re mine!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried wildly... the\r\n      moonlight twisted in through the vines and listened... the\r\n      fireflies hung upon their whispers as if to win his glance from\r\n      the glory of their eyes.\r\n\r\n\r\n      THE END OF SUMMER\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo wind is stirring in the grass; not one wind stirs... the\r\n      water in the hidden pools, as glass, fronts the full moon and so\r\n      inters the golden token in its icy mass,\xe2\x80\x9d chanted Eleanor to the\r\n      trees that skeletoned the body of the night. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t it ghostly\r\n      here? If you can hold your horse\xe2\x80\x99s feet up, let\xe2\x80\x99s cut through the\r\n      woods and find the hidden pools.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s after one, and you\xe2\x80\x99ll get the devil,\xe2\x80\x9d he objected, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\r\n      don\xe2\x80\x99t know enough about horses to put one away in the pitch\r\n      dark.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cShut up, you old fool,\xe2\x80\x9d she whispered irrelevantly, and, leaning\r\n      over, she patted him lazily with her riding-crop. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can leave\r\n      your old plug in our stable and I\xe2\x80\x99ll send him over to-morrow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBut my uncle has got to drive me to the station with this old\r\n      plug at seven o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t be a spoil-sport\xe2\x80\x94remember, you have a tendency toward\r\n      wavering that prevents you from being the entire light of my\r\n      life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory drew his horse up close beside, and, leaning toward her,\r\n      grasped her hand.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSay I am\xe2\x80\x94_quick_, or I\xe2\x80\x99ll pull you over and make you ride behind\r\n      me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      She looked up and smiled and shook her head excitedly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, do!\xe2\x80\x94or rather, don\xe2\x80\x99t! Why are all the exciting things so\r\n      uncomfortable, like fighting and exploring and ski-ing in Canada?\r\n      By the way, we\xe2\x80\x99re going to ride up Harper\xe2\x80\x99s Hill. I think that\r\n      comes in our programme about five o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou little devil,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory growled. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re going to make me stay\r\n      up all night and sleep in the train like an immigrant all day\r\n      to-morrow, going back to New York.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHush! some one\xe2\x80\x99s coming along the road\xe2\x80\x94let\xe2\x80\x99s go! Whoo-ee-oop!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      And with a shout that probably gave the belated traveller a\r\n      series of shivers, she turned her horse into the woods and Amory\r\n      followed slowly, as he had followed her all day for three weeks.\r\n\r\n      The summer was over, but he had spent the days in watching\r\n      Eleanor, a graceful, facile Manfred, build herself intellectual\r\n      and imaginative pyramids while she revelled in the\r\n      artificialities of the temperamental teens and they wrote poetry\r\n      at the dinner-table.\r\n\r\n  When Vanity kissed Vanity, a hundred happy Junes ago, he pondered\r\n  o\xe2\x80\x99er her breathlessly, and, that all men might ever know, he rhymed\r\n  her eyes with life and death:\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cThru Time I\xe2\x80\x99ll save my love!\xe2\x80\x9d he said... yet Beauty vanished with\r\n  his breath, and, with her lovers, she was dead...\r\n  \xe2\x80\x94Ever his wit and not her eyes, ever his art and not her hair:\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99d learn a trick in rhyme, be wise and pause before his sonnet\r\n  there\xe2\x80\x9d...  So all my words, however true, might sing you to a\r\n  thousandth June, and no one ever _know_ that you were Beauty for an\r\n  afternoon.\r\n\r\n      So he wrote one day, when he pondered how coldly we thought of\r\n      the \xe2\x80\x9cDark Lady of the Sonnets,\xe2\x80\x9d and how little we remembered her\r\n      as the great man wanted her remembered. For what Shakespeare\r\n      _must_ have desired, to have been able to write with such divine\r\n      despair, was that the lady should live... and now we have no real\r\n      interest in her.... The irony of it is that if he had cared\r\n      _more_ for the poem than for the lady the sonnet would be only\r\n      obvious, imitative rhetoric and no one would ever have read it\r\n      after twenty years....\r\n\r\n      This was the last night Amory ever saw Eleanor. He was leaving in\r\n      the morning and they had agreed to take a long farewell trot by\r\n      the cold moonlight. She wanted to talk, she said\xe2\x80\x94perhaps the last\r\n      time in her life that she could be rational (she meant pose with\r\n      comfort). So they had turned into the woods and rode for half an\r\n      hour with scarcely a word, except when she whispered \xe2\x80\x9cDamn!\xe2\x80\x9d at a\r\n      bothersome branch\xe2\x80\x94whispered it as no other girl was ever able to\r\n      whisper it. Then they started up Harper\xe2\x80\x99s Hill, walking their\r\n      tired horses.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGood Lord! It\xe2\x80\x99s quiet here!\xe2\x80\x9d whispered Eleanor; \xe2\x80\x9cmuch more\r\n      lonesome than the woods.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI hate woods,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory said, shuddering. \xe2\x80\x9cAny kind of foliage or\r\n      underbrush at night. Out here it\xe2\x80\x99s so broad and easy on the\r\n      spirit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThe long slope of a long hill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd the cold moon rolling moonlight down it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd thee and me, last and most important.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      It was quiet that night\xe2\x80\x94the straight road they followed up to the\r\n      edge of the cliff knew few footsteps at any time. Only an\r\n      occasional negro cabin, silver-gray in the rock-ribbed moonlight,\r\n      broke the long line of bare ground; behind lay the black edge of\r\n      the woods like a dark frosting on white cake, and ahead the\r\n      sharp, high horizon. It was much colder\xe2\x80\x94so cold that it settled\r\n      on them and drove all the warm nights from their minds.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThe end of summer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eleanor softly. \xe2\x80\x9cListen to the beat of\r\n      our horses\xe2\x80\x99 hoofs\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x98tump-tump-tump-a-tump.\xe2\x80\x99 Have you ever been\r\n      feverish and had all noises divide into \xe2\x80\x98tump-tump-tump\xe2\x80\x99 until\r\n      you could swear eternity was divisible into so many tumps? That\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      the way I feel\xe2\x80\x94old horses go tump-tump.... I guess that\xe2\x80\x99s the\r\n      only thing that separates horses and clocks from us. Human beings\r\n      can\xe2\x80\x99t go \xe2\x80\x98tump-tump-tump\xe2\x80\x99 without going crazy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The breeze freshened and Eleanor pulled her cape around her and\r\n      shivered.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAre you very cold?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Amory.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99m thinking about myself\xe2\x80\x94my black old inside self, the real\r\n      one, with the fundamental honesty that keeps me from being\r\n      absolutely wicked by making me realize my own sins.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      They were riding up close by the cliff and Amory gazed over.\r\n      Where the fall met the ground a hundred feet below, a black\r\n      stream made a sharp line, broken by tiny glints in the swift\r\n      water.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cRotten, rotten old world,\xe2\x80\x9d broke out Eleanor suddenly, \xe2\x80\x9cand the\r\n      wretchedest thing of all is me\xe2\x80\x94oh, _why_ am I a girl? Why am I\r\n      not a stupid\xe2\x80\x94? Look at you; you\xe2\x80\x99re stupider than I am, not much,\r\n      but some, and you can lope about and get bored and then lope\r\n      somewhere else, and you can play around with girls without being\r\n      involved in meshes of sentiment, and you can do anything and be\r\n      justified\xe2\x80\x94and here am I with the brains to do everything, yet\r\n      tied to the sinking ship of future matrimony. If I were born a\r\n      hundred years from now, well and good, but now what\xe2\x80\x99s in store\r\n      for me\xe2\x80\x94I have to marry, that goes without saying. Who? I\xe2\x80\x99m too\r\n      bright for most men, and yet I have to descend to their level and\r\n      let them patronize my intellect in order to get their attention.\r\n      Every year that I don\xe2\x80\x99t marry I\xe2\x80\x99ve got less chance for a\r\n      first-class man. At the best I can have my choice from one or two\r\n      cities and, of course, I have to marry into a dinner-coat.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cListen,\xe2\x80\x9d she leaned close again, \xe2\x80\x9cI like clever men and\r\n      good-looking men, and, of course, no one cares more for\r\n      personality than I do. Oh, just one person in fifty has any\r\n      glimmer of what sex is. I\xe2\x80\x99m hipped on Freud and all that, but\r\n      it\xe2\x80\x99s rotten that every bit of _real_ love in the world is\r\n      ninety-nine per cent passion and one little soupcon of jealousy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      She finished as suddenly as she began.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOf course, you\xe2\x80\x99re right,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory agreed. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a rather\r\n      unpleasant overpowering force that\xe2\x80\x99s part of the machinery under\r\n      everything. It\xe2\x80\x99s like an actor that lets you see his mechanics!\r\n      Wait a minute till I think this out....\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He paused and tried to get a metaphor. They had turned the cliff\r\n      and were riding along the road about fifty feet to the left.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou see every one\xe2\x80\x99s got to have some cloak to throw around it.\r\n      The mediocre intellects, Plato\xe2\x80\x99s second class, use the remnants\r\n      of romantic chivalry diluted with Victorian sentiment\xe2\x80\x94and we who\r\n      consider ourselves the intellectuals cover it up by pretending\r\n      that it\xe2\x80\x99s another side of us, has nothing to do with our shining\r\n      brains; we pretend that the fact that we realize it is really\r\n      absolving us from being a prey to it. But the truth is that sex\r\n      is right in the middle of our purest abstractions, so close that\r\n      it obscures vision.... I can kiss you now and will. ...\xe2\x80\x9d He\r\n      leaned toward her in his saddle, but she drew away.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t kiss you now\xe2\x80\x94I\xe2\x80\x99m more sensitive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re more stupid then,\xe2\x80\x9d he declared rather impatiently.\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIntellect is no protection from sex any more than convention\r\n      is...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is?\xe2\x80\x9d she fired up. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Catholic Church or the maxims of\r\n      Confucius?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory looked up, rather taken aback.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s your panacea, isn\xe2\x80\x99t it?\xe2\x80\x9d she cried. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, you\xe2\x80\x99re just an\r\n      old hypocrite, too. Thousands of scowling priests keeping the\r\n      degenerate Italians and illiterate Irish repentant with\r\n      gabble-gabble about the sixth and ninth commandments. It\xe2\x80\x99s just\r\n      all cloaks, sentiment and spiritual rouge and panaceas. I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell\r\n      you there is no God, not even a definite abstract goodness; so\r\n      it\xe2\x80\x99s all got to be worked out for the individual by the\r\n      individual here in high white foreheads like mine, and you\xe2\x80\x99re too\r\n      much the prig to admit it.\xe2\x80\x9d She let go her reins and shook her\r\n      little fists at the stars.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIf there\xe2\x80\x99s a God let him strike me\xe2\x80\x94strike me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cTalking about God again after the manner of atheists,\xe2\x80\x9d Amory\r\n      said sharply. His materialism, always a thin cloak, was torn to\r\n      shreds by Eleanor\xe2\x80\x99s blasphemy.... She knew it and it angered him\r\n      that she knew it.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnd like most intellectuals who don\xe2\x80\x99t find faith convenient,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\n      continued coldly, \xe2\x80\x9clike Napoleon and Oscar Wilde and the rest of\r\n      your type, you\xe2\x80\x99ll yell loudly for a priest on your death-bed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Eleanor drew her horse up sharply and he reined in beside her.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWill I?\xe2\x80\x9d she said in a queer voice that scared him. \xe2\x80\x9cWill I?\r\n      Watch! _I\xe2\x80\x99m going over the cliff!_\xe2\x80\x9d And before he could interfere\r\n      she had turned and was riding breakneck for the end of the\r\n      plateau.\r\n\r\n      He wheeled and started after her, his body like ice, his nerves\r\n      in a vast clangor. There was no chance of stopping her. The moon\r\n      was under a cloud and her horse would step blindly over. Then\r\n      some ten feet from the edge of the cliff she gave a sudden shriek\r\n      and flung herself sideways\xe2\x80\x94plunged from her horse and, rolling\r\n      over twice, landed in a pile of brush five feet from the edge.\r\n      The horse went over with a frantic whinny. In a minute he was by\r\n      Eleanor\xe2\x80\x99s side and saw that her eyes were open.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cEleanor!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried.\r\n\r\n      She did not answer, but her lips moved and her eyes filled with\r\n      sudden tears.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cEleanor, are you hurt?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo; I don\xe2\x80\x99t think so,\xe2\x80\x9d she said faintly, and then began weeping.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMy horse dead?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGood God\xe2\x80\x94Yes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh!\xe2\x80\x9d she wailed. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought I was going over. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He helped her gently to her feet and boosted her onto his saddle.\r\n      So they started homeward; Amory walking and she bent forward on\r\n      the pommel, sobbing bitterly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve got a crazy streak,\xe2\x80\x9d she faltered, \xe2\x80\x9ctwice before I\xe2\x80\x99ve done\r\n      things like that. When I was eleven mother went\xe2\x80\x94went mad\xe2\x80\x94stark\r\n      raving crazy. We were in Vienna\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      All the way back she talked haltingly about herself, and Amory\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      love waned slowly with the moon. At her door they started from\r\n      habit to kiss good night, but she could not run into his arms,\r\n      nor were they stretched to meet her as in the week before. For a\r\n      minute they stood there, hating each other with a bitter sadness.\r\n      But as Amory had loved himself in Eleanor, so now what he hated\r\n      was only a mirror. Their poses were strewn about the pale dawn\r\n      like broken glass. The stars were long gone and there were left\r\n      only the little sighing gusts of wind and the silences between...\r\n      but naked souls are poor things ever, and soon he turned homeward\r\n      and let new lights come in with the sun.\r\n\r\n\r\n      A POEM THAT ELEANOR SENT AMORY SEVERAL YEARS LATER\r\n\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cHere, Earth-born, over the lilt of the water, Lisping its music and\r\n bearing a burden of light, Bosoming day as a laughing and radiant\r\n daughter... Here we may whisper unheard, unafraid of the night.\r\n Walking alone... was it splendor, or what, we were bound with, Deep in\r\n the time when summer lets down her hair? Shadows we loved and the\r\n patterns they covered the ground with Tapestries, mystical, faint in\r\n the breathless air.\r\n  That was the day... and the night for another story, Pale as a dream\r\n  and shadowed with pencilled trees\xe2\x80\x94 Ghosts of the stars came by who\r\n  had sought for glory, Whispered to us of peace in the plaintive\r\n  breeze, Whispered of old dead faiths that the day had shattered,\r\n  Youth the penny that bought delight of the moon; That was the urge\r\n  that we knew and the language that mattered That was the debt that we\r\n  paid to the usurer June.\r\n  Here, deepest of dreams, by the waters that bring not Anything back\r\n  of the past that we need not know, What if the light is but sun and\r\n  the little streams sing not, We are together, it seems... I have\r\n  loved you so... What did the last night hold, with the summer over,\r\n  Drawing us back to the home in the changing glade? _What leered out\r\n  of the dark in the ghostly clover?_ God!... till you stirred in your\r\n  sleep... and were wild afraid...\r\n  Well... we have passed... we are chronicle now to the eerie. Curious\r\n  metal from meteors that failed in the sky; Earth-born the tireless is\r\n  stretched by the water, quite weary, Close to this ununderstandable\r\n  changeling that\xe2\x80\x99s I... Fear is an echo we traced to Security\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n  daughter; Now we are faces and voices... and less, too soon,\r\n  Whispering half-love over the lilt of the water... Youth the penny\r\n  that bought delight of the moon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n      A POEM AMORY SENT TO ELEANOR AND WHICH HE CALLED \xe2\x80\x9cSUMMER STORM\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cFaint winds, and a song fading and leaves falling, Faint winds, and\r\n   far away a fading laughter... And the rain and over the fields a\r\n   voice calling...\r\n    Our gray blown cloud scurries and lifts above, Slides on the sun\r\n    and flutters there to waft her Sisters on.  The shadow of a dove\r\n    Falls on the cote, the trees are filled with wings; And down the\r\n    valley through the crying trees The body of the darker storm flies;\r\n    brings With its new air the breath of sunken seas And slender\r\n    tenuous thunder... But I wait... Wait for the mists and for the\r\n    blacker rain\xe2\x80\x94 Heavier winds that stir the veil of fate, Happier\r\n    winds that pile her hair; Again They tear me, teach me, strew the\r\n    heavy air Upon me, winds that I know, and storm.\r\n    There was a summer every rain was rare; There was a season every\r\n    wind was warm.... And now you pass me in the mist... your hair\r\n    Rain-blown about you, damp lips curved once more In that wild\r\n    irony, that gay despair That made you old when we have met before;\r\n    Wraith-like you drift on out before the rain, Across the fields,\r\n    blown with the stemless flowers, With your old hopes, dead leaves\r\n    and loves again\xe2\x80\x94 Dim as a dream and wan with all old hours\r\n    (Whispers will creep into the growing dark... Tumult will die over\r\n    the trees) Now night Tears from her wetted breast the splattered\r\n    blouse Of day, glides down the dreaming hills, tear-bright, To\r\n    cover with her hair the eerie green... Love for the dusk... Love\r\n    for the glistening after; Quiet the trees to their last tops...\r\n    serene...\r\n    Faint winds, and far away a fading laughter...\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n      CHAPTER 4. The Supercilious Sacrifice\r\n\r\n\r\n      Atlantic City. Amory paced the board walk at day\xe2\x80\x99s end, lulled by\r\n      the everlasting surge of changing waves, smelling the\r\n      half-mournful odor of the salt breeze. The sea, he thought, had\r\n      treasured its memories deeper than the faithless land. It seemed\r\n      still to whisper of Norse galleys ploughing the water world under\r\n      raven-figured flags, of the British dreadnoughts, gray bulwarks\r\n      of civilization steaming up through the fog of one dark July into\r\n      the North Sea.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94Amory Blaine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory looked down into the street below. A low racing car had\r\n      drawn to a stop and a familiar cheerful face protruded from the\r\n      driver\xe2\x80\x99s seat.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCome on down, goopher!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Alec.\r\n\r\n      Amory called a greeting and descending a flight of wooden steps\r\n      approached the car. He and Alec had been meeting intermittently,\r\n      but the barrier of Rosalind lay always between them. He was sorry\r\n      for this; he hated to lose Alec.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Blaine, this is Miss Waterson, Miss Wayne, and Mr. Tully.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHow d\xe2\x80\x99y do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAmory,\xe2\x80\x9d said Alec exuberantly, \xe2\x80\x9cif you\xe2\x80\x99ll jump in we\xe2\x80\x99ll take you\r\n      to some secluded nook and give you a wee jolt of Bourbon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory considered.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s an idea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cStep in\xe2\x80\x94move over, Jill, and Amory will smile very handsomely at\r\n      you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory squeezed into the back seat beside a gaudy,\r\n      vermilion-lipped blonde.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHello, Doug Fairbanks,\xe2\x80\x9d she said flippantly. \xe2\x80\x9cWalking for\r\n      exercise or hunting for company?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI was counting the waves,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Amory gravely. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m going in\r\n      for statistics.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t kid me, Doug.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      When they reached an unfrequented side street Alec stopped the\r\n      car among deep shadows.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat you doing down here these cold days, Amory?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded,\r\n      as he produced a quart of Bourbon from under the fur rug.\r\n\r\n      Amory avoided the question. Indeed, he had had no definite reason\r\n      for coming to the coast.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDo you remember that party of ours, sophomore year?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked\r\n      instead.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDo I? When we slept in the pavilions up in Asbury Park\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLord, Alec! It\xe2\x80\x99s hard to think that Jesse and Dick and Kerry are\r\n      all three dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Alec shivered.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t talk about it. These dreary fall days depress me enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Jill seemed to agree.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDoug here is sorta gloomy anyways,\xe2\x80\x9d she commented. \xe2\x80\x9cTell him to\r\n      drink deep\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s good and scarce these days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat I really want to ask you, Amory, is where you are\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, New York, I suppose\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI mean to-night, because if you haven\xe2\x80\x99t got a room yet you\xe2\x80\x99d\r\n      better help me out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGlad to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou see, Tully and I have two rooms with bath between at the\r\n      Ranier, and he\xe2\x80\x99s got to go back to New York. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to have\r\n      to move. Question is, will you occupy one of the rooms?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory was willing, if he could get in right away.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll find the key in the office; the rooms are in my name.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Declining further locomotion or further stimulation, Amory left\r\n      the car and sauntered back along the board walk to the hotel.\r\n\r\n      He was in an eddy again, a deep, lethargic gulf, without desire\r\n      to work or write, love or dissipate. For the first time in his\r\n      life he rather longed for death to roll over his generation,\r\n      obliterating their petty fevers and struggles and exultations.\r\n      His youth seemed never so vanished as now in the contrast between\r\n      the utter loneliness of this visit and that riotous, joyful party\r\n      of four years before. Things that had been the merest\r\n      commonplaces of his life then, deep sleep, the sense of beauty\r\n      around him, all desire, had flown away and the gaps they left\r\n      were filled only with the great listlessness of his disillusion.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cTo hold a man a woman has to appeal to the worst in him.\xe2\x80\x9d This\r\n      sentence was the thesis of most of his bad nights, of which he\r\n      felt this was to be one. His mind had already started to play\r\n      variations on the subject. Tireless passion, fierce jealousy,\r\n      longing to possess and crush\xe2\x80\x94these alone were left of all his\r\n      love for Rosalind; these remained to him as payment for the loss\r\n      of his youth\xe2\x80\x94bitter calomel under the thin sugar of love\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      exaltation.\r\n\r\n      In his room he undressed and wrapping himself in blankets to keep\r\n      out the chill October air drowsed in an armchair by the open\r\n      window.\r\n\r\n      He remembered a poem he had read months before:\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cOh staunch old heart who toiled so long for me, I waste my years\r\n   sailing along the sea\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Yet he had no sense of waste, no sense of the present hope that\r\n      waste implied. He felt that life had rejected him.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cRosalind! Rosalind!\xe2\x80\x9d He poured the words softly into the\r\n      half-darkness until she seemed to permeate the room; the wet salt\r\n      breeze filled his hair with moisture, the rim of a moon seared\r\n      the sky and made the curtains dim and ghostly. He fell asleep.\r\n\r\n      When he awoke it was very late and quiet. The blanket had slipped\r\n      partly off his shoulders and he touched his skin to find it damp\r\n      and cold.\r\n\r\n      Then he became aware of a tense whispering not ten feet away.\r\n\r\n      He became rigid.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t make a sound!\xe2\x80\x9d It was Alec\xe2\x80\x99s voice. \xe2\x80\x9cJill\xe2\x80\x94do you hear me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d breathed very low, very frightened. They were in the\r\n      bathroom.\r\n\r\n      Then his ears caught a louder sound from somewhere along the\r\n      corridor outside. It was a mumbling of men\xe2\x80\x99s voices and a\r\n      repeated muffled rapping.
