Amory threw off the blankets and moved\r\n      close to the bathroom door.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMy God!\xe2\x80\x9d came the girl\xe2\x80\x99s voice again. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll have to let them\r\n      in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Suddenly a steady, insistent knocking began at Amory\xe2\x80\x99s hall door\r\n      and simultaneously out of the bathroom came Alec, followed by the\r\n      vermilion-lipped girl. They were both clad in pajamas.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAmory!\xe2\x80\x9d an anxious whisper.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the trouble?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s house detectives. My God, Amory\xe2\x80\x94they\xe2\x80\x99re just looking for a\r\n      test-case\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, better let them in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t understand. They can get me under the Mann Act.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The girl followed him slowly, a rather miserable, pathetic figure\r\n      in the darkness.\r\n\r\n      Amory tried to plan quickly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou make a racket and let them in your room,\xe2\x80\x9d he suggested\r\n      anxiously, \xe2\x80\x9cand I\xe2\x80\x99ll get her out by this door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThey\xe2\x80\x99re here too, though. They\xe2\x80\x99ll watch this door.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCan\xe2\x80\x99t you give a wrong name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo chance. I registered under my own name; besides, they\xe2\x80\x99d trail\r\n      the auto license number.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSay you\xe2\x80\x99re married.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cJill says one of the house detectives knows her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The girl had stolen to the bed and tumbled upon it; lay there\r\n      listening wretchedly to the knocking which had grown gradually to\r\n      a pounding. Then came a man\xe2\x80\x99s voice, angry and imperative:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOpen up or we\xe2\x80\x99ll break the door in!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      In the silence when this voice ceased Amory realized that there\r\n      were other things in the room besides people... over and around\r\n      the figure crouched on the bed there hung an aura, gossamer as a\r\n      moonbeam, tainted as stale, weak wine, yet a horror, diffusively\r\n      brooding already over the three of them... and over by the window\r\n      among the stirring curtains stood something else, featureless and\r\n      indistinguishable, yet strangely familiar.... Simultaneously two\r\n      great cases presented themselves side by side to Amory; all that\r\n      took place in his mind, then, occupied in actual time less than\r\n      ten seconds.\r\n\r\n      The first fact that flashed radiantly on his comprehension was\r\n      the great impersonality of sacrifice\xe2\x80\x94he perceived that what we\r\n      call love and hate, reward and punishment, had no more to do with\r\n      it than the date of the month. He quickly recapitulated the story\r\n      of a sacrifice he had heard of in college: a man had cheated in\r\n      an examination; his roommate in a gust of sentiment had taken the\r\n      entire blame\xe2\x80\x94due to the shame of it the innocent one\xe2\x80\x99s entire\r\n      future seemed shrouded in regret and failure, capped by the\r\n      ingratitude of the real culprit. He had finally taken his own\r\n      life\xe2\x80\x94years afterward the facts had come out. At the time the\r\n      story had both puzzled and worried Amory. Now he realized the\r\n      truth; that sacrifice was no purchase of freedom. It was like a\r\n      great elective office, it was like an inheritance of power\xe2\x80\x94to\r\n      certain people at certain times an essential luxury, carrying\r\n      with it not a guarantee but a responsibility, not a security but\r\n      an infinite risk. Its very momentum might drag him down to\r\n      ruin\xe2\x80\x94the passing of the emotional wave that made it possible\r\n      might leave the one who made it high and dry forever on an island\r\n      of despair.\r\n\r\n      ... Amory knew that afterward Alec would secretly hate him for\r\n      having done so much for him....\r\n\r\n      ... All this was flung before Amory like an opened scroll, while\r\n      ulterior to him and speculating upon him were those two\r\n      breathless, listening forces: the gossamer aura that hung over\r\n      and about the girl and that familiar thing by the window.\r\n\r\n      Sacrifice by its very nature was arrogant and impersonal;\r\n      sacrifice should be eternally supercilious.\r\n\r\n      _Weep not for me but for thy children._\r\n\r\n      That\xe2\x80\x94thought Amory\xe2\x80\x94would be somehow the way God would talk to me.\r\n\r\n      Amory felt a sudden surge of joy and then like a face in a\r\n      motion-picture the aura over the bed faded out; the dynamic\r\n      shadow by the window, that was as near as he could name it,\r\n      remained for the fraction of a moment and then the breeze seemed\r\n      to lift it swiftly out of the room. He clinched his hands in\r\n      quick ecstatic excitement... the ten seconds were up....\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDo what I say, Alec\xe2\x80\x94do what I say. Do you understand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Alec looked at him dumbly\xe2\x80\x94his face a tableau of anguish.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou have a family,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Amory slowly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have a family\r\n      and it\xe2\x80\x99s important that you should get out of this. Do you hear\r\n      me?\xe2\x80\x9d He repeated clearly what he had said. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you hear me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI hear you.\xe2\x80\x9d The voice was curiously strained, the eyes never\r\n      for a second left Amory\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAlec, you\xe2\x80\x99re going to lie down here. If any one comes in you act\r\n      drunk. You do what I say\xe2\x80\x94if you don\xe2\x80\x99t I\xe2\x80\x99ll probably kill you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      There was another moment while they stared at each other. Then\r\n      Amory went briskly to the bureau and, taking his pocket-book,\r\n      beckoned peremptorily to the girl. He heard one word from Alec\r\n      that sounded like \xe2\x80\x9cpenitentiary,\xe2\x80\x9d then he and Jill were in the\r\n      bathroom with the door bolted behind them.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re here with me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said sternly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve been with me all\r\n      evening.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      She nodded, gave a little half cry.\r\n\r\n      In a second he had the door of the other room open and three men\r\n      entered. As Amory slipped into Alec\xe2\x80\x99s B. V. D.\xe2\x80\x99s he found\r\n      that his attitude toward the situation was agreeably humorous.\r\n      The aggrieved virtue of the burly man made him want to laugh.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAnybody else here?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Olson, trying to look keen and\r\n      ferret-like.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cFellow who had the rooms,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory carelessly. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s drunk as\r\n      an owl, though. What\xe2\x80\x99s your name? Sarah Murphy? I don\xe2\x80\x99t want my mother to\r\n      know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCome on now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cShut up!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Amory at Olson.\r\n\r\n      An instant\xe2\x80\x99s pause.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cStella Robbins,\xe2\x80\x9d she faltered finally. \xe2\x80\x9cGeneral Delivery,\r\n      Rugway, New Hampshire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Olson snapped his note-book shut and looked at them very\r\n      ponderously.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBy rights the hotel could turn the evidence over to the police\r\n      and you\xe2\x80\x99d go to penitentiary, you would, for bringin\xe2\x80\x99 a girl from\r\n      one State to \xe2\x80\x99nother f\xe2\x80\x99r immoral purp\xe2\x80\x99ses\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d He paused to let the\r\n      majesty of his words sink in. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\xe2\x80\x94the hotel is going to let you\r\n      off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t want to get in the papers,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Jill fiercely. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\r\n      us off! Huh!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      A great lightness surrounded Amory. There\xe2\x80\x99s been too much of this stuff, and we got\r\n      a \xe2\x80\x99rangement with the newspapers so that you get a little free\r\n      publicity. Not the name of the hotel, but just a line sayin\xe2\x80\x99 that\r\n      you had a little trouble in \xe2\x80\x99lantic City. See?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re gettin\xe2\x80\x99 off light\xe2\x80\x94damn light\xe2\x80\x94but\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cCome on,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory briskly. \xe2\x80\x9cLet\xe2\x80\x99s get out of here. He reached out and\r\n      tapped Olson on the arm.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWould you mind taking off your hat? Nobody\xe2\x80\x99ll ever know about this\xe2\x80\x94except our friends and\r\n      enemies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Dawn was breaking over the sea.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s getting blue,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt does very well,\xe2\x80\x9d agreed Amory critically, and then as an\r\n      after-thought: \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s almost breakfast-time\xe2\x80\x94do you want something\r\n      to eat?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cFood\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d she said with a cheerful laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cFood is what queered the\r\n      party. We ordered a big supper to be sent up to the room about\r\n      two o\xe2\x80\x99clock. Alec didn\xe2\x80\x99t give the waiter a tip, so I guess the\r\n      little bastard snitched.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Jill\xe2\x80\x99s low spirits seemed to have gone faster than the scattering\r\n      night. \xe2\x80\x9cLet me tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said emphatically, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen you want\r\n      to stage that sorta party stay away from liquor, and when you\r\n      want to get tight stay away from bedrooms.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll remember.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He tapped suddenly at the glass and they drew up at the door of\r\n      an all-night restaurant.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIs Alec a great friend of yours?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Jill as they perched\r\n      themselves on high stools inside, and set their elbows on the\r\n      dingy counter.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHe used to be. He probably won\xe2\x80\x99t want to be any more\xe2\x80\x94and never\r\n      understand why.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt was sorta crazy you takin\xe2\x80\x99 all that blame. Is he pretty\r\n      important? Kinda more important than you are?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory laughed.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat remains to be seen,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the question.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n      THE COLLAPSE OF SEVERAL PILLARS\r\n\r\n      Two days later back in New York Amory found in a newspaper what\r\n      he had been searching for\xe2\x80\x94a dozen lines which announced to whom\r\n      it might concern that Mr. Amory Blaine, who \xe2\x80\x9cgave his address\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      as, etc., had been requested to leave his hotel in Atlantic City\r\n      because of entertaining in his room a lady _not_ his wife.\r\n\r\n      Then he started, and his fingers trembled, for directly above was\r\n      a longer paragraph of which the first words were:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMr. and Mrs. Leland R. Connage are announcing the engagement of\r\n      their daughter, Rosalind, to Mr. J. Dawson Ryder, of Hartford,\r\n      Connecticut\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He dropped the paper and lay down on his bed with a frightened,\r\n      sinking sensation in the pit of his stomach. She was gone,\r\n      definitely, finally gone. Until now he had half unconsciously\r\n      cherished the hope deep in his heart that some day she would need\r\n      him and send for him, cry that it had been a mistake, that her\r\n      heart ached only for the pain she had caused him. Never again\r\n      could he find even the sombre luxury of wanting her\xe2\x80\x94not this\r\n      Rosalind, harder, older\xe2\x80\x94nor any beaten, broken woman that his\r\n      imagination brought to the door of his forties\xe2\x80\x94Amory had wanted\r\n      her youth, the fresh radiance of her mind and body, the stuff\r\n      that she was selling now once and for all. So far as he was\r\n      concerned, young Rosalind was dead.\r\n\r\n      A day later came a crisp, terse letter from Mr. Barton in\r\n      Chicago, which informed him that as three more street-car\r\n      companies had gone into the hands of receivers he could expect\r\n      for the present no further remittances. Last of all, on a dazed\r\n      Sunday night, a telegram told him of Monsignor Darcy\xe2\x80\x99s sudden\r\n      death in Philadelphia five days before.\r\n\r\n      He knew then what it was that he had perceived among the curtains\r\n      of the room in Atlantic City.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n      CHAPTER 5. The Egotist Becomes a Personage\r\n\r\n\r\n   \xe2\x80\x9cA fathom deep in sleep I lie With old desires, restrained before,\r\n   To clamor lifeward with a cry, As dark flies out the greying door;\r\n   And so in quest of creeds to share I seek assertive day again... But\r\n   old monotony is there: Endless avenues of rain.\r\n    Oh, might I rise again!  Might I Throw off the heat of that old\r\n    wine, See the new morning mass the sky With fairy towers, line on\r\n    line; Find each mirage in the high air A symbol, not a dream\r\n    again... But old monotony is there: Endless avenues of rain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Under the glass portcullis of a theatre Amory stood, watching the\r\n      first great drops of rain splatter down and flatten to dark\r\n      stains on the sidewalk. The air became gray and opalescent; a\r\n      solitary light suddenly outlined a window over the way; then\r\n      another light; then a hundred more danced and glimmered into\r\n      vision. Under his feet a thick, iron-studded skylight turned\r\n      yellow; in the street the lamps of the taxi-cabs sent out\r\n      glistening sheens along the already black pavement. The unwelcome\r\n      November rain had perversely stolen the day\xe2\x80\x99s last hour and\r\n      pawned it with that ancient fence, the night.\r\n\r\n      The silence of the theatre behind him ended with a curious\r\n      snapping sound, followed by the heavy roaring of a rising crowd\r\n      and the interlaced clatter of many voices. The matinee was over.\r\n\r\n      He stood aside, edged a little into the rain to let the throng\r\n      pass. A small boy rushed out, sniffed in the damp, fresh air and\r\n      turned up the collar of his coat; came three or four couples in a\r\n      great hurry; came a further scattering of people whose eyes as\r\n      they emerged glanced invariably, first at the wet street, then at\r\n      the rain-filled air, finally at the dismal sky; last a dense,\r\n      strolling mass that depressed him with its heavy odor compounded\r\n      of the tobacco smell of the men and the fetid sensuousness of\r\n      stale powder on women. After the thick crowd came another\r\n      scattering; a stray half-dozen; a man on crutches; finally the\r\n      rattling bang of folding seats inside announced that the ushers\r\n      were at work.\r\n\r\n      New York seemed not so much awakening as turning over in its bed.\r\n      Pallid men rushed by, pinching together their coat-collars; a\r\n      great swarm of tired, magpie girls from a department-store\r\n      crowded along with shrieks of strident laughter, three to an\r\n      umbrella; a squad of marching policemen passed, already\r\n      miraculously protected by oilskin capes.\r\n\r\n      The rain gave Amory a feeling of detachment, and the numerous\r\n      unpleasant aspects of city life without money occurred to him in\r\n      threatening procession. There was the ghastly, stinking crush of\r\n      the subway\xe2\x80\x94the car cards thrusting themselves at one, leering out\r\n      like dull bores who grab your arm with another story; the\r\n      querulous worry as to whether some one isn\xe2\x80\x99t leaning on you; a\r\n      man deciding not to give his seat to a woman, hating her for it;\r\n      the woman hating him for not doing it; at worst a squalid\r\n      phantasmagoria of breath, and old cloth on human bodies and the\r\n      smells of the food men ate\xe2\x80\x94at best just people\xe2\x80\x94too hot or too\r\n      cold, tired, worried.\r\n\r\n      He pictured the rooms where these people lived\xe2\x80\x94where the patterns\r\n      of the blistered wall-papers were heavy reiterated sunflowers on\r\n      green and yellow backgrounds, where there were tin bathtubs and\r\n      gloomy hallways and verdureless, unnamable spaces in back of the\r\n      buildings; where even love dressed as seduction\xe2\x80\x94a sordid murder\r\n      around the corner, illicit motherhood in the flat above. And\r\n      always there was the economical stuffiness of indoor winter, and\r\n      the long summers, nightmares of perspiration between sticky\r\n      enveloping walls... dirty restaurants where careless, tired\r\n      people helped themselves to sugar with their own used\r\n      coffee-spoons, leaving hard brown deposits in the bowl.\r\n\r\n      It was not so bad where there were only men or else only women;\r\n      it was when they were vilely herded that it all seemed so rotten.\r\n      It was some shame that women gave off at having men see them\r\n      tired and poor\xe2\x80\x94it was some disgust that men had for women who\r\n      were tired and poor. It was dirtier than any battle-field he had\r\n      seen, harder to contemplate than any actual hardship moulded of\r\n      mire and sweat and danger, it was an atmosphere wherein birth and\r\n      marriage and death were loathsome, secret things.\r\n\r\n      He remembered one day in the subway when a delivery boy had\r\n      brought in a great funeral wreath of fresh flowers, how the smell\r\n      of it had suddenly cleared the air and given every one in the car\r\n      a momentary glow.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI detest poor people,\xe2\x80\x9d thought Amory suddenly. \xe2\x80\x9cI hate them for\r\n      being poor. Poverty may have been beautiful once, but it\xe2\x80\x99s rotten\r\n      now. It\xe2\x80\x99s the ugliest thing in the world. It\xe2\x80\x99s essentially\r\n      cleaner to be corrupt and rich than it is to be innocent and\r\n      poor.\xe2\x80\x9d He seemed to see again a figure whose significance had\r\n      once impressed him\xe2\x80\x94a well-dressed young man gazing from a club\r\n      window on Fifth Avenue and saying something to his companion with\r\n      a look of utter disgust. Probably, thought Amory, what he said\r\n      was: \xe2\x80\x9cMy God! Aren\xe2\x80\x99t people horrible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Never before in his life had Amory considered poor people. He\r\n      thought cynically how completely he was lacking in all human\r\n      sympathy. O. Henry had found in these people romance, pathos,\r\n      love, hate\xe2\x80\x94Amory saw only coarseness, physical filth, and\r\n      stupidity. He made no self-accusations: never any more did he\r\n      reproach himself for feelings that were natural and sincere. He\r\n      accepted all his reactions as a part of him, unchangeable,\r\n      unmoral. This problem of poverty transformed, magnified, attached\r\n      to some grander, more dignified attitude might some day even be\r\n      his problem; at present it roused only his profound distaste.\r\n\r\n      He walked over to Fifth Avenue, dodging the blind, black menace\r\n      of umbrellas, and standing in front of Delmonico\xe2\x80\x99s hailed an\r\n      auto-bus. Buttoning his coat closely around him he climbed to the\r\n      roof, where he rode in solitary state through the thin,\r\n      persistent rain, stung into alertness by the cool moisture\r\n      perpetually reborn on his cheek. Somewhere in his mind a\r\n      conversation began, rather resumed its place in his attention. It\r\n      was composed not of two voices, but of one, which acted alike as\r\n      questioner and answerer:\r\n\r\n      Question.\xe2\x80\x94Well\xe2\x80\x94what\xe2\x80\x99s the situation?\r\n\r\n      Answer.\xe2\x80\x94That I have about twenty-four dollars to my name.\r\n\r\n      Q.\xe2\x80\x94You have the Lake Geneva estate.\r\n\r\n      A.\xe2\x80\x94But I intend to keep it.\r\n\r\n      Q.\xe2\x80\x94Can you live?\r\n\r\n      A.\xe2\x80\x94I can\xe2\x80\x99t imagine not being able to. People make money in books\r\n      and I\xe2\x80\x99ve found that I can always do the things that people do in\r\n      books. Really they are the only things I can do.\r\n\r\n      Q.\xe2\x80\x94Be definite.\r\n\r\n      A.\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what I\xe2\x80\x99ll do\xe2\x80\x94nor have I much curiosity. To-morrow\r\n      I\xe2\x80\x99m going to leave New York for good. It\xe2\x80\x99s a bad town unless\r\n      you\xe2\x80\x99re on top of it.\r\n\r\n      Q.\xe2\x80\x94Do you want a lot of money?\r\n\r\n      A.\xe2\x80\x94No. I am merely afraid of being poor.\r\n\r\n      Q.\xe2\x80\x94Very afraid?\r\n\r\n      A.\xe2\x80\x94Just passively afraid.\r\n\r\n      Q.\xe2\x80\x94Where are you drifting?\r\n\r\n      A.\xe2\x80\x94Don\xe2\x80\x99t ask _me!_\r\n\r\n      Q.\xe2\x80\x94Don\xe2\x80\x99t you care?\r\n\r\n      A.\xe2\x80\x94Rather. I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to commit moral suicide.\r\n\r\n      Q.\xe2\x80\x94Have you no interests left?\r\n\r\n      A.\xe2\x80\x94None. I\xe2\x80\x99ve no more virtue to lose. Just as a cooling pot gives\r\n      off heat, so all through youth and adolescence we give off\r\n      calories of virtue. That\xe2\x80\x99s what\xe2\x80\x99s called ingenuousness.\r\n\r\n      Q.\xe2\x80\x94An interesting idea.\r\n\r\n      A.\xe2\x80\x94That\xe2\x80\x99s why a \xe2\x80\x9cgood man going wrong\xe2\x80\x9d attracts people. They\r\n      stand around and literally _warm themselves_ at the calories of\r\n      virtue he gives off. Sarah makes an unsophisticated remark and\r\n      the faces simper in delight\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cHow _innocent_ the poor child is!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      They\xe2\x80\x99re warming themselves at her virtue. Only she feels a little\r\n      colder after that.\r\n\r\n      Q.\xe2\x80\x94All your calories gone?\r\n\r\n      A.\xe2\x80\x94All of them. I\xe2\x80\x99m beginning to warm myself at other people\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      virtue.\r\n\r\n      Q.\xe2\x80\x94Are you corrupt?\r\n\r\n      A.\xe2\x80\x94I think so. I\xe2\x80\x99m not sure. Youth is like having a big plate of candy.\r\n      Sentimentalists think they want to be in the pure, simple state\r\n      they were in before they ate the candy. They don\xe2\x80\x99t. They just\r\n      want the fun of eating it all over again. The matron doesn\xe2\x80\x99t want\r\n      to repeat her girlhood\xe2\x80\x94she wants to repeat her honeymoon. I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      want to repeat my innocence. I want the pleasure of losing it\r\n      again.\r\n\r\n      Q.\xe2\x80\x94Where are you drifting?\r\n\r\n      This dialogue merged grotesquely into his mind\xe2\x80\x99s most familiar\r\n      state\xe2\x80\x94a grotesque blending of desires, worries, exterior\r\n      impressions and physical reactions.\r\n\r\n      One Hundred and Twenty-seventh Street\xe2\x80\x94or One Hundred and\r\n      Thirty-seventh Street.... Two and three look alike\xe2\x80\x94no, not much.\r\n      Seat damp... are clothes absorbing wetness from seat, or seat\r\n      absorbing dryness from clothes?... What a dirty river\xe2\x80\x94want to go down\r\n      there and see if it\xe2\x80\x99s dirty\xe2\x80\x94French rivers all brown or black, so\r\n      were Southern rivers. Twenty-four dollars meant four hundred and\r\n      eighty doughnuts. He could live on it three months and sleep in\r\n      the park. Wonder where Jill was\xe2\x80\x94Jill Bayne, Fayne, Sayne\xe2\x80\x94what the\r\n      devil\xe2\x80\x94neck hurts, darned uncomfortable seat. No desire to sleep\r\n      with Jill, what could Alec see in her? Alec had a coarse taste in\r\n      women. Own taste the best; Isabelle, Clara, Rosalind, Eleanor,\r\n      were all-American. Eleanor would pitch, probably southpaw.\r\n      Rosalind was outfield, wonderful hitter, Clara first base, maybe.\r\n      Wonder what Humbird\xe2\x80\x99s body looked like now. If he himself hadn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      been bayonet instructor he\xe2\x80\x99d have gone up to line three months\r\n      sooner, probably been killed. Where\xe2\x80\x99s the darned bell\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n      The street numbers of Riverside Drive were obscured by the mist\r\n      and dripping trees from anything but the swiftest scrutiny, but\r\n      Amory had finally caught sight of one\xe2\x80\x94One Hundred and\r\n      Twenty-seventh Street. He got off and with no distinct\r\n      destination followed a winding, descending sidewalk and came out\r\n      facing the river, in particular a long pier and a partitioned\r\n      litter of shipyards for miniature craft: small launches, canoes,\r\n      rowboats, and catboats. The hulls of many boats in\r\n      various stages of repair were around him; he smelled sawdust and\r\n      paint and the scarcely distinguishable fiat odor of the Hudson. A\r\n      man approached through the heavy gloom.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHello,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGot a pass?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo. Is this private?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThis is the Hudson River Sporting and Yacht Club.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh! I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know. I\xe2\x80\x99m just resting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d began the man dubiously.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ll go if you want me to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The man made non-committal noises in his throat and passed on.\r\n      Amory seated himself on an overturned boat and leaned forward\r\n      thoughtfully until his chin rested in his hand.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cMisfortune is liable to make me a damn bad man,\xe2\x80\x9d he said slowly.\r\n\r\n\r\n      IN THE DROOPING HOURS\r\n\r\n      While the rain drizzled on Amory looked futilely back at the\r\n      stream of his life, all its glitterings and dirty shallows. To\r\n      begin with, he was still afraid\xe2\x80\x94not physically afraid any more,\r\n      but afraid of people and prejudice and misery and monotony. Yet,\r\n      deep in his bitter heart, he wondered if he was after all worse\r\n      than this man or the next. He knew that he could sophisticate\r\n      himself finally into saying that his own weakness was just the\r\n      result of circumstances and environment; that often when he raged\r\n      at himself as an egotist something would whisper ingratiatingly:\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo. Genius!\xe2\x80\x9d That was one manifestation of fear, that voice\r\n      which whispered that he could not be both great and good, that\r\n      genius was the exact combination of those inexplicable grooves\r\n      and twists in his mind, that any discipline would curb it to\r\n      mediocrity. Probably more than any concrete vice or failing Amory\r\n      despised his own personality\xe2\x80\x94he loathed knowing that to-morrow\r\n      and the thousand days after he would swell pompously at a\r\n      compliment and sulk at an ill word like a third-rate musician or\r\n      a first-class actor. He was ashamed of the fact that very simple\r\n      and honest people usually distrusted him; that he had been cruel,\r\n      often, to those who had sunk their personalities in him\xe2\x80\x94several\r\n      girls, and a man here and there through college, that he had been\r\n      an evil influence on; people who had followed him here and there\r\n      into mental adventures from which he alone rebounded unscathed.\r\n\r\n      Usually, on nights like this, for there had been many lately, he\r\n      could escape from this consuming introspection by thinking of\r\n      children and the infinite possibilities of children\xe2\x80\x94he leaned and\r\n      listened and he heard a startled baby awake in a house across the\r\n      street and lend a tiny whimper to the still night. Quick as a\r\n      flash he turned away, wondering with a touch of panic whether\r\n      something in the brooding despair of his mood had made a darkness\r\n      in its tiny soul. He shivered. What if some day the balance was\r\n      overturned, and he became a thing that frightened children and\r\n      crept into rooms in the dark, approached dim communion with those\r\n      phantoms who whispered shadowy secrets to the mad of that dark\r\n      continent upon the moon....\r\n\r\n\r\n      Amory smiled a bit.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re too much wrapped up in yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d he heard some one say.\r\n      And again\xe2\x80\x94\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGet out and do some real work\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cStop worrying\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      He fancied a possible future comment of his own.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94I was perhaps an egotist in youth, but I soon found it made\r\n      me morbid to think too much about myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n      Suddenly he felt an overwhelming desire to let himself go to the\r\n      devil\xe2\x80\x94not to go violently as a gentleman should, but to sink\r\n      safely and sensuously out of sight. He pictured himself in an\r\n      adobe house in Mexico, half-reclining on a rug-covered couch, his\r\n      slender, artistic fingers closed on a cigarette while he listened\r\n      to guitars strumming melancholy undertones to an age-old dirge of\r\n      Castile and an olive-skinned, carmine-lipped girl caressed his\r\n      hair. Here he might live a strange litany, delivered from right\r\n      and wrong and from the hound of heaven and from every God (except\r\n      the exotic Mexican one who was pretty slack himself and rather\r\n      addicted to Oriental scents)\xe2\x80\x94delivered from success and hope and\r\n      poverty into that long chute of indulgence which led, after all,\r\n      only to the artificial lake of death.\r\n\r\n      There were so many places where one might deteriorate pleasantly:\r\n      Port Said, Shanghai, parts of Turkestan, Constantinople, the\r\n      South Seas\xe2\x80\x94all lands of sad, haunting music and many odors, where\r\n      lust could be a mode and expression of life, where the shades of\r\n      night skies and sunsets would seem to reflect only moods of\r\n      passion: the colors of lips and poppies.\r\n\r\n\r\n      STILL WEEDING\r\n\r\n      Once he had been miraculously able to scent evil as a horse\r\n      detects a broken bridge at night, but the man with the queer feet\r\n      in Phoebe\xe2\x80\x99s room had diminished to the aura over Jill. His\r\n      instinct perceived the fetidness of poverty, but no longer\r\n      ferreted out the deeper evils in pride and sensuality.\r\n\r\n      There were no more wise men; there were no more heroes; Burne\r\n      Holiday was sunk from sight as though he had never lived;\r\n      Monsignor was dead. Amory had grown up to a thousand books, a\r\n      thousand lies; he had listened eagerly to people who pretended to\r\n      know, who knew nothing. The mystical reveries of saints that had\r\n      once filled him with awe in the still hours of night, now vaguely\r\n      repelled him. The Byrons and Brookes who had defied life from\r\n      mountain tops were in the end but flaneurs and poseurs, at best\r\n      mistaking the shadow of courage for the substance of wisdom. The\r\n      pageantry of his disillusion took shape in a world-old procession\r\n      of Prophets, Athenians, Martyrs, Saints, Scientists, Don Juans,\r\n      Jesuits, Puritans, Fausts, Poets, Pacifists; like costumed alumni\r\n      at a college reunion they streamed before him as their dreams,\r\n      personalities, and creeds had in turn thrown colored lights on\r\n      his soul; each had tried to express the glory of life and the\r\n      tremendous significance of man; each had boasted of synchronizing\r\n      what had gone before into his own rickety generalities; each had\r\n      depended after all on the set stage and the convention of the\r\n      theatre, which is that man in his hunger for faith will feed his\r\n      mind with the nearest and most convenient food.\r\n\r\n      Women\xe2\x80\x94of whom he had expected so much; whose beauty he had hoped\r\n      to transmute into modes of art; whose unfathomable instincts,\r\n      marvellously incoherent and inarticulate, he had thought to\r\n      perpetuate in terms of experience\xe2\x80\x94had become merely consecrations\r\n      to their own posterity. Isabelle, Clara, Rosalind, Eleanor, were\r\n      all removed by their very beauty, around which men had swarmed,\r\n      from the possibility of contributing anything but a sick heart\r\n      and a page of puzzled words to write.\r\n\r\n      Amory based his loss of faith in help from others on several\r\n      sweeping syllogisms. Granted that his generation, however bruised\r\n      and decimated from this Victorian war, were the heirs of\r\n      progress. Waving aside petty differences of conclusions which,\r\n      although they might occasionally cause the deaths of several\r\n      millions of young men, might be explained away\xe2\x80\x94supposing that\r\n      after all Bernard Shaw and Bernhardi, Bonar Law and\r\n      Bethmann-Hollweg were mutual heirs of progress if only in\r\n      agreeing against the ducking of witches\xe2\x80\x94waiving the antitheses\r\n      and approaching individually these men who seemed to be the\r\n      leaders, he was repelled by the discrepancies and contradictions\r\n      in the men themselves.\r\n\r\n      There was, for example, Thornton Hancock, respected by half the\r\n      intellectual world as an authority on life, a man who had\r\n      verified and believed the code he lived by, an educator of\r\n      educators, an adviser to Presidents\xe2\x80\x94yet Amory knew that this man\r\n      had, in his heart, leaned on the priest of another religion.\r\n\r\n      And Monsignor, upon whom a cardinal rested, had moments of\r\n      strange and horrible insecurity\xe2\x80\x94inexplicable in a religion that\r\n      explained even disbelief in terms of its own faith: if you\r\n      doubted the devil it was the devil that made you doubt him. Amory\r\n      had seen Monsignor go to the houses of stolid philistines, read\r\n      popular novels furiously, saturate himself in routine, to escape\r\n      from that horror.\r\n\r\n      And this priest, a little wiser, somewhat purer, had been, Amory\r\n      knew, not essentially older than he.\r\n\r\n      Amory was alone\xe2\x80\x94he had escaped from a small enclosure into a\r\n      great labyrinth. He was where Goethe was when he began \xe2\x80\x9cFaust\xe2\x80\x9d;\r\n      he was where Conrad was when he wrote \xe2\x80\x9cAlmayer\xe2\x80\x99s Folly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory said to himself that there were essentially two sorts of\r\n      people who through natural clarity or disillusion left the\r\n      enclosure and sought the labyrinth. There were men like Wells and\r\n      Plato, who had, half unconsciously, a strange, hidden orthodoxy,\r\n      who would accept for themselves only what could be accepted for\r\n      all men\xe2\x80\x94incurable romanticists who never, for all their efforts,\r\n      could enter the labyrinth as stark souls; there were on the other\r\n      hand sword-like pioneering personalities, Samuel Butler, Renan,\r\n      Voltaire, who progressed much slower, yet eventually much\r\n      further, not in the direct pessimistic line of speculative\r\n      philosophy but concerned in the eternal attempt to attach a\r\n      positive value to life....\r\n\r\n      Amory stopped. He began for the first time in his life to have a\r\n      strong distrust of all generalities and epigrams. They were too\r\n      easy, too dangerous to the public mind. Yet all thought usually\r\n      reached the public after thirty years in some such form: Benson\r\n      and Chesterton had popularized Huysmans and Newman; Shaw had\r\n      sugar-coated Nietzsche and Ibsen and Schopenhauer. The man in the\r\n      street heard the conclusions of dead genius through some one\r\n      else\xe2\x80\x99s clever paradoxes and didactic epigrams.\r\n\r\n      Life was a damned muddle... a football game with every one\r\n      off-side and the referee gotten rid of\xe2\x80\x94every one claiming the\r\n      referee would have been on his side....\r\n\r\n      Progress was a labyrinth... people plunging blindly in and then\r\n      rushing wildly back, shouting that they had found it... the\r\n      invisible king\xe2\x80\x94the elan vital\xe2\x80\x94the principle of evolution...\r\n      writing a book, starting a war, founding a school....\r\n\r\n      Amory, even had he not been a selfish man, would have started all\r\n      inquiries with himself. He was his own best example\xe2\x80\x94sitting in\r\n      the rain, a human creature of sex and pride, foiled by chance and\r\n      his own temperament of the balm of love and children, preserved\r\n      to help in building up the living consciousness of the race.\r\n\r\n      In self-reproach and loneliness and disillusion he came to the\r\n      entrance of the labyrinth.\r\n\r\n\r\n      Another dawn flung itself across the river, a belated taxi\r\n      hurried along the street, its lamps still shining like burning\r\n      eyes in a face white from a night\xe2\x80\x99s carouse. A melancholy siren\r\n      sounded far down the river.\r\n\r\n\r\n      MONSIGNOR\r\n\r\n      Amory kept thinking how Monsignor would have enjoyed his own\r\n      funeral. It was magnificently Catholic and liturgical. Bishop\r\n      O\xe2\x80\x99Neill sang solemn high mass and the cardinal gave the final\r\n      absolutions. Thornton Hancock, Mrs. Lawrence, the British and\r\n      Italian ambassadors, the papal delegate, and a host of friends\r\n      and priests were there\xe2\x80\x94yet the inexorable shears had cut through\r\n      all these threads that Monsignor had gathered into his hands. To\r\n      Amory it was a haunting grief to see him lying in his coffin,\r\n      with closed hands upon his purple vestments. His face had not\r\n      changed, and, as he never knew he was dying, it showed no pain or\r\n      fear. It was Amory\xe2\x80\x99s dear old friend, his and the others\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x94for the\r\n      church was full of people with daft, staring faces, the most\r\n      exalted seeming the most stricken.\r\n\r\n      The cardinal, like an archangel in cope and mitre, sprinkled the\r\n      holy water; the organ broke into sound; the choir began to sing\r\n      the Requiem Eternam.\r\n\r\n      All these people grieved because they had to some extent depended\r\n      upon Monsignor. Their grief was more than sentiment for the\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9ccrack in his voice or a certain break in his walk,\xe2\x80\x9d as Wells put\r\n      it. These people had leaned on Monsignor\xe2\x80\x99s faith, his way of\r\n      finding cheer, of making religion a thing of lights and shadows,\r\n      making all light and shadow merely aspects of God. People felt\r\n      safe when he was near.\r\n\r\n      Of Amory\xe2\x80\x99s attempted sacrifice had been born merely the full\r\n      realization of his disillusion, but of Monsignor\xe2\x80\x99s funeral was\r\n      born the romantic elf who was to enter the labyrinth with him. He\r\n      found something that he wanted, had always wanted and always\r\n      would want\xe2\x80\x94not to be admired, as he had feared; not to be loved,\r\n      as he had made himself believe; but to be necessary to people, to\r\n      be indispensable; he remembered the sense of security he had\r\n      found in Burne.\r\n\r\n      Life opened up in one of its amazing bursts of radiance and Amory\r\n      suddenly and permanently rejected an old epigram that had been\r\n      playing listlessly in his mind: \xe2\x80\x9cVery few things matter and\r\n      nothing matters very much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      On the contrary, Amory felt an immense desire to give people a\r\n      sense of security.\r\n\r\n\r\n      THE BIG MAN WITH GOGGLES\r\n\r\n      On the day that Amory started on his walk to Princeton the sky\r\n      was a colorless vault, cool, high and barren of the threat of\r\n      rain. It was a gray day, that least fleshly of all weathers; a\r\n      day of dreams and far hopes and clear visions. It was a day\r\n      easily associated with those abstract truths and purities that\r\n      dissolve in the sunshine or fade out in mocking laughter by the\r\n      light of the moon. The trees and clouds were carved in classical\r\n      severity; the sounds of the countryside had harmonized to a\r\n      monotone, metallic as a trumpet, breathless as the Grecian urn.\r\n\r\n      The day had put Amory in such a contemplative mood that he caused\r\n      much annoyance to several motorists who were forced to slow up\r\n      considerably or else run him down. So engrossed in his thoughts\r\n      was he that he was scarcely surprised at that strange\r\n      phenomenon\xe2\x80\x94cordiality manifested within fifty miles of\r\n      Manhattan\xe2\x80\x94when a passing car slowed down beside him and a voice\r\n      hailed him. He looked up and saw a magnificent Locomobile in\r\n      which sat two middle-aged men, one of them small and anxious\r\n      looking, apparently an artificial growth on the other who was\r\n      large and begoggled and imposing.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDo you want a lift?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the apparently artificial growth,\r\n      glancing from the corner of his eye at the imposing man as if for\r\n      some habitual, silent corroboration.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou bet I do. Thanks.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The chauffeur swung open the door, and, climbing in, Amory\r\n      settled himself in the middle of the back seat. He took in his\r\n      companions curiously. The chief characteristic of the big man\r\n      seemed to be a great confidence in himself set off against a\r\n      tremendous boredom with everything around him. That part of his\r\n      face which protruded under the goggles was what is generally\r\n      termed \xe2\x80\x9cstrong\xe2\x80\x9d; rolls of not undignified fat had collected near\r\n      his chin; somewhere above was a wide thin mouth and the rough\r\n      model for a Roman nose, and, below, his shoulders collapsed\r\n      without a struggle into the powerful bulk of his chest and belly.\r\n      He was excellently and quietly dressed. Amory noticed that he was\r\n      inclined to stare straight at the back of the chauffeur\xe2\x80\x99s head as\r\n      if speculating steadily but hopelessly some baffling hirsute\r\n      problem.\r\n\r\n      The smaller man was remarkable only for his complete submersion\r\n      in the personality of the other. He was of that lower secretarial\r\n      type who at forty have engraved upon their business cards:\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAssistant to the President,\xe2\x80\x9d and without a sigh consecrate the\r\n      rest of their lives to second-hand mannerisms.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGoing far?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the smaller man in a pleasant disinterested\r\n      way.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cQuite a stretch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHiking for exercise?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d responded Amory succinctly, \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m walking because I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      afford to ride.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Then again:\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAre you looking for work? Because there\xe2\x80\x99s lots of work,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\n      continued rather testily. \xe2\x80\x9cAll this talk of lack of work. The\r\n      West is especially short of labor.\xe2\x80\x9d He expressed the West with a\r\n      sweeping, lateral gesture. Amory nodded politely.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHave you a trade?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      No\xe2\x80\x94Amory had no trade.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cClerk, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      No\xe2\x80\x94Amory was not a clerk.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhatever your line is,\xe2\x80\x9d said the little man, seeming to agree\r\n      wisely with something Amory had said, \xe2\x80\x9cnow is the time of\r\n      opportunity and business openings.\xe2\x80\x9d He glanced again toward the\r\n      big man, as a lawyer grilling a witness glances involuntarily at\r\n      the jury.\r\n\r\n      Amory decided that he must say something and for the life of him\r\n      could think of only one thing to say.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOf course I want a great lot of money\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The little man laughed mirthlessly but conscientiously.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what every one wants nowadays, but they don\xe2\x80\x99t want to\r\n      work for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cA very natural, healthy desire. Almost all normal people want to\r\n      be rich without great effort\xe2\x80\x94except the financiers in problem\r\n      plays, who want to \xe2\x80\x98crash their way through.\xe2\x80\x99 Don\xe2\x80\x99t you want easy\r\n      money?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOf course not,\xe2\x80\x9d said the secretary indignantly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBut,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Amory disregarding him, \xe2\x80\x9cbeing very poor at\r\n      present I am contemplating socialism as possibly my forte.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Both men glanced at him curiously.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThese bomb throwers\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d The little man ceased as words lurched\r\n      ponderously from the big man\xe2\x80\x99s chest.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIf I thought you were a bomb thrower I\xe2\x80\x99d run you over to the\r\n      Newark jail. That\xe2\x80\x99s what I think of Socialists.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory laughed.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you,\xe2\x80\x9d asked the big man, \xe2\x80\x9cone of these parlor\r\n      Bolsheviks, one of these idealists? I must say I fail to see the\r\n      difference. The idealists loaf around and write the stuff that\r\n      stirs up the poor immigrants.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory, \xe2\x80\x9cif being an idealist is both safe and\r\n      lucrative, I might try it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s your difficulty? Lost your job?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNot exactly, but\xe2\x80\x94well, call it that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat was it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWriting copy for an advertising agency.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cLots of money in advertising.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory smiled discreetly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99ll admit there\xe2\x80\x99s money in it eventually. Talent doesn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      starve any more. Even art gets enough to eat these days. Artists\r\n      draw your magazine covers, write your advertisements, hash out\r\n      rag-time for your theatres. By the great commercializing of\r\n      printing you\xe2\x80\x99ve found a harmless, polite occupation for every\r\n      genius who might have carved his own niche. But beware the artist\r\n      who\xe2\x80\x99s an intellectual also. The artist who doesn\xe2\x80\x99t fit\xe2\x80\x94the\r\n      Rousseau, the Tolstoi, the Samuel Butler, the Amory Blaine\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWho\xe2\x80\x99s he?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded the little man suspiciously.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory, \xe2\x80\x9che\xe2\x80\x99s a\xe2\x80\x94he\xe2\x80\x99s an intellectual personage not\r\n      very well known at present.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The little man laughed his conscientious laugh, and stopped\r\n      rather suddenly as Amory\xe2\x80\x99s burning eyes turned on him.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you laughing at?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThese _intellectual_ people\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDo you know what it means?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The little man\xe2\x80\x99s eyes twitched nervously.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhy, it _usually_ means\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt _always_ means brainy and well-educated,\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted Amory.\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt means having an active knowledge of the race\xe2\x80\x99s experience.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n      Amory decided to be very rude. He turned to the big man. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\n      young man,\xe2\x80\x9d he indicated the secretary with his thumb, and said\r\n      young man as one says bell-boy, with no implication of youth,\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9chas the usual muddled connotation of all popular words.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou object to the fact that capital controls printing?\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\n      big man, fixing him with his goggles.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes\xe2\x80\x94and I object to doing their mental work for them. It seemed\r\n      to me that the root of all the business I saw around me consisted\r\n      in overworking and underpaying a bunch of dubs who submitted to\r\n      it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHere now,\xe2\x80\x9d said the big man, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99ll have to admit that the\r\n      laboring man is certainly highly paid\xe2\x80\x94five and six hour days\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      ridiculous. You can\xe2\x80\x99t buy an honest day\xe2\x80\x99s work from a man in the\r\n      trades-unions.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ve brought it on yourselves,\xe2\x80\x9d insisted Amory. \xe2\x80\x9cYou people\r\n      never make concessions until they\xe2\x80\x99re wrung out of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat people?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYour class; the class I belonged to until recently; those who by\r\n      inheritance or industry or brains or dishonesty have become the\r\n      moneyed class.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDo you imagine that if that road-mender over there had the money\r\n      he\xe2\x80\x99d be any more willing to give it up?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo, but what\xe2\x80\x99s that got to do with it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The older man considered.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I\xe2\x80\x99ll admit it hasn\xe2\x80\x99t. It rather sounds as if it had though.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIn fact,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Amory, \xe2\x80\x9che\xe2\x80\x99d be worse. The lower classes are\r\n      narrower, less pleasant and personally more selfish\xe2\x80\x94certainly\r\n      more stupid. But all that has nothing to do with the question.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cJust exactly what is the question?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Here Amory had to pause to consider exactly what the question\r\n      was.\r\n\r\n\r\n      AMORY COINS A PHRASE\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhen life gets hold of a brainy man of fair education,\xe2\x80\x9d began\r\n      Amory slowly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat is, when he marries he becomes, nine times\r\n      out of ten, a conservative as far as existing social conditions\r\n      are concerned. He may be unselfish, kind-hearted, even just in\r\n      his own way, but his first job is to provide and to hold fast.\r\n      His wife shoos him on, from ten thousand a year to twenty\r\n      thousand a year, on and on, in an enclosed treadmill that hasn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      any windows. He\xe2\x80\x99s done! Life\xe2\x80\x99s got him! He\xe2\x80\x99s no help! He\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\n      spiritually married man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory paused and decided that it wasn\xe2\x80\x99t such a bad phrase.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cSome men,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, \xe2\x80\x9cescape the grip. Maybe their wives\r\n      have no social ambitions; maybe they\xe2\x80\x99ve hit a sentence or two in\r\n      a \xe2\x80\x98dangerous book\xe2\x80\x99 that pleased them; maybe they started on the\r\n      treadmill as I did and were knocked off. Anyway, they\xe2\x80\x99re the\r\n      congressmen you can\xe2\x80\x99t bribe, the Presidents who aren\xe2\x80\x99t\r\n      politicians, the writers, speakers, scientists, statesmen who\r\n      aren\xe2\x80\x99t just popular grab-bags for a half-dozen women and\r\n      children.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s the natural radical?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory. \xe2\x80\x9cHe may vary from the disillusioned critic\r\n      like old Thornton Hancock, all the way to Trotsky. Now this\r\n      spiritually unmarried man hasn\xe2\x80\x99t direct power, for unfortunately\r\n      the spiritually married man, as a by-product of his money chase,\r\n      has garnered in the great newspaper, the popular magazine, the\r\n      influential weekly\xe2\x80\x94so that Mrs. Newspaper, Mrs. Magazine, Mrs.\r\n      Weekly can have a better limousine than those oil people across\r\n      the street or those cement people \xe2\x80\x99round the corner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhy not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt makes wealthy men the keepers of the world\xe2\x80\x99s intellectual\r\n      conscience and, of course, a man who has money under one set of\r\n      social institutions quite naturally can\xe2\x80\x99t risk his family\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      happiness by letting the clamor for another appear in his\r\n      newspaper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cBut it appears,\xe2\x80\x9d said the big man.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhere?\xe2\x80\x94in the discredited mediums. Rotten cheap-papered\r\n      weeklies.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAll right\xe2\x80\x94go on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWell, my first point is that through a mixture of conditions of\r\n      which the family is the first, there are these two sorts of\r\n      brains. One sort takes human nature as it finds it, uses its\r\n      timidity, its weakness, and its strength for its own ends.\r\n      Opposed is the man who, being spiritually unmarried, continually\r\n      seeks for new systems that will control or counteract human\r\n      nature. His problem is harder. It is not life that\xe2\x80\x99s complicated,\r\n      it\xe2\x80\x99s the struggle to guide and control life. That is his\r\n      struggle. He is a part of progress\xe2\x80\x94the spiritually married man is\r\n      not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The big man produced three big cigars, and proffered them on his\r\n      huge palm. The little man took one, Amory shook his head and\r\n      reached for a cigarette.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cGo on talking,\xe2\x80\x9d said the big man. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve been wanting to hear one\r\n      of you fellows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n      GOING FASTER\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cModern life,\xe2\x80\x9d began Amory again, \xe2\x80\x9cchanges no longer century by\r\n      century, but year by year, ten times faster than it ever has\r\n      before\xe2\x80\x94populations doubling, civilizations unified more closely\r\n      with other civilizations, economic interdependence, racial\r\n      questions, and\xe2\x80\x94we\xe2\x80\x99re _dawdling_ along. My idea is that we\xe2\x80\x99ve got\r\n      to go very much faster.\xe2\x80\x9d He slightly emphasized the last words\r\n      and the chauffeur unconsciously increased the speed of the car.\r\n      Amory and the big man laughed; the little man laughed, too, after\r\n      a pause.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cEvery child,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory, \xe2\x80\x9cshould have an equal start. If his\r\n      father can endow him with a good physique and his mother with\r\n      some common sense in his early education, that should be his\r\n      heritage. If the father can\xe2\x80\x99t give him a good physique, if the\r\n      mother has spent in chasing men the years in which she should\r\n      have been preparing herself to educate her children, so much the\r\n      worse for the child. He shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t be artificially bolstered up\r\n      with money, sent to these horrible tutoring schools, dragged\r\n      through college... Every boy ought to have an equal start.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAll right,\xe2\x80\x9d said the big man, his goggles indicating neither\r\n      approval nor objection.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNext I\xe2\x80\x99d have a fair trial of government ownership of all\r\n      industries.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s been proven a failure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94it merely failed. If we had government ownership we\xe2\x80\x99d have\r\n      the best analytical business minds in the government working for\r\n      something besides themselves. We\xe2\x80\x99d have Mackays instead of\r\n      Burlesons; we\xe2\x80\x99d have Morgans in the Treasury Department; we\xe2\x80\x99d\r\n      have Hills running interstate commerce. We\xe2\x80\x99d have the best\r\n      lawyers in the Senate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThey wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t give their best efforts for nothing. McAdoo\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory, shaking his head. \xe2\x80\x9cMoney isn\xe2\x80\x99t the only\r\n      stimulus that brings out the best that\xe2\x80\x99s in a man, even in\r\n      America.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou said a while ago that it was.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIt is, right now. But if it were made illegal to have more than\r\n      a certain amount the best men would all flock for the one other\r\n      reward which attracts humanity\xe2\x80\x94honor.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The big man made a sound that was very like _boo_.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the silliest thing you\xe2\x80\x99ve said yet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNo, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t silly. It\xe2\x80\x99s quite plausible. If you\xe2\x80\x99d gone to\r\n      college you\xe2\x80\x99d have been struck by the fact that the men there\r\n      would work twice as hard for any one of a hundred petty honors as\r\n      those other men did who were earning their way through.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cKids\xe2\x80\x94child\xe2\x80\x99s play!\xe2\x80\x9d scoffed his antagonist.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cNot by a darned sight\xe2\x80\x94unless we\xe2\x80\x99re all children. Did you ever\r\n      see a grown man when he\xe2\x80\x99s trying for a secret society\xe2\x80\x94or a rising\r\n      family whose name is up at some club? They\xe2\x80\x99ll jump when they hear\r\n      the sound of the word. The idea that to make a man work you\xe2\x80\x99ve\r\n      got to hold gold in front of his eyes is a growth, not an axiom.\r\n      We\xe2\x80\x99ve done that for so long that we\xe2\x80\x99ve forgotten there\xe2\x80\x99s any\r\n      other way. We\xe2\x80\x99ve made a world where that\xe2\x80\x99s necessary. Let me tell\r\n      you\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Amory became emphatic\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9cif there were ten men insured against\r\n      either wealth or starvation, and offered a green ribbon for five\r\n      hours\xe2\x80\x99 work a day and a blue ribbon for ten hours\xe2\x80\x99 work a day,\r\n      nine out of ten of them would be trying for the blue ribbon. That\r\n      competitive instinct only wants a badge. If the size of their\r\n      house is the badge they\xe2\x80\x99ll sweat their heads off for that. If\r\n      it\xe2\x80\x99s only a blue ribbon, I damn near believe they\xe2\x80\x99ll work just as\r\n      hard. They have in other ages.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t agree with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI know it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory nodding sadly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter any\r\n      more though. I think these people are going to come and take what\r\n      they want pretty soon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      A fierce hiss came from the little man.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9c_Machine-guns!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cAh, but you\xe2\x80\x99ve taught them their use.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The big man shook his head.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIn this country there are enough property owners not to permit\r\n      that sort of thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory wished he knew the statistics of property owners and\r\n      non-property owners; he decided to change the subject.\r\n\r\n      But the big man was aroused.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhen you talk of \xe2\x80\x98taking things away,\xe2\x80\x99 you\xe2\x80\x99re on dangerous\r\n      ground.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cHow can they get it without taking it? You\xe2\x80\x99ve got to be sensational to get attention.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cRussia is your example of a beneficent violence, I suppose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cQuite possibly,\xe2\x80\x9d admitted Amory. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course, it\xe2\x80\x99s overflowing\r\n      just as the French Revolution did, but I\xe2\x80\x99ve no doubt that it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\n      really a great experiment and well worth while.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you believe in moderation?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou won\xe2\x80\x99t listen to the moderates, and it\xe2\x80\x99s almost too late. The\r\n      truth is that the public has done one of those startling and\r\n      amazing things that they do about once in a hundred years.\r\n      They\xe2\x80\x99ve seized an idea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThat however the brains and abilities of men may differ, their\r\n      stomachs are essentially the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n      THE LITTLE MAN GETS HIS\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cIf you took all the money in the world,\xe2\x80\x9d said the little man\r\n      with much profundity, \xe2\x80\x9cand divided it up in equ\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cOh, shut up!\xe2\x80\x9d said Amory briskly and, paying no attention to the\r\n      little man\xe2\x80\x99s enraged stare, he went on with his argument.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThe human stomach\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d he began; but the big man interrupted rather\r\n      impatiently.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m letting you talk, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut please avoid\r\n      stomachs. I\xe2\x80\x99ve been feeling mine all day. Anyway, I don\xe2\x80\x99t agree\r\n      with one-half you\xe2\x80\x99ve said. Government ownership is the basis of\r\n      your whole argument, and it\xe2\x80\x99s invariably a beehive of corruption.\r\n      Men won\xe2\x80\x99t work for blue ribbons, that\xe2\x80\x99s all rot.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      When he ceased the little man spoke up with a determined nod, as\r\n      if resolved this time to have his say out.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThere are certain things which are human nature,\xe2\x80\x9d he asserted\r\n      with an owl-like look, \xe2\x80\x9cwhich always have been and always will\r\n      be, which can\xe2\x80\x99t be changed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      Amory looked from the small man to the big man helplessly.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cListen to that! _That\xe2\x80\x99s_ what makes me discouraged with\r\n      progress. _Listen_ to that! I can name offhand over one hundred\r\n      natural phenomena that have been changed by the will of man\xe2\x80\x94a\r\n      hundred instincts in man that have been wiped out or are now held\r\n      in check by civilization. What this man here just said has been\r\n      for thousands of years the last refuge of the associated\r\n      mutton-heads of the world. It negates the efforts of every\r\n      scientist, statesman, moralist, reformer, doctor, and philosopher\r\n      that ever gave his life to humanity\xe2\x80\x99s service. It\xe2\x80\x99s a flat\r\n      impeachment of all that\xe2\x80\x99s worth while in human nature. Every\r\n      person over twenty-five years old who makes that statement in\r\n      cold blood ought to be deprived of the franchise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The little man leaned back against the seat, his face purple with\r\n      rage. Amory continued, addressing his remarks to the big man.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThese quarter-educated, stale-minded men such as your friend\r\n      here, who _think_ they think, every question that comes up,\r\n      you\xe2\x80\x99ll find his type in the usual ghastly muddle. They haven\xe2\x80\x99t clear logical ideas on one\r\n      single subject except a sturdy, stolid opposition to all change.\r\n      They don\xe2\x80\x99t think uneducated people should be highly paid, but\r\n      they won\xe2\x80\x99t see that if they don\xe2\x80\x99t pay the uneducated people their\r\n      children are going to be uneducated too, and we\xe2\x80\x99re going round\r\n      and round in a circle. That\xe2\x80\x94is the great middle class!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The big man with a broad grin on his face leaned over and smiled\r\n      at the little man.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re catching it pretty heavy, Garvin; how do you feel?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      The little man made an attempt to smile and act as if the whole\r\n      matter were so ridiculous as to be beneath notice. But Amory was\r\n      not through.\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cThe theory that people are fit to govern themselves rests on\r\n      this man. If\r\n      he can\xe2\x80\x99t, then I don\xe2\x80\x99t think it matters much what happens to man\r\n      or his systems, now or hereafter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cI am both interested and amused,\xe2\x80\x9d said the big man. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are\r\n      very young.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n      \xe2\x80\x9cWhich may only mean that I have neither been corrupted nor made\r\n      timid by contemporary experience.
